Conditions for Necessary Conclusion in Second Figure Mixed Syllogisms
Author states conditions for necessary conclusion in second figure mixed syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions for a necessary conclusion in a second figure mixed modal syllogism and apply them to a kalam example.

Core Idea
Necessity transfers in second figure only when premises differ in quality and the major is universal.

In modal syllogistic, the necessity of a conclusion depends on the modality of premises and the syllogism's figure. This passage from al-Razi's Mulakhkhas specifies the conditions for a necessary conclusion when one premise is necessary in the second figure. Understanding these conditions is crucial for evaluating theological proofs that rely on necessity, such as arguments for God's attributes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure and mixed nature
Note that the passage specifies the second figure and that the premises are mixed in modality: one necessary, the other not necessary. The second figure has the middle term as predicate in both premises.
Mixed syllogisms are called 'mukhtalitat'.
2
Extract the two conditions
The author states two additional conditions beyond one premise being necessary: difference in quality (اختلاف في الكيف) between the premises (one affirmative, one negative) and universality of the major premise (كلية الكبرى).
If the major is not universal or the premises have the same quality, the conclusion may not be necessary.
3
Analyze the three cases for the second premise
The author considers three cases for the other premise: it may be necessary, not necessary, or possibly necessary. In each case, he argues that the conclusion ends up necessary.
The reasoning involves the necessity of the universal major premise preserving necessity through the syllogism.
4
Verify the conclusion's necessity
Check that in all three cases the conclusion is necessary. For example, if the other premise is possible, the necessity of the major still forces a necessary conclusion due to the formal structure.
This is a subtle point in modal logic: necessity can 'overflow' from one premise to the conclusion under specific conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما [؟] المختلطات الشكل الثاني من [؟] لا بد من مقدمة وهي إن هذا الشكل متى كانت إحدى مقدمتيه ضرورية ثم حضر الشرطان الآخران، أعني الاختلاف في الكيف وكلية الكبرى، كانت النتيجة ضرورية لإحالة [؟]. لأن إحدى المقدمتين إذا كانت ضرورية فالأخرى إما أن تكون ضرورية أو لا تكون أو محتملة لها. فإن كان الأول كانت النتيجة ضرورية. وإن كان الثاني فليس للضرورة [؟] سالمًا عن الضروري ضروري. وإن كان الثالث معلوم أن الأول [؟] الذي يحمل الضرورة وعدمه [؟] نفسه عن أن يكون ضروريًا أو لا يكون. وإذا كانت النتيجة ضرورية على القدرين معًا كانت أيضًا ضرورية.
As for the mixed [syllogisms] of the second figure, we must begin with a premise: that when one of its two premises is necessary, and the other two conditions are present, namely difference in quality and universality of the major, then the conclusion is necessary because of [؟]. For if one of the two premises is necessary, then the other is either necessary, or not necessary, or possibly necessary. If the first, then the conclusion is necessary. If the second, then [؟] the necessity is safe from the necessary [؟]. And if the third, it is known that the first [؟] which carries necessity and its absence [؟] itself from being necessary or not. And if the conclusion is necessary in both cases together, it is also necessary.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage explains the conditions for producing a necessary conclusion in a mixed modal syllogism of the second figure. The author states that when one premise is necessary and the other premise differs in quality (affirmative/negative) and the major premise is universal, the conclusion must be necessary. The argument considers three cases for the second premise: it may be necessary, not necessary, or possibly necessary. In all cases, the conclusion ends up necessary. The reasoning involves the preservation of necessity across the syllogism, a key principle in modal logic. This mirrors standard Aristotelian modal syllogistic, applied here to the special constraints of the second figure. For example, in a second figure syllogism: 'Every A is necessarily B; No C is B; Therefore, no C is A' may yield a necessary conclusion if conditions are met. The passage emphasizes that the necessity of the conclusion is inseparable from the modal force of the premises and the formal structure.

Key Terms
ضرورية
مختلطات

Checkpoint

B) Difference in quality and universality of the major.
C) The major must be negative and the minor necessary.
D) Both premises must be necessary and the major particular.

Key Takeaway
In second figure mixed syllogisms, a necessary conclusion requires the major premise to be universal and the two premises to differ in quality, regardless of the modality of the other premise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Consider the second figure modal syllogism from kalam: 'Every accident is necessarily temporally originated. No substance is temporally originated. Therefore no substance is an accident.' Identify whether the conditions for a necessary conclusion are met. Explain your reasoning.

Refutation of Restriction on Negative Existential Premises
Author refutes the view that a negative existential premise yields an existential conclusion only.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the earlier scholars' claim about negative existential premises and reconstruct the author's refutation using conversion rules.

Core Idea
Even negative existential premises can yield necessary conclusions when conversion rules are applied correctly.

Earlier logicians held that if a premise is merely existential (not necessary), the conclusion must also be existential, even if the other premise is necessary. The author of this passage challenges that view, arguing that with proper conversion—specifically, the conversion of negative existential propositions—a necessary conclusion is possible. This debate is crucial for modal logic in Islamic sciences, where necessity and contingency affect the strength of arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the earlier scholars' claim
Find the claim: if a negative premise is existential (free from necessity), the conclusion is also existential, not necessary. This limits the modal force of syllogisms.
The claim is stated at the beginning of the passage.
2
Examine the example given
The example uses 'existentially, no white is [something]' and 'necessarily, every human is an animal'. The earlier scholars would infer an existential conclusion, but the author argues that a necessary conclusion ('necessarily, no white is a human') would be false under their view.
The example is partially legible due to OCR damage, but the structure is clear.
3
Analyse the author's objection regarding conversion
The author claims that the existential negative converts to a 'particular customary' (urfiyya khassa) and also has a general conversion. This conversion allows the premise to combine with the necessary minor premise in the first figure to yield a necessary conclusion.
The precise nature of 'urfiyya khassa' is unclear, but it refers to a contingent universal proposition.
4
Reconstruct the correct logical result
If the conversion is properly applied, the conclusion can indeed be necessary. The earlier scholars' restriction is therefore unfounded.
This step requires understanding that conversion of a negative existential proposition does not always weaken modality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولقد كان من مذهب المتقدمين أن السالبة إذا كانت وجودية خالية عن الضرورة كانت النتيجة وجودية خالية عن الضرورة. مثال: بالوجود لا شيء من الأبيض بعنوان [؟] وبالضرورة كل إنسان حيوان. فلو كانت النتيجة ضرورية لكان الحق بالضرورة لا شيء من الأبيض بإنسان وهو باطل. الجواب عن الأول: أن السالبة الوجودية لا تعكس إلا إلى العرفية الخاصة [؟] وجنين لا يكون عليها عكس خاص بل عكس عام محتمل للصورة وهو مع الصدى [؟] الضرورية النتيجة [؟] على ما من [؟]. ولئن علمنا كونها عرفية خاصة لكن [؟] هذه العرفية [؟] مع الصغرى التامة في الشكل الأول.
It is from the doctrine of the earlier scholars that if a negative [premise] is existential, free from necessity, then the conclusion is existential, free from necessity. Example: Existentially, no white is [؟]; and necessarily, every human is an animal. If the conclusion were necessary, it would be true necessarily that no white is a human, which is false. The answer to the first [objection]: that the existential negative does not convert except to a particular customary [؟]; and thus it does not have a particular conversion but a general conversion possible for the form, and it is with the echo [؟] the necessary conclusion [؟]. And if we know it is a particular customary, then this customary [؟] with the complete minor in the first figure.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage critiques a view held by earlier logicians: that a negative existential premise (a proposition that is merely existential, not necessary) combined with a necessary premise yields only an existential conclusion, not a necessary one. The author gives an example: 'Existentially, no white is [something]' and 'Necessarily, every human is an animal.' If the conclusion were necessary, it would be 'Necessarily, no white is a human,' which is deemed false. The rebuttal focuses on the conversion of negative existential propositions. The earlier scholars claimed that such a negative converts only to a particular customary (i.e., a contingent universal?); but the author argues that the conversion is not limited to that, and that with the correct conversion and the complete minor in the first figure, the conclusion can indeed be necessary. This is a technical point in modal conversion theory. The argument shows that the earlier scholars' restriction is unfounded, and the proper rules of conversion allow a necessary conclusion even with an existential negative premise under certain conditions.

عرفية خاصة

Refuting the Necessity of Existential Negative Conclusions
Author refutes earlier logicians' claim about existential negative premises in modal syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of a proof by contradiction (khulf) involving modal syllogisms and evaluate the validity of the claim that existential negative premises necessitate existential conclusions.

Core Idea
The modality of a syllogism's conclusion is not merely inherited from the premise's existential nature.

This passage presents a dialectical dispute in classical Islamic logic. Earlier logicians argued that if the minor premise is a negative existential proposition (e.g., 'no C is B' without necessity), the conclusion must be existential as well. They supported this with a proof by contradiction. Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, the author, refutes their view by exposing flaws in the conversion rules and the truth of their example. We will dissect this exchange to sharpen your ability to handle modal syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the earlier doctrine and their example
Locate the statement: 'It was the doctrine of the earlier scholars that if the negative is existential without necessity, the conclusion is existential without necessity.' Then find their example: 'By existence, nothing of C is B; by necessity, every A is C; therefore, by existence, nothing of A is B.'
The variables A, B, C are reconstructed from the corrupted Arabic; the actual text uses ج، ب، رج.
2
Reconstruct their proof by contradiction (khulf)
They assume the conclusion is necessary (e.g., 'by necessity, nothing of A is B'). Then, by converting the minor premise and combining it with the other premises, they derive a contradiction involving a necessary particular negative. Since that contradicts the original existential claim, the assumption is false, so the conclusion must be existential.
This is a compound argument: modus tollens on the conditional 'if conclusion necessary then contradiction'.
3
List the author's three objections
The author objects: (1) The existential negative does not convert in the way assumed; its converse is restricted. (2) The particular state of the example may not generalize. (3) The example itself is false because 'some white thing is necessarily an animal' is true, so the premise that led to contradiction is false.
Objection 3 undermines the truth of the minor premise in their example.
4
Understand the key rule: conversion of existential negatives
An existential negative ('no S is P' without necessity) does not convert simply to a universal or particular negative; the conversion preserves modality and requires specific conditions. The earlier scholars overlooked this, which invalidates their proof.
This is a central lesson in modal logic: conversion rules depend on the modality of the proposition.
5
Evaluate the overall refutation
The author's three objections cumulatively show that the earlier claim is not universally valid. The first objection is technical, the second logical, and the third empirical. Together they demonstrate that the conclusion's modality does not automatically follow from the premise's existential nature.
In modern terms, this refines our understanding of modal syllogistics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لقد كان من مذهب المتقدمين أن السالبة إذا كانت وجودية خالية عن الضرورة كانت النتيجة وجودية خالية عن الضرورة. مثال: بالوجود لا شيء من ج، وبالضرورة كل ب ج، فأنتجوا بالوجود لا شيء من ب. واحتجوا عليه بوجه: وهو أن نعكس الصغرى السالبة ونجعلها كبرى، ونجعل الكبرى [؟] كلات، وبالوجود لا شيء من به فنا، فالوجود لا شيء من رج. فإن الخلف لو كانت النتيجة بالضرورة لا شيء من ج، وهو بالضرورة لا شيء من اج حقا، فتجعلها كبرى، ونعكس كبرى الفناس الأول صغرى هكذا: بعض ب أو بالضرورة لا شيء من اج، فبالضرورة ليس بعض تج. هذا خلف؛ لأن الصغرى التالية الوجودية تنعكس وجودية، وكون السلبية وجودية في الكل يلزم كون ضرورية في البعض، ولم يلزم ذلك إلا من فرضنا النتيجة ضرورية. فهي إذن ليست بضرورية بل وجودية. ومثلوا بهذا المثال: بالوجود لا شيء من الأبيض بعنوان، وبالضرورة كل إنسان حيوان. فلو كانت النتيجة ضرورية لكان الحق بالضرورة لا شيء من الأبيض بإنسان، وهو باطل. الجواب عن الأول: أن السالبة الوجودية لا تعكس إلا إلى عكس [؟] عفية خاصة، وجنين لا يكون عليها ع فيا خاصا بل عقبا عاما محتملا للصورة وهو مع الصدى [؟] الفورية نت الدائمة على ما من. ولئن علمنا كون عليها عفا خاصا لكنا بناات هذه العرفية الإقليم مع الصغرى التامة في الشكل الأول. وعن الثاني: أن الحالة خاصة. وعن الثالث: أن التالية كاذبة؛ لأن بعض ما يقال له أبيض فهو بالضرورة حيوان.
It was the doctrine of the earlier scholars that if the negative is existential without necessity, the conclusion is existential without necessity. Example: By existence, nothing of J is B? Actually, the text is corrupt. The argument involves converting the minor negative premise and using a proof by contradiction (khulf). They argue that if the conclusion were necessary, a contradiction would arise, so it must be existential. The example: By existence, nothing of white is something? And by necessity, every human is an animal. If the conclusion were necessary, then 'by necessity, nothing of white is human' would be true, which is false. The answers: First, the existential negative does not convert except to a specific [?] converse. Second, the state is specific. Third, the proposition is false because some white thing is necessarily an animal.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical exchange regarding modal syllogisms. The author first states the earlier scholars' view: if the minor premise is a negative existential (i.e., 'no S is P' without necessity), then the conclusion is also existential without necessity. They provide an example: 'By existence, nothing of C is B' and 'By necessity, every A is C' yields 'By existence, nothing of A is B'. They then offer a proof by contradiction: assuming the conclusion is necessary leads to a contradiction when the premises are manipulated. The author then presents three objections to this proof. First, the existential negative does not convert in the way assumed; its converse is not simply universal but requires specific conditions. Second, the particular state of the example may not generalize. Third, the example itself is false because some white things (like snow? but snow is not an animal) are necessarily animals? Actually, the author argues that 'some white thing is necessarily an animal' is true, so the premise that led to the contradiction is false. Thus, the original view is refuted. The discussion highlights the importance of proper conversion rules and the interplay of modality in syllogisms.

Key Terms
سالبة وجودية (sāliba wujūdiyya): A negative proposition without modal necessity.
خلف (khulf): Proof by contradiction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) The conclusion was necessary, not existential.
D) The proof used a false assumption.

Key Takeaway
The modality of a syllogism's conclusion cannot be inferred solely from the modality of its premises without considering conversion rules and truth conditions.

Show the steps and any contradictions.
?

Classification of Modal Propositions and Second Figure Rules
Author classifies thirteen modal propositions and states second-figure rule for necessary premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the thirteen modal propositions into those that are universal and convertible and those that are not, and determine the modal strength of conclusions in second-figure syllogisms when one premise is necessary.

Core Idea
Modal strength dominates: a necessary premise ensures a necessary conclusion in the second figure.

This passage from al-Razi's Mulakhkhas lays out the foundation for modal syllogisms by first dividing the thirteen modal proposition types into two broad categories. Understanding this classification is crucial because the modal quality of premises directly determines the modal quality of conclusions, especially in the second figure where necessity dominates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two broad categories
The passage states that the thirteen propositions divide into those that are universal and convertible and those that are not. Count how many types fall into each category—the first category has six types (universal and convertible), and the second has seven types (non-universal and non-convertible).
The exact list of the six is not given, but the second category's seven types are listed (though corrupted).
2
List the seven non-universal/non-convertible types
The passage mentions: necessary (ḍarūriyya), permanent (dāʾima), two something (likely 'gharībatān' meaning strange/exceptive), and two conditional (mashrūṭatān). Note that necessary and permanent are emphasized as they carry necessity.
Due to OCR corruption, the exact terms for 'gharībatān' and 'mashrūṭatān' may vary.
3
Understand the rule for the second figure
The passage explicitly states: if one premise is necessary or permanent, and the other premise is any premise, then the conclusion is necessary or permanent. This rule applies only to the second figure and shows that necessity dominates.
This is a key rule for modal syllogisms: a necessary premise forces a necessary conclusion regardless of the other premise's modality.
4
Apply the rule to specific premise combinations
The passage examines cases where the minor premise is one of the seven types and the major is from a set of four (likely 'urfi propositions). For example, if the minor is absolute general or absolute special, the conclusion is possible general. This illustrates how the rule works with non-necessary premises.
The specific combinations are partially preserved; the main logical point is the dominance of necessity when present.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن القضايا الثلث عشرة تنقسم إلى ما سوى لها الكلية منعكسة وإلى ما لا يكون كذلك. والثاني سبعة أنواع: الضرورية والدائمة والغرافيتان والمشروطنان والنظر فيها يقع على مئة أوجه: أحدها انعقاد القياس من بعضها مع بعض بسطا أو مختلطا، وثانيها الاقية التي تجعل منها أحد السبعة صغرى وأحد السنة كبرى، وثالثها الاقية التي تكون بالعكس من ذلك. لكنك لعرفت أن هذا الشكل (الثاني) إذا كانت إحدى مقدمتيه ضرورية أو دائمة وكانت المقدمة الأخرى أي مقدمة كانت كانت النتيجة ضرورية أو دائمة. فإن لم يكن في تقصير أقسامها فائدة فقط من الست انسان ولقيت أريع العرفينات والمشروطنات. المنظور الأول من الاقسة التي تجعل فيها إحدى السبعة متوى وأحدى الأربعة كبرى: فلو كانت الصغرى مطلقة عامة أو خاصة كانت النتيجة ممكنة عامة، وإن كانت إحدى الخير الباقية عن المطلقة العامة والوجودية اللاضرورية والوجودية...
Thus the thirteen propositions divide into those that are universal and convertible and those that are not. The second group has seven types: necessary, permanent, the two [something], the two conditional... and the consideration of them occurs in a hundred aspects: first, forming a syllogism from some with others, simply or mixed; second, the [?] which make one of the seven a minor and one of the six a major; third, the [ess?] which are the opposite of that. But you have known that this figure (the second) if one of its premises is necessary or permanent and the other premise is any premise, the conclusion is necessary or permanent. So if there is no shortcoming in its divisions, the benefit is only from the six [?] and you meet the four [?] and the conditionals. The first perspective from the [propositions?] which make one of the seven a minor and one of the four a major: if the minor is absolute general or special, the conclusion is possible general; but if it is one of the remaining [types] besides absolute general, existential non-necessary, and existential...
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage classifies the thirteen types of modal propositions (like necessary, permanent, etc.) into two broad categories: those that are universal and convertible, and those that are not. The second category contains seven types, which are listed (though the OCR obscures the list). The author then discusses the ways these propositions can combine in syllogisms, focusing on the second figure. He states that if one premise is necessary or permanent (both of which imply necessity), then regardless of the other premise, the conclusion will be necessary or permanent. Then he examines specific mixtures: when the minor is one of the seven types and the major is one of the four types (likely the 'urfi' propositions), the conclusion varies. The passage ends mid-sentence, indicating a detailed analysis of moods. The purpose is to establish the rules for valid modal syllogisms in the second figure, emphasizing that necessity in one premise forces necessity in the conclusion. The classification of propositions is foundational for this analysis.

Mixed Modal Syllogisms in Second Figure: Rules and Proof
Author explains results when one of seven modal propositions is minor and one of four is major.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to predict the modality of the conclusion in a second-figure mixed modal syllogism given the types of minor and major premises, and explain the logical reasoning for negative major premises.

Core Idea
The minor's type determines the conclusion's modality; the major's quality shapes the reasoning.

In the second figure of categorical syllogisms, mixing premises of different modalities (necessary, possible, absolute) yields conclusions with specific modal strengths. Fakhr al-Din al-Razi in his Mulakkhas investigates combinations where the minor is from seven modal types and the major from four categorical types. This lesson walks through his rules and the proof for negative major premises, using the logical variables he employs (middle, minor, major).

Guided Path
1
Classify the minor premise
Identify which of the seven types the minor belongs to: is it 'hakiyya' (absolute) general/particular, or one of the other five (absolute general, non-necessary existential, etc.)? This classification determines the rule to apply.
The term 'hakiyya' is uncertain; it likely refers to absolute propositions.
2
Apply the corresponding rule
If the minor is 'hakiyya' general or particular, the conclusion is 'possible general' (imkan 'amm). If it is any of the other five, the conclusion is 'absolute general' (itlaq 'amm). The major premise's quantity (universal/particular) does not change this rule.
The rule is stated without proof for the second case; the passage only proves the first.
3
Trace the proof for negative majors
Assume the major is negative. It indicates that the middle term never coexists with the major term. The minor indicates that the minor term can separate from the middle. From this, it follows that the minor can be empty of the major (since if A excludes B, and C can separate from A, then C can be without B). The modality 'general possibility' results because the separation could be either necessary or contingent.
This uses the principle 'from the possibility of the opposite to the opposite'.
4
Consider affirmative majors
If the major is affirmative, it indicates that the major does not separate from the middle. The minor shows the minor can separate from the middle. Therefore, the minor can be empty of the major (if it lacks the middle, it lacks the major). The modality becomes 'absolute general', covering both necessary and contingent cases.
This relies on the principle 'if A is necessary for B, then lacking A implies lacking B'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النظر الأول من الأقيسة التي تجعل فيها إحدى السبعة صغرى وإحدى الأربعة كبرى: فقولك الصغرى إن كانت حكية عامة أو خاصة كانت النتيجة مكنة عامة، وإن كانت إحدى الخمس الباقية عن المطلقة العامة والوجودية اللاضرورية والوجودية اللادائمة والوقتية والمنتشرة كانت مطلقة عامة. بيان الأول: إن الكبريات الأربع إن كانت سالبة فهي مشتركة في الدلالة على أن الأوسط لا يجتمع مع الأكبر، والصغرى تدل على جواز انفكاك الأصغر عن الأوسط، فإذا دلت الصغرى على جواز انفكاك الأمر بالأوسط وجب في تلك الحالة جواز خلوه عن الأكبر استدلالاً بجواز المنافي على المنافي، ثم احتمال كون ذلك الانتفاء ضرورياً أو غير ضروري، والمشترك هو الإمكان العام. وإن كانت موجبة فهي مشتركة في الدلالة على أن الأكبر لا ينفك عن الأوسط، والصغرى دالة على جواز خلو الأصغر عن الأوسط، ففي تلك الحالة يجب جواز خلوه عن الأكبر استدلالاً بجواز الخلو عن اللازم على جواز الخلو عن الملزوم، ثم احتمال كون ذلك الخلو واجباً أو غير واجب، والمشترك هو الإطلاق العام.
The first consideration of the syllogisms in which one of the seven is made a minor premise and one of the four a major premise: You say, if the minor is absolute general or absolute particular, the conclusion is possible general. If it is one of the remaining five—the absolute general, the non-necessary existential, the non-perpetual existential, the temporal, and the widespread—the conclusion is absolute general. Explanation of the first: The four major premises, if negative, are common in indicating that the middle does not coexist with the major. The minor indicates the possibility of the separation of the minor from the middle. So when the minor indicates the possibility of separation by the middle, in that situation the possibility of its being empty of the major is necessary, by inference from the possibility of the opposite to the opposite. Then the possibility that this negation is necessary or not necessary; the common factor is general possibility. If they are affirmative, they are common in indicating that the major does not separate from the middle. The minor indicates the possibility of the minor being empty of the middle. In that situation, the possibility of its being empty of the major is necessary, by inference from the possibility of being empty of the necessary to the possibility of being empty of the necessitated. Then the possibility that this emptiness is necessary or not necessary; the common factor is absolute generality.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage is a technical discussion of mixed modal syllogisms in the second figure. It begins by defining the 'first consideration' (al-nazar al-awwal): cases where one of the seven types of modal propositions (the 'seven' likely refers to necessary, perpetual, affirmative general, negative general, possible, temporal, and widespread) serves as the minor premise, and one of the four (the 'four' likely refers to the categorical propositions: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative) serves as the major premise. The author states two rules: (1) if the minor is 'hakiyya' (possibly meaning 'judgmental' or 'absolute') general or particular, the conclusion is 'possible general' (mumkina 'amma); (2) if the minor is any of the other five types (absolute general, existential non-necessary, existential non-perpetual, temporal, or widespread), the conclusion is 'absolute general' (mutlaqa 'amma). The author then provides a detailed proof for the first rule, considering both negative and affirmative major premises. For negative majors: since the major premise indicates that the middle and major never co-exist, and the minor indicates that the minor can separate from the middle, it follows that the minor can also be empty of the major (by the rule that if A excludes B, and C can separate from A, then C can be without B). The conclusion's modality is 'general possibility' (imkan 'amm) because the separation could be either necessary or contingent. For affirmative majors: the major indicates that the major does not separate from the middle. The minor indicates the minor can separate from the middle. Therefore, the minor can be empty of the major (since if it can lack the middle, which is necessary for the major, it can lack the major). The resulting modality is 'absolute general' (itlaq 'amm), which covers both necessary and contingent possibilities. This passage is dense and assumes familiarity with the classification of modal propositions typical in medieval Islamic logic.

General Absolute from Modal Premises
Author explains combination of modal premises yielding general absolute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to trace how a negative or positive major premise combined with a possible minor yields a general absolute conclusion.

Core Idea
General absolute is the common result of modal syllogisms when the minor has possibility.

This passage from al-Razi's Mukhtasar al-Mulakhkhas examines the first consideration of modal syllogisms. Al-Razi explains that when the major premise is negative or positive, and the minor premise asserts possibility, the resulting conclusion is a general absolute – a modalized judgment that does not specify necessity or perpetuity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases
The passage distinguishes a negative major premise and a positive major premise. Both are combined with a minor premise that asserts possibility.
2
Analyze the negative major case
The negative major indicates that the middle and greater do not combine. When the minor asserts the possibility of the subject being the middle, we infer the possibility of the subject lacking the greater. This common result is called 'general possibility' (al-imkan al-'amm).
The absence of the greater could be necessary or not – the general possibility covers both.
3
Analyze the positive major case
The positive major indicates that the greater is not less than the middle (i.e., the greater encompasses the middle). When the minor asserts the possibility of the subject separating from the middle, we infer the possibility of the subject being empty of the greater. The common result is the 'general absolute' (al-itlaq al-'amm).
Here too, the separation could be necessary or not.
4
Recognize the common outcome
In both cases, the conclusion is a general absolute – a proposition that is true in actuality without specifying necessity, perpetuity, or other modal restrictions.
This general absolute is the basic form of modal judgment used in further syllogistic reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The non-permanent, the temporal, and the spread were general absolute. By the first of the four major premises: if it is negative, they share in indicating that the middle and the greater do not combine. So if the minor indicates the possibility of the things being described by the middle, then the necessity of [something] remains, its emptiness from the sphere, so in that case [we] infer from the possibility of the contrary to the impossibility of the [original], [considering] that it is possible that that impossibility be necessary or not; and the common is the general possibility. And if it is positive, they share in indicating that the greater is not less than the middle. Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the first consideration in modal syllogisms, specifically analyzing the four types of major premises (kubrayat arba') when combined with minor premises. The author first considers the case where the major premise is negative, indicating that the middle and the greater cannot coexist. When the minor premise asserts the possibility of the subject being described by the middle, a inference is drawn: because the contrary of the middle (the greater) is possible, its absence from the subject is also possible, but this absence could be necessary or not—the common result is 'general possibility' (al-imkan al-'amm). Next, the author considers the positive major, which indicates that the greater is not less than the middle (i.e., the greater includes the middle). When the minor asserts the possibility of the smaller separating from the middle, we infer the possibility of the smaller being empty of the greater. Again, this emptiness could be necessary or not, and the common product is called 'general' (al-itlaq al-'amm), meaning a modalized absolute judgment. The author uses terms like 'general absolute' and 'general possibility' to capture the logical force of such combinations.

Why Some Modal Syllogisms Fail: The Second Consideration
Author examines syllogisms where the major is one of four and the minor is one of seven, showing non-productivity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify invalid modal syllogisms where major and minor premises have mismatched modal categories, using al-Razi's analysis from al-Mulakhkhas.

Core Idea
Mismatched modalities break syllogistic productivity.

In this passage, al-Razi examines a second type of syllogism where the major premise is one of four modal categories (possible affirmative, possible negative, necessary affirmative, necessary negative) and the minor is one of seven (absolute, possible, necessary, etc.). He shows that when the major is a possible premise, no valid conclusion follows. This is a critical insight in Islamic modal logic: the modal status of premises must align for a syllogism to produce certain knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four major types and seven minor types
Al-Razi mentions 'one of the four' and 'one of the seven'. The four majors likely include the two possible (mumkin) and two necessary (daruri) premises; the seven minors cover absolute, possible, necessary, etc. Understand that these are modal categories.
The exact classification is not fully recoverable due to OCR issues, but the general idea is clear.
2
Analyze the negative possible major case
If the major is a possible negative (e.g., 'It is possible that the greater is not the middle'), and the minor is one of the four types indicating that the smaller is not less than the middle (i.e., the smaller includes the middle), then from 'possibly not' and 'necessarily includes' you cannot conclude that the smaller is empty of the greater. The syllogism is unproductive.
Al-Razi notes that the possibility of the smaller being empty of the greater does not follow.
3
Analyze the positive possible major case
If the major is a possible positive (e.g., 'It is possible that the greater is described by the middle'), and the minor indicates that the smaller and middle are incompatible, then the greater being possibly described by the middle implies the greater separates from the smaller, but not that the smaller separates from the greater. The desired conclusion (smaller separates from greater) does not follow.
This shows that the direction of separation matters in modal syllogisms.
4
Recognize the general principle
Al-Razi explains using two contradictory attributes: if an essence can possibly have each attribute but not both, then conditionally necessarily, whatever has one lacks the other. But this conditional necessity does not yield a simple categorical conclusion. The mismatch between possibility and necessity modes prevents entailment.
This highlights the subtlety of modal logic: necessity and possibility interact in complex ways.

The second consideration of the syllogisms in which one of the four is made major and one of the seven minor. So you say: 'something of that substance is not open'. If we take the major as one of the two possibilities, if it is negative it indicates the possibility of the greater separating from the middle, and the four minors indicate that the smaller is not less than the middle; so from the possibility of the greater separating from the middle which is necessary for the smaller, follows the possibility of the smaller being empty of it, but you know that from this does not follow the correctness of its emptiness from the greater, so the conclusion is not obtained. And if it is positive, it indicates the possibility of the greater being described by the middle, and the minors indicate that the smaller and the middle do not combine; so from the openness of the greater being described by the middle which prevents the smaller, follows the correctness of its separation from the smaller, but from this does not follow the correctness of the smaller separating from the greater at all, so that was the desired [but] not productive. And if we assume two contradictory attributes for the same essence, and it is not possible for one of them to occur except on that essence, and each of them is possibly separable from it, then it is correct to say conditionally necessarily: 'whatever has one of the two attributes is not described by the other', and every such essence is possibly described by that other, but it does not follow that it is said necessarily... [text corrupt]. And if we make the major one of the remaining parts, if it is negative its meaning is the separation of the greater from the middle, and this requires the middle to be empty of the greater, because you know that these absolutes are not restricted... then there is no safety from the smaller being described by the middle and being empty of the greater... and if it is positive its meaning is the description of the greater by the middle, so it is [something] and emptiness from it... so if we [combine] with the minors on the smaller from the middle, there is no [conclusion].

Commentary
This passage is an analysis of the second consideration in modal syllogisms, where the major premise is one of the four major types (e.g., possible affirmative, possible negative, necessary affirmative, etc.) and the minor premise is one of the seven minor types (e.g., absolute, possible, necessary, etc.). The author first considers when the major is one of the two 'possible' types (al-mumkinayn). If the major is negative, it states the possibility of the greater separating from the middle. The four minors indicate that the smaller is not less than the middle (i.e., the smaller includes the middle). From these, we can infer that the smaller possibly lacks the greater? Actually, the reasoning is: if the greater can separate from the middle, and the middle is necessary for the smaller (since the smaller includes the middle), then the smaller could be empty of the middle? Wait, the author says: from the possibility of the greater separating from the middle, which is necessary for the smaller, follows the possibility of the smaller being empty of it (i.e., empty of the middle?), but this does not yield the conclusion that the smaller is empty of the greater. So no valid conclusion. Similarly, if the major is positive, it indicates the possibility of the greater being described by the middle, while the minors say the smaller and the middle are incompatible. So the greater being described by the middle prevents the smaller, so the greater separates from the smaller, but not necessarily vice versa. Thus the desired conclusion (that the smaller separates from the greater) does not follow, so the syllogism is not productive. The author then gives a general principle about two contradictory attributes: if an essence can have each attribute possibly but not both, then conditionally necessarily, whatever has one attribute is not described by the other, but this still does not yield a simple necessary judgment. The passage concludes that when the major is taken from the remaining types (not the two possibilities), similar issues arise: if the major is negative, it requires the middle to be empty of the greater, but the absolutes are not restricted, so no valid syllogism; if it is positive, it requires the greater to be described by the middle, and again no conclusion. Overall, this shows that combining one of the four majors with one of the seven minors does not produce a sound syllogism due to modal mismatches.

Third Figure Syllogisms with General Customary Premises
Examination of third figure syllogisms with general customary premises

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the conclusion type of a third-figure syllogism when both premises are general customary propositions ('urfiyya 'amma).

Core Idea
In the third figure, general customary premises yield a general customary conclusion, without necessity.

This passage from al-Mulakhkhas examines the third figure of categorical syllogisms when both premises are 'urfiyya 'amma (general customary) propositions—those that assert permanent affirmation or negation without implying necessity. The author shows that the conclusion retains this same modal status. Understanding this rule helps you handle modal syllogisms in Islamic logic, where knowing the modality (necessity vs. permanence) is crucial for sound reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure and premise types
Note that the syllogism is in the third figure (the middle term is the subject of both premises). Both premises are labeled 'urfiyya 'amma (general customary), meaning they assert permanent truth without necessity.
The third figure is also called shakl thālith.
2
Analyze the consequence for the affirmative case
The author argues that when both premises are positive and 'urfiyya 'amma, the middle term's permanent relation to one extreme and permanent negation of the other entails a permanent opposition between the minor and major descriptions, but without necessity. Therefore the conclusion is also general customary.
Consider why necessity is absent: the permanence observed does not guarantee metaphysical necessity.
3
Consider the negative major case
When the major premise is negative, after conversion it remains negative. The mixture with the affirmative minor in the first figure yields a general customary conclusion. The reasoning: the major indicates impossibility of conjunction of middle and major, but the minor only shows the minor is not free from the middle without necessity, so the impossibility of minor-major conjunction is unknown, hence a general customary negative conclusion.
The author references a known rule from the first figure (al-ikhtilāṭ fī al-awwal).
4
Examine the positive major case and the special customary
If the major is affirmative, applying the reasoning leads to a negative conclusion that is general customary (via conversion). The text mentions a 'special customary' (ʿurfiyya khāṣṣa) intermediate step, but the final conclusion is still general customary.
The special customary differs in implying necessity; the general customary does not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب الثالث في الأقيسة المنعقدة من هذه الأربع. ولنذكرها على سبيل التحديد: في الصغرى العرفية العامة والكبرى العرفية العامة النتيجة عرفية عامة، لأن الأوسط دائم الثبوت باتصاف أحد الطرفين ودائم السلب بدوام اتصاف الطرف الآخر من غير أن نعرف أن ذلك الدوام في السلب والإيجاب ضروري أم لا، وذلك يقتضي دوام المبانية بين وصف الأصغر ووصف الأكبر من غير أن نعرف أن ذلك الدوام ضروري أم لا. فالناتج عرفية عامة. أما إن كانت الكبرى سالبة فلأنها بعد العكس تبقى على كيفها، وقد عرفت أن هذا الاختلاط في الأول ينتج العرفية العامة، ولأن الكبرى تدل على استحالة اجتماع الأوسط والأكبر، لكن الصغرى تدل على أن وصف الأصغر لا ينفك عن الأوسط من غير أن نعرف أن ذلك الانفكاك محال أم لا، وبتقدير حصول ذلك الانفكاك فلا ندري أن اجتماع الأصغر والأكبر هل هو ممكن أم لا، فالمسألة إذن: إن الأصغر والأكبر هل يجتمعان؟ فأما استحالة اجتماعهما فغير معلوم، فلاجرم كانت النتيجة عرفية عامة. وأما إن كانت موجبة فلنجعل التالية العرفية الكبرى فتحصل النتيجة سالبة عرفية عامة وتبقى بعد العكس كذلك، ولأن الكبرى دلت على استحالة خلو الأكبر عن الأوسط، والصغرى دلت على خلو الأصغر عن الأوسط من غير أن نعرف أن ذلك الخلو واجب أم لا، فلازم منه نجاة الأصغر عن الأكبر من غير أن نعرف أن ذلك واجب أم لا، وهو العرفية الخاصة، فالناتج عرفية عامة والنتيجة تابعة لها، ولأنها تدل على اتتلاف وصف الأكبر والأوسط الجمعان، فحين حصول الأوسط للأصغر وجب اجتماع الأصغر والأكبر، فتنعكس عرفية عامة مع ما إن كانت الكبرى سالبة فلأنها أزان [؟]
The third chapter on syllogisms composed of these four. We will mention them by way of specification: When the minor premise is a general customary proposition and the major premise is a general customary proposition, the conclusion is a general customary proposition, because the middle term is permanently affirmed in connection with one of the two extremes and permanently negated with the permanent affirmation of the other extreme, without us knowing whether that permanence in negation and affirmation is necessary or not, and that necessitates permanent opposition between the description of the minor and the description of the major without us knowing whether that permanence is necessary or not. So the result is a general customary proposition. As for if the major is negative, then because after conversion it remains in its form, and you already know that this mixture in the first produces a general customary proposition, and because the major indicates the impossibility of the conjunction of the middle and the major, but the minor indicates that the description of the minor is not free from the middle without us knowing whether that separation is impossible or not, and given that separation, we do not know whether the conjunction of the minor and the major is possible or not; the issue then is: do the minor and the major come together? As for the impossibility of their conjunction, it is unknown, so consequently the conclusion is a general customary proposition. And as for if it is positive, then let us make the following [?] the general customary major, so we obtain a negative conclusion that is general customary and remains after conversion likewise, and because the major indicates the impossibility of the major's being free from the middle, and the minor indicates the minor's being free from the middle without us knowing whether that freedom is necessary or not, then it follows from that the minor's escape from the major without us knowing whether that is necessary or not, and it is the special customary [proposition]; so the result is a general customary proposition and the conclusion is subordinate to it, and because it indicates the combination of the description of the major and the middle together, then when the middle is attained by the minor, the conjunction of the minor and the major becomes necessary, so it is converted into a general customary [proposition] along with if the major is negative then because it [?].
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage is a technical discussion of the third figure of the categorical syllogism when both premises are 'urufiyya 'amma (general customary propositions). The author first states that when both minor and major are 'urufiyya 'amma, the conclusion is also 'urufiyya 'amma. The reason is that the middle term's relation to one extreme is permanent in affirmation or negation, but without any necessity. This yields a permanent opposition (mubāyana) between the minor and major descriptions, again without necessity; hence the conclusion is a general customary proposition (wujūdī or salbī without necessity). Next, the author considers the case where the major is negative. Since the major proposition, after conversion (perhaps 'aks), remains negative, and the minor remains affirmative (as 'urufiyya 'amma), the combination in the first figure would produce a general customary conclusion. The logic is that the major indicates the impossibility of the middle and major co-existing, while the minor indicates that the minor description does not separate from the middle, but we do not know if that separation is impossible. Even if separation occurs, we do not know if the minor and major can meet. So the impossibility of their meeting is unknown, thus the conclusion is still 'urufiyya 'amma (i.e., a permanent negative without necessity). Finally, the author handles the case where the major is positive (i.e., affirmative). By treating the major as a general customary affirmative, the conclusion becomes a negative 'urufiyya 'amma (via conversion from an affirmative? The text is obscure). The reasoning: the major indicates the impossibility of the major being free from the middle, and the minor indicates the minor's freedom from the middle without necessity. This entails the minor's escape from the major (i.e., the minor is always separate from the major) again without necessity; that is a 'urufiyya khāṣṣa (special customary) proposition, but the conclusion is 'urufiyya 'amma (general customary) because it is subordinate. Because it indicates the combination of the major and middle together, when the middle holds for the minor, the conjunction of minor and major becomes necessary, and that converts (or is reflected) as a general customary proposition. The final clause about 'if the major is negative then because ...' is cut off. This passage illustrates the nuanced rules for determining the modality of conclusions in mixed syllogisms of the third figure, characteristic of post-Avicennan logic.

العرفية الخاصة

Rule for First Figure Mixed Syllogism with Two 'Urfiya 'Amma Premises
Author states rule for conclusion when both premises are 'urfiya 'amma in first figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conclusion type in a first-figure categorical syllogism when both premises are 'urfiya 'amma and explain why.

Core Idea
Two 'urfiya 'amma premises in first figure yield a 'urfiya 'amma conclusion.

In this passage, the author provides a key rule for generating conclusions from mixed syllogisms in the first figure when both premises are 'urfiya 'amma. Understanding this rule helps you determine the modality of the conclusion without needing to analyze each case individually. This is crucial for constructing valid syllogistic proofs in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
Locate the minor premise (ṣughrā) and major premise (kubrā). In the author's statement, both are described as 'urfiya 'amma.
2
Determine the figure
The passage specifies the first figure (al-shakl al-awwal), where the middle term is subject of one premise and predicate of the other.
3
State the rule
The conclusion is also 'urfiya 'amma, as explicitly stated: النتيجة عرفية عامة.
4
Understand the reasoning
The middle term is permanently predicated of one extreme and permanently negated of the other. This permanence ensures the conclusion holds as long as the descriptions remain, making it 'urfiya 'amma.
The permanence is not necessarily necessary (ضروري), just customary (عرفي).
5
Apply to an example
Consider: 'Every moving thing is changing' (major, 'urfiya 'amma) and 'Every body is moving' (minor, 'urfiya 'amma). The middle term 'moving' is permanently related to body and to change. Therefore, the conclusion 'Every body is changing' is also 'urfiya 'amma.

في الصغرى العرفية العامة والكبرى العرفية العامة النتيجة عرفية عامة لان الاوسط دائم الوصف لاحد الطرفين ودائم السلب عن الطرف الآخر من غير أن يكون ذلك الدوام ضروريا او لا.
In the minor premise being 'urfiya 'amma and the major premise being 'urfiya 'amma, the conclusion is 'urfiya 'amma, because the middle term is always described with one of the two extremes and always negated from the other extreme, without that permanence being necessary or not.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage describes a mixed syllogism in the first figure where both premises are 'urfiya 'amma (permanent descriptive propositions). The author states that the conclusion is also 'urfiya 'amma. The reason given is that the middle term is permanently (دائم) predicated of one extreme (the subject of the conclusion) and permanently negated of the other extreme (the predicate of the conclusion), without specifying whether this permanence is necessary (ضروري) or not. This is a rule for generating a conclusion under the qiyas (syllogism) framework. The permanence ensures that the relationship between the subject and predicate of the conclusion holds as long as the description remains, which is the characteristic of 'urfiya 'amma propositions. An example in Islamic logic: 'Every moving thing is changing' and 'Every body is moving' yields 'Every body is changing' – here the middle term 'moving' is permanently (as long as motion exists) predicated of body and permanently (as long as motion exists) negated of non-change. Thus the conclusion is a permanent descriptive statement.

The Syllogism

Understanding Modal Premises in First-Figure Syllogisms
Detailed case analysis of modal syllogisms in the first figure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the modality (necessary, customary, conditional) of the conclusion in a first-figure categorical syllogism when given the modal types of the premises.

Core Idea
In modal syllogisms, the conclusion's modality degrades to the weakest permanent relation among the premises.

Al-Rāzī's Mulakkhkhaṣ systematically examines how different modal premises combine in the first figure. The key is to assess whether the relations between terms are necessary (ḍarūrī), perpetual (dāʾim), or merely customary (ʿurfī). We will walk through one clear example: a minor premise that is a particular conditional (musharriṭa khāṣṣa) and a major that is a general customary (ʿurfiyya ʿāmma).

Guided Path
1
Identify the modal types
Read the premises and label their modality: 1) Minor: particular conditional (musharriṭa khāṣṣa) – the predicate holds of the subject only under a certain condition, not always. 2) Major: general customary (ʿurfiyya ʿāmma) – the predicate holds of the subject as a general rule, but not necessarily.
2
Check the middle term's relation
In the first figure, the middle term (ḥadd al-awsaṭ) appears in both premises. Determine whether the middle is necessarily or permanently related to the extremes.
Here, the minor only asserts a conditional relation, so the middle is not necessarily always present in the minor.
3
Determine the conclusion's modality
Since the minor lacks absolute permanence (dawām), the conclusion cannot be necessary. The major provides a customary generalization, so the conclusion inherits the customary modality.
The text says: 'al-natīja ʿurfiyya ʿāmma' (the conclusion is general customary).
4
Generalize the rule
In first-figure modal syllogisms, if any premise is not necessary, the conclusion becomes at most customary. Only when both premises are necessary (ḍarūrī) or perpetual does the conclusion match that strength.
This rule is confirmed by other cases in the passage.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of how various modal premises combine in first-figure syllogisms to yield conclusions of specific modal types. The author proceeds case by case, explaining the logical reasoning for each combination. He begins with the case where the minor premise is a particular conditional (مشروطة خاصة) and the major is a general customary (عرفية عامة), yielding a general customary conclusion. Then he discusses the minor general conditional (مشروطة عامة) with the major general customary, also yielding a general customary conclusion. Next, the minor general conditional with the major general conditional yields a general conditional conclusion, because the middle term is necessarily related to both extremes in the premises. For negative premises, he explains how conversion (عكس) operates and how the conclusion's modality is determined by the permanence or non-permanence of the negation. The author also addresses combinations with particular customary (عرفية خاصة) premises, showing that the conclusion is often general customary, as the particularity of the premise does not guarantee permanence. Throughout, he relies on the principles of the first figure and the nature of modal propositions. The key insight is that the modality of the conclusion is not simply a combination of the premises' modalities; rather, it depends on whether the relations are necessary, permanent, or merely conditional. The author uses terms like 'دوام' (perpetuity) and 'ضرورة' (necessity) to distinguish between strict necessity and customary generality. This passage is typical of medieval Islamic logic where logicians worked out exhaustive tables of modal syllogisms.

Conversion (ʿAks) in Modal Syllogisms of the First Figure
Use of conversion to obtain conclusions in modal syllogisms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to use the rule of conversion (عكس) to derive the conclusion in a first-figure modal syllogism when the major premise is negative and conditional.

Core Idea
Conversion of a negative conditional premise yields a proposition with the same modality, enabling a valid first-figure syllogism that otherwise might not yield a conclusion.

In his treatment of modal syllogisms, al-Rāzī often invokes conversion (عكس) to generate the conclusion, especially when the major premise is negative and of a particular modal type, such as particular customary (عرفية خاصة) or particular conditional (مشروطة خاصة). This lesson examines how conversion preserves or alters modality and how it is used in syllogistic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the need for conversion
When the major premise is negative and the minor is affirmative, the standard first-figure rule may not directly yield a conclusion unless the major is converted. The text says: 'لأن هذه الكبرى تبقى بعد العكس' (this major remains after conversion).
2
Perform the conversion
For a negative conditional premise like 'No B is C under condition D', conversion gives 'No C is B under condition D' (or similar). The modality is preserved if the condition is permanent.
The text treats the conversion of a particular customary (عرفية خاصة) as still customary.
3
Apply the first-figure rule after conversion
After converting the major, we have a new premise pair. For example, if the original minor is 'All A are B' (or under some condition) and the converted major is 'No C is B', then in first figure we get 'No A is C' with the same modality as the major's conversion.
The text says: 'فالنتيجة عرفية عامة' for the particular customary case.
4
Generalize the condition
Conversion is necessary when the major is negative and not absolutely necessary. The conclusion then takes the modality of the converted major, which is often customary because the original was not necessary.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of how various modal premises combine in first-figure syllogisms to yield conclusions of specific modal types. The author proceeds case by case, explaining the logical reasoning for each combination. He begins with the case where the minor premise is a particular conditional (مشروطة خاصة) and the major is a general customary (عرفية عامة), yielding a general customary conclusion. Then he discusses the minor general conditional (مشروطة عامة) with the major general customary, also yielding a general customary conclusion. Next, the minor general conditional with the major general conditional yields a general conditional conclusion, because the middle term is necessarily related to both extremes in the premises. For negative premises, he explains how conversion (عكس) operates and how the conclusion's modality is determined by the permanence or non-permanence of the negation. The author also addresses combinations with particular customary (عرفية خاصة) premises, showing that the conclusion is often general customary, as the particularity of the premise does not guarantee permanence. Throughout, he relies on the principles of the first figure and the nature of modal propositions. The key insight is that the modality of the conclusion is not simply a combination of the premises' modalities; rather, it depends on whether the relations are necessary, permanent, or merely conditional. The author uses terms like 'دوام' (perpetuity) and 'ضرورة' (necessity) to distinguish between strict necessity and customary generality. This passage is typical of medieval Islamic logic where logicians worked out exhaustive tables of modal syllogisms.

Modal Mixed Syllogisms in Third and Fourth Figures
Author explains the modality of conclusions in mixed syllogisms of third and fourth figures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the modality of the conclusion in a mixed syllogism of the third or fourth figure given the modalities of the premises.

Core Idea
The modality of the conclusion depends on the premises and figure constraints.

This passage from al-Razi's Mulakhkhas explains how to derive the modal strength of conclusions in mixed syllogisms of the third and fourth figures. Unlike the first two figures, these figures have special restrictions that often weaken the conclusion from necessary to conditional or possible. Understanding these rules is essential for evaluating theological and legal syllogisms where modal nuances matter.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure
Determine if the syllogism is in the third figure (middle term is subject of major and predicate of minor) or fourth figure (middle term is predicate of both premises).
2
Analyze premise modalities
Classify each premise as conditional (mashrūṭa), absolute (ʿurfiyya), or possible (mumkina). Note whether they are universal or particular.
A particular conditional minor asserts impossibility of absence in some cases.
3
Apply third figure rules
If minor is particular conditional and major is universal absolute, conclusion is universal conditional. If minor is universal conditional and major is particular absolute, conclusion is universal absolute.
The rule depends on whether the major is negative or affirmative.
4
Apply fourth figure rules
The first two modes (specific premise combinations) yield a universal possible conclusion. The remaining modes yield necessary conclusions after conversion.
The middle term is not necessary for its subjects, so possibility arises.
5
Verify the conclusion
Check that the conclusion's modality matches the rule and consider any exceptional cases mentioned by the author.

Mixed syllogisms in the third figure: The particular conditional minor and the absolute universal major produce a universal conditional conclusion. The reason is clear: because the major indicates the impossibility of the co-existence of the major term and the middle, and the minor indicates the impossibility of the absence of the middle from the minor term, so in this case it is impossible for the minor to be described by the major. As for the universal conditional with the particular absolute, it produces a universal absolute conclusion. If the major is negative, it indicates that the major and middle did not occur together without specifying whether that is necessary or not, but the description of the minor by the middle requires the certainty of its absence from the major without specifying whether that absence is necessary or not, so the conclusion is universal absolute. And if it is affirmative, we make the minor a universal major. As for the particular conditional that produces a universal conditional. In the fourth figure: The first and second modes produce a universal possible because the minor is necessary for the middle and the middle is not necessary for any of its subjects, so one of the two possibilities must be the case, which is universal possibility. As for the remaining modes, their conclusions are necessary because...
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage discusses the modality of conclusions in mixed syllogisms of the third and fourth figures. The author explains how different combinations of premise modalities yield different conclusion modalities. In the third figure, when the minor is a particular conditional proposition (premise that asserts the impossibility of the absence of a predicate) and the major is a universal absolute proposition, the conclusion is a universal conditional. The reasoning is that the major shows the major and middle cannot coexist, and the minor shows the minor cannot lack the middle, so the minor cannot have the major. When the premises are reversed (universal conditional minor and particular absolute major), the conclusion becomes universal absolute. For negative major premises, since the negation is not necessarily eternal, the conclusion is only absolute. In the fourth figure, the first two modes yield possible conclusions because the minor is necessarily connected to the middle, but the middle is not necessarily connected to any subject, so the conclusion is only possible. The remaining modes yield necessary conclusions due to conversion. Throughout, the author emphasizes the dependence of modal conclusions on premise types and figure constraints. For example, in fiqh, if we know that every intoxicant is necessarily haram (necessary major) and that wine is necessarily an intoxicant (necessary minor), the conclusion that wine is haram is necessary. But if the minor is only possible, the conclusion becomes possible.

Mixed Modal Syllogisms: Third Figure
Author analyzes mixed modal syllogisms in the third figure, determining when conclusions are necessary or possible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the modality (necessary or possible) of the conclusion in mixed modal syllogisms of the third figure by applying conversion and reduction to the second figure.

Core Idea
In the third figure, negative conclusions are necessary when a premise is necessary after reduction to the second figure.

Modal syllogisms combine premises with different modalities (e.g., necessary and absolute). In the third figure, the mood and the modality of the premises determine whether the conclusion is necessary or merely possible. This passage from al-Razi's Mulakhkhas explains two key results: (1) when the conclusion is a particular affirmative, the conclusion is a general possible; (2) when the conclusion is negative, after converting the major premise and reducing to the second figure, the conclusion becomes necessary. Understanding these rules is essential for constructing valid modal arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the modal premises in the third figure
Note that the author considers cases where one premise is absolute (غير ضرورية) and the other is necessary (ضرورية). The major premise is made necessary. Determine whether the conclusion is affirmative or negative.
The passage distinguishes two types: products for particular affirmatives and products for negatives.
2
Analyze the case of particular affirmative conclusion
For the particular affirmative, the conclusion is 'general possible' (ممكنة عامة). The minor premise shows the middle term is necessarily true of the major; since the minor is affirmed of all or some of the major, the major is possibly true of part of the minor.
The possibility here is general, not temporally restricted.
3
Analyze the case of negative conclusion
For the negative conclusions (three moods that produce two negatives), the conclusion is necessary. The reduction to the second figure is achieved by converting the major premise (which is negative and necessary). In the second figure, if one premise is necessary, the conclusion is necessary.
Conversion rules for modal propositions: a necessary negative converts to a necessary negative.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والموجبة المكنية كيف كانت تنعكس مكنة عامة أو بالرد إلى الثالث بعكس الكبرى وهى تعكس ممكنة عامة اختلاط المطلق والضروري ونجعل الكبرى ضرورية لها. فالمنتجات للموجبة الجزئية فالنتيجة فيهما ممكنة عامة لان الصغرى مكانة المنجي دلت على ان الأوسط ضروري الثبوت للأكبر او لبعضه فلام إن الأول لكون الاصغر ثابت لكل الأكبر أو لبعضه وذلك يقتضي أن يكون الأكبر ممكن الثبوت لما علمنا لبعض الاصغر فبالامكان العام بعض الاصغر. والثلاث المنتجة للسالبتين فالنتيجة فيها ضرورية لاننا بعكس الكبرى ترتد إلى الثاني والكبرى ضرورية ومتى كانت إحدى المقدمتين في الثاني ضرورية كانت النتيجة ضرورية.
And the affirmative possible proposition, however it is, converts to a general possible, or by reduction to the third by converting the major premise, and it converts to a general possible. The mixing of absolute and necessary, and we make the major premise necessary for it. As for the products for the particular affirmative, the conclusion in both is general possible, because the minor premise indicates that the middle is necessarily affirmed of the major or some of it; and accordingly, because the minor is affirmed of all the major or some of it, it necessitates that the major is possibly affirmed of that which we know is some of the minor, so by general possibility it is some of the minor. And the three that produce the two negatives, the conclusion in them is necessary, because we reduce it to the second by converting the major premise, and the major premise is necessary, and whenever one of the two premises in the second is necessary, the conclusion is necessary.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage is a technical analysis of mixed modal syllogisms in the third figure. The author examines two cases. First, when the conclusion is a particular affirmative (موجبة جزئية) and the premises are mixed (absolute and necessary), the conclusion is a general possible (ممكنة عامة). The justification is that the minor premise shows the middle term is necessarily true of the major, and since the minor is affirmed of all or part of the major, the major must be possibly true of that part of the minor, resulting in a general possible. Second, when both premises produce a negative conclusion (سالبتين), the conclusion is necessary (ضرورية). This is because by converting the major premise (which is negative and necessary), the syllogism reduces to the second figure, where one premise is necessary, and in the second figure, if one premise is necessary, the conclusion is necessary. Note: The author uses 'three that produce the two negatives' referring to three valid moods in the third figure that yield negative conclusions. The reduction method relies on conversion rules for modal propositions.

Modal Syllogisms in the Second Figure
Author discusses conclusions from mixed modal premises in the second figure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the conclusion modality (necessary or possible) in a second-figure categorical syllogism when one premise is necessary and the other possible.

Core Idea
In the second figure, a necessary premise produces a necessary conclusion only if the syllogism is negative; affirmative syllogisms yield possible conclusions.

In this lesson, we examine how the second figure of categorical syllogism handles mixed modal premises. The author of the Mulakhkhas explains the rules for when a necessary premise (al-daruriyya) is combined with a possible premise (al-mumkina), focusing on the conclusions that follow for affirmative particular and negative universal/particular syllogisms. Understanding these rules is essential for constructing sound modal arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure and premises
First, confirm the syllogism is in the second figure (middle term predicate in both premises). Then note the modality of each premise: one is necessary (e.g., 'Every A is necessarily B') and the other is possible (e.g., 'Some C is possibly B').
The second figure's mood determines the conclusion's modality.
2
Check the quality and quantity
Determine if the syllogism is affirmative or negative, and universal or particular. The passage distinguishes two cases: affirmative particular (وجبة جزئية) and negative universal/particular (سالتين).
Affirmative particular means the conclusion is 'Some S is P'; negative means 'No S is P' or 'Some S is not P'.
3
Apply the modal rule for affirmative particular
If the syllogism is affirmative particular, the conclusion is 'possibly general' (ممكنة عامة). This means the predicate holds possibly for all or some of the subject, because the minor premise indicates possibility for the middle, and the major indicates necessity for the major term, so the major term is possibly affirmed for the minor.
The Arabic phrase 'ممكنة عامة' indicates general possibility, not particular.
4
Apply the modal rule for negative syllogisms
If the syllogism is negative (either universal or particular), the conclusion is necessary. By converting the minor premise (عكس), the syllogism reduces to a second-figure form where both premises are necessary (or one necessary and one after conversion), yielding a necessary conclusion.
Conversion of the minor changes the figure but preserves truth; the modal rule for the second figure then applies.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المنتجات للموجبة الجزئية فالنتيجة فيهما ممكنة عامة لأن الصغرى ممكنة دلت على أن الأوسط ضروري الثبوت للأكبر أو لبعضه فإن الأول لكون الأصغر ثابت لكل الأكبر أو لبعضه وذلك يقتضي أن يكون الأكبر ممكن الثبوت للأصغر ... أما الثلاث المنتجة للسالبتين فالنتيجة فيها ضرورية لأننا بعكس الصغرى ترتد إلى الثاني والكبرى ضرورية ومتى كانت إحدى المقدمتين في الثاني ضرورية كانت النتيجة ضرورية. ولنجعل الضرورية كبرى فاما المنتجات للموجبة الجزئية فالنتيجة فيها ممكنة عامة لأن الصغرى دلت على أن الأصغر ممكن الثبوت للأوسط والكبرى دلت على أن الأوسط ضروري الثبوت للأكبر أو لبعضه فيلزم أن يكون الأكبر ممكن الثبوت للأصغر إمكانا عاما. وأما المنتجات السالبة الجزئية فالنتيجة فيها ضرورية لأنها تعكس الصغرى يريدان إلى الثاني وقد عرفت أن هذا الاختلاط ينتج الضروري هناك.
As for the products for the affirmative particular, the conclusion in both is possibly general because the minor is possible, indicating that the middle is necessarily affirmed for the major or for some of it, because the minor is fixed for all the major or for some of it, and that requires that the major be possibly affirmed for the minor... As for the three products for the two negatives, the conclusion in them is necessary because by converting the minor we return to the second figure and the major is necessary, and whenever one of the two premises in the second is necessary, the conclusion is necessary. Let us make the necessary major: as for the products for the affirmative particular, the conclusion in them is possibly general because the minor indicates that the minor is possibly affirmed for the middle, and the major indicates that the middle is necessarily affirmed for all or some of the major, so it follows that the major is possibly affirmed for the minor with general possibility. As for the products for the negative particular, the conclusion in them is necessary because they convert the minor to the second figure, and you have known that this mixture produces the necessary there.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage is a syllogistic classification of modal syllogisms in the second figure (qiyas iqtirani with middle predicate in both). The author explains the conclusion types based on the modality (possible or necessary) and quantity (universal/particular, affirmative/negative) of the premises. He states that when we have a possible minor and a necessary major in an affirmative particular syllogism, the conclusion is 'possibly general' (mumkin 'amm) because the minor's possibility combined with the middle's necessity for the major yields that the major is possibly true for the minor. For the negative universal syllogisms, the conclusion is necessary because after conversion of the minor (عكس), the syllogism reduces to the second figure with a necessary major, and in that figure a necessary premise produces a necessary conclusion. The passage then considers the case where the major is necessary and the minor is possible, yielding a 'possibly general' conclusion for affirmative particulars, and a necessary conclusion for negative particulars (after conversion). This follows the standard doctrine in Modal Syllogistics within the Aristotelian tradition as transmitted by al-Razi. The examples, though not explicit, would relate to Islamic categories such as: 'Every human is possibly writing. Every writing is necessarily created. Therefore, some created is possibly human.' But the text does not provide concrete examples.
