Valid and Invalid Inferences in Conjunctive Conditionals
Inference rules for conjunctive conditionals and their validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify valid and invalid inference forms in a conjunctive conditional (muttasila lizumiyya) and explain the logical reason for the invalidity of denying the antecedent and affirming the consequent.

Core Idea
In a conjunctive conditional, only affirming the antecedent (modus ponens) and denying the consequent (modus tollens) are valid.

Conditional propositions (al-shartiyya al-muttasila) form the backbone of much logical reasoning in Islamic theology and jurisprudence. But not every inference that looks plausible is actually valid. This passage by Fakhr al-Din al-Razi walks us through the four possible inference patterns and shows exactly which two are sound and why the other two fail. Understanding this will protect you from common logical fallacies when reasoning about conditional statements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of conjunctive conditionals
The author divides conjunctive conditionals into implicational (lizumiyya) and accidental (ittifaqiyya). Focus on the implicational, where the antecedent necessarily entails the consequent.
Accidental conditionals (e.g., 'If the moon is eclipsed, then the sky is clear') do not support inference.
2
List the four possible inference forms
The author describes four operations on a conditional: (1) affirm the antecedent, (2) deny the consequent, (3) deny the antecedent, (4) affirm the consequent. Which are valid?
These correspond to modus ponens, modus tollens, denying the antecedent fallacy, and affirming the consequent fallacy.
3
Determine validity of each form based on the text
The author states that forms (1) and (2) are valid, while (3) and (4) are not. The reason is that the consequent may be more general than the antecedent (al-lazim a'amm min al-malzum).
For example: 'If it is a cat, then it is an animal.' Affirming the animal (consequent) doesn't guarantee it is a cat.
4
Examine the author's refutation of the equivalence claim
Some people claimed that if antecedent and consequent are equivalent, all four moods become valid. The author refutes this because implication is directional; even if two propositions are materially equivalent, the conditional itself remains directional.
The contrapositive (denying the consequent) is actually a different conditional: 'If not B then not A'.
5
Analyze the given example: 'If man is rational, he brays.'
The author uses this false conditional to illustrate that even with a false premise, the logic of inference can be studied. Affirming 'man is rational' leads to 'he brays' only if the conditional is accepted; but that requires prior knowledge of the two sides, which would be circular. Denying 'he brays' leads to 'man is not rational' — this is valid reasoning per modus tollens.
The example highlights that the conditional must be known independently, otherwise circularity occurs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مركبا من المتصلة والمنفصلة القسم الأول لذلكات الشرطية متصلة وهي إما لزومية أو اتفاقية. أما اللزومية فاستثناء عين المقدم يوجب عين التالي، واستثناء نقيض الثاني يوجب نقيض المقدم على تفصيل سنذكره. وأما الاستثناء نقيض المقدم واستثناء عين التالي فلا ينتجان لاحتمال كون اللازم أعم من الملزوم، ولا يلزم من وجود العام وجود الخاص بل من انتفاء الخاص انتفاء العام. ومن الناس من قال: اللازم لو كان مساويا حصلت الشكل الأربع وهو خطأ، لأن لزوم هذا لذلك غير كذا. فلا علام البتة من لزوم هذا لذلك بل من لزوم ذلك لهذا، وذلك في الحقيقة شرطية أخرى. ثم اعلم أن البين من هاتين النتيجتين استثناء عين المقدم لإنتاج عين التالي. فأما استثناء نقيض الثاني لإنتاج نقيض المقدم فهو بواسطة الضرب الأول، فإنه لما لزم من وجود الملزوم وجود اللازم كان عدم اللازم ملزوما لعدم الملزوم، فيكون الاستدراك بانتفاء الملازم على انتفاء الملزوم راجعا عند التحقيق إلى الاستدلال بوجود الملزوم على وجود اللازم. فإذا قلت: كلما كان الإنسان ناطقا فهو ناهق، فلو قلت: لكن الإنسان ناطق يلزم أنه ناهق، لأن العلم بصرف تلك الشرطة موقوف على العلم بوجود جانبيها، فلو استفيد العلم بواحد منهما منها لزم الدور. ولو قلت: لكن بما ليس ناهق يلزم فالإنسان ليس بناطق.
A compound of the conjunctive and disjunctive: the first part of that of conditional propositions is the conjunctive, and it is either implicational or accidental. As for the implicational, the exclusion of the identical antecedent necessitates the identical consequent, and the exclusion of the contradictory of the second necessitates the contradictory of the antecedent, with a detail we will mention. As for the exclusion of the contradictory of the antecedent and the exclusion of the identical consequent, they do not produce [the conclusion] because of the possibility that the necessary is more general than the conditioned; the existence of the general does not necessitate the existence of the specific, but from the non-existence of the specific [follows] the non-existence of the general. And among people there is one who said: if the necessary is equivalent, then four forms result, and that is wrong, because the implication of this for that is not such. So there is no sign at all from the implication of this for that, but rather from the implication of that for this, and that in truth is another conditional. Then know that the clear from these two results is the exclusion of the identical antecedent for producing the identical consequent. As for the exclusion of the contradictory of the consequent for producing the contradictory of the antecedent, it is via the first mood, because when the existence of the conditioned necessitates the existence of the necessary, the non-existence of the necessary is a necessary condition for the non-existence of the conditioned; thus the correction by the non-existence of the necessary upon the non-existence of the conditioned returns, upon investigation, to the proof by the existence of the conditioned upon the existence of the necessary. So if you say: Whenever man is rational, he brays; if you say: but man is rational, then it follows that he brays, because knowing that very conditional is dependent on knowing the existence of both its sides; if knowledge of one of them is derived from it, circularity would result. And if you say: but it is not that he brays, then it follows that man is not rational.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage discusses compound conditionals, specifically the conjunctive conditional (muttasila) which may be either implicational (lizumiyya) or accidental (ittifaqiyya). The author states the valid inference rules: affirming the antecedent yields the consequent, and denying the consequent yields denial of the antecedent. Invalid moods are denying the antecedent and affirming the consequent, because the consequent may be more general than the antecedent. The author then addresses a claim that if antecedent and consequent are equivalent, four valid moods result, but refutes it because the implication is one-directional. He clarifies that the apparent inference of denying the consequent is actually a different conditional (the contrapositive). The text then explains that the exclusion of the contradictory of the consequent works via the first mood (contrapositive). Finally, an example is given: 'If man is rational, he brays.' If we affirm the antecedent, we conclude the consequent, but this is circular because the conditional itself is known only when both sides are known. Denying the consequent (he does not bray) leads to the negation of the antecedent (man is not rational). This illustrates the dependence of the conditional on prior knowledge.

Modus Ponens in Conjunctive Conditional Syllogisms
Author presents the first valid mood: affirming the antecedent yields the consequent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a valid modus ponens inference from a necessary conjunctive conditional and verify its soundness.

Core Idea
Affirming the antecedent yields the consequent in a necessary conditional.

Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, in his Mulakhkhas, introduces the valid moods of conjunctive conditional syllogisms. The first and most direct valid mood is modus ponens: when we have a necessary conditional 'If A then B', affirming A directly allows us to conclude B. This inference is the foundation of many arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional premise
Find a statement of the form 'If A then B' where the relation is necessary (luzumiyya). The antecedent is A, the consequent is B.
2
Locate the second premise
The second premise must affirm the antecedent, e.g., 'A is true'. This is called istithna' 'ayn al-muqaddam.
3
Derive the conclusion
According to the author, the conclusion is the consequent B. This is the modus ponens form.
The author states: 'استثناء عين المقدم لانتاج عين الثانى' (excepting the affirmation of the antecedent produces the affirmation of the consequent).
4
Verify necessity
Ensure the conditional is necessary (luzumiyya) and not accidental (ittifaqiyya). Only necessary conditionals guarantee the inference.
Accidental conditionals lead to circularity or no inference.

Compound from conjunctive and disjunctive. The first division of conditional propositions: conjunctive, and it is either necessary or accidental. As for necessary: its form is to affirm the antecedent [and] bring the consequent, [or] deny the consequent necessitates denying the antecedent, with a detail we will mention. And invalidation: [if] the necessary is found without the necessary [consequent], or the denial of the antecedent with the existence of the consequent, and such is entering [invalid]. As for excepting the negation of the antecedent or excepting the affirmation of the consequent, [they are invalid] because of the possibility that the necessary [consequent] is more general than the antecedent, and it does not follow from the generality of the thing the specificity of the particular; rather, from the denial of the particular [does not follow] the denial of the general. And invalidation of the general. And among people there is one who said: if the necessary were equal, the four [moods] would be produced, and that is an error, because the necessity of this [consequent] is not [due to] the existence of that [antecedent]... And when the two [propositions] are accidental, there is no indication at all from the necessity of this for that, but from the necessity of that for this, and that is in reality another conditional. Then know that the benefit from these two [conjunctive conditional syllogisms] is excepting the affirmation of the antecedent to produce the affirmation of the consequent. As for excepting the negation of the consequent to produce the negation of the antecedent, it is by means of the first mood, for since [the existence] of that [antecedent] necessitates [the existence] of something, the denial of that [consequent] is a necessary condition for the denial of the antecedent, so the deduction by the denial of the necessary [consequent] of the denial of the antecedent reverts, upon investigation, to the deduction by the existence of the antecedent of the existence of the consequent. As for the accidental, it is from a [different] side. So if you say: 'Whenever man is rational, then the donkey is brays?' And if you say: 'But man is rational,' it would entail that the donkey brays, because knowledge of that conditional depends on knowledge of its two sides; if knowledge of one of them is obtained from the other, circularity occurs. And if you say: 'But [the donkey] is not brays,' then 'Man is not rational' [follows]. False... because this conditional is not [valid]... So the accidental is that which matches existence to existence of something else, and what has no existence from itself but its existence matches the existence of another. And because if from agreement in truth there followed agreement in falsehood, the fallacy of the consequent would be invalidated. So when 'Whenever man is rational, the donkey brays' is true, it would be true that if the donkey does not bray, then man is not rational, otherwise its contradictory would be true. But it may happen that when man is rational, the donkey brays, and that is true? For 'Whenever man is a speaker, the donkey brays' would necessitate that if the donkey is not brays, then man is not a speaker? — here there is a fallacy because the meaning may be: if the donkey is not brays at the time of the supposition, then man is not a speaker exists? And the attainment from the promised is that the necessary conditional clause must be universal [or] particular. If it is used in both ways, either both [antecedent and consequent] are universal or not; the first is from a [special] side because the meaning of 'always' is that it is true in all times and with all circumstances... So the inference occurs only when the conditional is universal, as we mentioned, and that proves our purpose: that the conditional must be universal for the conclusion.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage discusses the valid and invalid moods of conjunctive conditional syllogisms (qiyas istithna'i muttasil). The author first divides conjunctive conditionals into necessary (luzumiyya) and accidental (ittifaqiyya). For necessary conditionals, the valid moods are: (1) affirming the antecedent to produce the consequent (modus ponens), and (2) denying the consequent to produce the denial of the antecedent (modus tollens). The invalid moods are: denying the antecedent or affirming the consequent. The author explains that these invalid forms fail because the consequent may be more general than the antecedent; for example, 'If it is a man, then it is an animal' is true, but denying the antecedent ('it is not a man') does not allow denying the consequent (it may still be an animal). Similarly, affirming the consequent ('it is an animal') does not allow affirming the antecedent (it could be a horse). The author then addresses an objection: if the antecedent and consequent are equivalent (mutasawiyan), one might think all four moods become valid. He refutes this, noting that even with equivalence, the inference from denying the antecedent or affirming the consequent still does not hold directly; rather, the valid inference from denying the consequent to denying the antecedent actually reduces to modus ponens via contraposition. Next, he discusses accidental conditionals, where the relation is not necessary but merely coincidental. In such cases, the conditional cannot be known independently, and attempting to infer one side from the other leads to circularity (dawr). The author stresses that for a valid inference, the conditional must be universal (kulliyya), meaning the relation holds in all cases. If it is only particular, the inference does not produce certainty. The examples given are somewhat garbled but illustrate the distinction: e.g., 'Whenever man is rational, the donkey brays' is an accidental conditional because the truth of the antecedent does not necessitate the consequent; it is just a coincidence. The author emphasizes that only necessary and universal conditionals yield valid syllogistic conclusions.

Key Terms
شرطية متصلة
لزومية
استثناء عين المقدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The consequent is true.
D) The antecedent is false.

Key Takeaway
Modus ponens is the most basic valid inference in conditional logic: affirming the antecedent yields the consequent, provided the conditional is necessary.

Modus Tollens: Denying the Consequent in Conditional Syllogisms
Author presents the second valid mood: denying the consequent yields the denial of the antecedent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to use modus tollens to infer the denial of the antecedent from the denial of the consequent in a necessary conditional.

Core Idea
Denying the consequent yields the denial of the antecedent in a necessary conditional.

After establishing modus ponens, al-Razi presents the second valid mood: modus tollens. This form is derived from the first by contraposition: if A necessitates B, then not-B necessitates not-A. Al-Razi emphasizes that this inference reduces to modus ponens upon analysis, but it remains a distinct and common form in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional premise
Locate a necessary conditional 'If A then B'. Ensure it is luzumiyya (necessary).
2
Locate the second premise
The second premise must deny the consequent, i.e., 'B is false' or 'not-B'. This is istithna' naqid al-tali.
3
Derive the conclusion
According to the author, the conclusion is the denial of the antecedent: 'not-A'. This is modus tollens.
Al-Razi says: 'استثناء نقيض الثاني لانتاج نقيض المقدم' (excepting the negation of the consequent produces the negation of the antecedent).
4
Understand the reduction to modus ponens
Al-Razi explains that this inference is actually by means of the first mood (modus ponens) because 'the denial of the consequent is a necessary condition for the denial of the antecedent'. In effect, we treat the contrapositive 'If not-B then not-A' as a new conditional and then apply modus ponens with 'not-B'.
This shows the foundational role of modus ponens.

Compound from conjunctive and disjunctive. The first division of conditional propositions: conjunctive, and it is either necessary or accidental. As for necessary: its form is to affirm the antecedent [and] bring the consequent, [or] deny the consequent necessitates denying the antecedent, with a detail we will mention. And invalidation: [if] the necessary is found without the necessary [consequent], or the denial of the antecedent with the existence of the consequent, and such is entering [invalid]. As for excepting the negation of the antecedent or excepting the affirmation of the consequent, [they are invalid] because of the possibility that the necessary [consequent] is more general than the antecedent, and it does not follow from the generality of the thing the specificity of the particular; rather, from the denial of the particular [does not follow] the denial of the general. And invalidation of the general. And among people there is one who said: if the necessary were equal, the four [moods] would be produced, and that is an error, because the necessity of this [consequent] is not [due to] the existence of that [antecedent]... And when the two [propositions] are accidental, there is no indication at all from the necessity of this for that, but from the necessity of that for this, and that is in reality another conditional. Then know that the benefit from these two [conjunctive conditional syllogisms] is excepting the affirmation of the antecedent to produce the affirmation of the consequent. As for excepting the negation of the consequent to produce the negation of the antecedent, it is by means of the first mood, for since [the existence] of that [antecedent] necessitates [the existence] of something, the denial of that [consequent] is a necessary condition for the denial of the antecedent, so the deduction by the denial of the necessary [consequent] of the denial of the antecedent reverts, upon investigation, to the deduction by the existence of the antecedent of the existence of the consequent. As for the accidental, it is from a [different] side. So if you say: 'Whenever man is rational, then the donkey is brays?' And if you say: 'But man is rational,' it would entail that the donkey brays, because knowledge of that conditional depends on knowledge of its two sides; if knowledge of one of them is obtained from the other, circularity occurs. And if you say: 'But [the donkey] is not brays,' then 'Man is not rational' [follows]. False... because this conditional is not [valid]... So the accidental is that which matches existence to existence of something else, and what has no existence from itself but its existence matches the existence of another. And because if from agreement in truth there followed agreement in falsehood, the fallacy of the consequent would be invalidated. So when 'Whenever man is rational, the donkey brays' is true, it would be true that if the donkey does not bray, then man is not rational, otherwise its contradictory would be true. But it may happen that when man is rational, the donkey brays, and that is true? For 'Whenever man is a speaker, the donkey brays' would necessitate that if the donkey is not brays, then man is not a speaker? — here there is a fallacy because the meaning may be: if the donkey is not brays at the time of the supposition, then man is not a speaker exists? And the attainment from the promised is that the necessary conditional clause must be universal [or] particular. If it is used in both ways, either both [antecedent and consequent] are universal or not; the first is from a [special] side because the meaning of 'always' is that it is true in all times and with all circumstances... So the inference occurs only when the conditional is universal, as we mentioned, and that proves our purpose: that the conditional must be universal for the conclusion.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage discusses the valid and invalid moods of conjunctive conditional syllogisms (qiyas istithna'i muttasil). The author first divides conjunctive conditionals into necessary (luzumiyya) and accidental (ittifaqiyya). For necessary conditionals, the valid moods are: (1) affirming the antecedent to produce the consequent (modus ponens), and (2) denying the consequent to produce the denial of the antecedent (modus tollens). The invalid moods are: denying the antecedent or affirming the consequent. The author explains that these invalid forms fail because the consequent may be more general than the antecedent; for example, 'If it is a man, then it is an animal' is true, but denying the antecedent ('it is not a man') does not allow denying the consequent (it may still be an animal). Similarly, affirming the consequent ('it is an animal') does not allow affirming the antecedent (it could be a horse). The author then addresses an objection: if the antecedent and consequent are equivalent (mutasawiyan), one might think all four moods become valid. He refutes this, noting that even with equivalence, the inference from denying the antecedent or affirming the consequent still does not hold directly; rather, the valid inference from denying the consequent to denying the antecedent actually reduces to modus ponens via contraposition. Next, he discusses accidental conditionals, where the relation is not necessary but merely coincidental. In such cases, the conditional cannot be known independently, and attempting to infer one side from the other leads to circularity (dawr). The author stresses that for a valid inference, the conditional must be universal (kulliyya), meaning the relation holds in all cases. If it is only particular, the inference does not produce certainty. The examples given are somewhat garbled but illustrate the distinction: e.g., 'Whenever man is rational, the donkey brays' is an accidental conditional because the truth of the antecedent does not necessitate the consequent; it is just a coincidence. The author emphasizes that only necessary and universal conditionals yield valid syllogistic conclusions.

Key Terms
شرطية متصلة
لزومية
استثناء نقيض التالي

A) The antecedent is true.
B) The antecedent is false.
C) The consequent is true.
D) The conditional is false.

Key Takeaway
Modus tollens is a valid inference from the denial of the consequent to the denial of the antecedent, reducible to modus ponens via contraposition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Identify a modus tollens argument in a fiqh ruling: e.g., 'If this transaction involves riba, then it is invalid. This transaction is valid. Therefore, it does not involve riba.' Explain why the inference is valid only if the conditional is necessary.

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Invalid Moods and the Condition of Universality in Conditional Syllogisms
Author explains the invalidity of denying antecedent/affirming consequent and requires universality for necessary conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why denying the antecedent and affirming the consequent are invalid, and explain why necessary conditionals must be universal for valid inference.

Core Idea
Denying the antecedent and affirming the consequent fail because the consequent may be broader; only universal conditionals guarantee validity.

Al-Razi not only presents valid moods but also warns against common invalid ones: denying the antecedent and affirming the consequent. He explains their failure by noting that the consequent may be more general than the antecedent. Additionally, he emphasizes that for a necessary conditional to yield a certain conclusion, it must be universal (kulliyya), i.e., the implication holds in all cases. This lesson examines these critical constraints.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two invalid moods
The author lists two invalid forms: (1) denying the antecedent (استثناء نقيض المقدم) to infer the denial of the consequent, and (2) affirming the consequent (استثناء عين التالي) to infer the affirmation of the antecedent.
2
Understand why they fail: the generality problem
Al-Razi explains that the consequent may be more general than the antecedent (اللازم أع من الملزوم). For example, 'If it is a man, it is an animal' – the consequent 'animal' is broader. Denying the antecedent ('not a man') does not imply 'not an animal' (it could be a horse). Similarly, affirming the consequent ('animal') does not imply 'man' (it could be a dog).
The author says: 'لاحتمال كون اللازم اع من الملاوي' (because of the possibility that the necessary is more general than the antecedent).
3
Recognize the universality requirement
The author states that for a valid inference, the necessary conditional must be universal (كلية), meaning the implication holds in all times and circumstances. If it is only particular (جزئية), the inference does not produce certainty.
Al-Razi says: 'الشرطة اللزومية العالف تكون معلمة لو محصورةً' (the necessary conditional clause must be universal or particular). He then explains that only universal ones yield conclusions.
4
Test with an example
Consider the accidental conditional: 'Whenever man is rational, the donkey brays.' This is not necessary, and the inference from affirming the antecedent ('man is rational') to 'donkey brays' is invalid because the conditional is false. Even if it were true accidentally, no logical connection exists.
Al-Razi uses this example to illustrate the lack of inference in accidental conditionals.

Commentary
This passage discusses the valid and invalid moods of conjunctive conditional syllogisms (qiyas istithna'i muttasil). The author first divides conjunctive conditionals into necessary (luzumiyya) and accidental (ittifaqiyya). For necessary conditionals, the valid moods are: (1) affirming the antecedent to produce the consequent (modus ponens), and (2) denying the consequent to produce the denial of the antecedent (modus tollens). The invalid moods are: denying the antecedent or affirming the consequent. The author explains that these invalid forms fail because the consequent may be more general than the antecedent; for example, 'If it is a man, then it is an animal' is true, but denying the antecedent ('it is not a man') does not allow denying the consequent (it may still be an animal). Similarly, affirming the consequent ('it is an animal') does not allow affirming the antecedent (it could be a horse). The author then addresses an objection: if the antecedent and consequent are equivalent (mutasawiyan), one might think all four moods become valid. He refutes this, noting that even with equivalence, the inference from denying the antecedent or affirming the consequent still does not hold directly; rather, the valid inference from denying the consequent to denying the antecedent actually reduces to modus ponens via contraposition. Next, he discusses accidental conditionals, where the relation is not necessary but merely coincidental. In such cases, the conditional cannot be known independently, and attempting to infer one side from the other leads to circularity (dawr). The author stresses that for a valid inference, the conditional must be universal (kulliyya), meaning the relation holds in all cases. If it is only particular, the inference does not produce certainty. The examples given are somewhat garbled but illustrate the distinction: e.g., 'Whenever man is rational, the donkey brays' is an accidental conditional because the truth of the antecedent does not necessitate the consequent; it is just a coincidence. The author emphasizes that only necessary and universal conditionals yield valid syllogistic conclusions.

Key Takeaway

Universal Conditional with Non-Universal Parts
Author examines the problem when a universal conditional has non-universal parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a universal conditional whose parts are not universal and explain why modus tollens is invalid in such cases.

Core Idea
A universal conditional with a particular subject yields only modus ponens, not modus tollens.

Al-Razi introduces a subtle problem: when a conditional proposition is universal (e.g., 'Whenever this is a human, it is laughing') but its antecedent and consequent refer to a particular individual, the naive assumption that both modus ponens and modus tollens are valid breaks down. This lesson unpacks the logical mechanics behind this asymmetry.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the conditional form
Identify that the conditional is universal in quantification (e.g., 'كلما') but its parts are not universal because the subject is a specific individual (e.g., 'هذا' meaning 'this').
2
Extract antecedent and consequent
The antecedent is 'this is a human' and the consequent is 'it is actually laughing'. Note that both refer to a particular entity, not a universal class.
3
Test modus ponens
If we affirm the antecedent (say, 'this is a human'), the universal conditional guarantees the consequent ('it is laughing') because the conditional covers all times when this individual is a human.
Modus ponens is valid.
4
Test modus tollens
If we deny the consequent ('this is not laughing'), does it follow that it is not a human? Al-Razi says no, because the conditional only applies when the individual is a human; the negation of the consequent does not imply the negation of the antecedent in an absolute sense—there may be humans who are not laughing at a given moment.
Modus tollens is invalid.
5
Generalize the principle
When the parts of a universal conditional are not universal (i.e., they involve a particular instance), modus tollens fails because the conditional's scope is limited to the times when the antecedent is true.
This guards against overreaching in conditional arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا كانت الشرطية كلية، فإن لم يكن جزؤها كليين، عاد الإشكال. وإن كان فلا خلوص، ما لم يكن الدوام معتبراً لفظاً مجرداً أو لا يكون. فالأول كقولنا: كلما كان هذا إنساناً فهو ضاحك بالفعل. وهاهنا تحصل النتيجتان. وأما الثاني: فإن استثنينا المقدم يتعين الثاني واستثناء الثاني لا ينتج لانتفاء المقدم. وذلك أنه إذا جعلت موضوع المطلقة العامة المحلية مقيداً بما حُوِّل تالياً كما إذا قلنا: كلما كان هذا إنساناً فهو ضاحك بالفعل، فلو قلنا: لكنه إنسان، لزم أنه ضاحك بالفعل بالإطلاق العام. فلو قلنا: لكنه ليس بضاحك بالفعل بالإطلاق العام، لم يلزم أنه ليس بإنسان بالإطلاق العام، فإن بعض من ليس بضاحك بالفعل بالضرورة إنسان. فلا يقال: قولكم "كلما كان هذا إنساناً فهو ضاحك بالفعل" قضية كاذبة.
If the conditional is universal, then if its two parts are not both universal, the problem returns. And if they are both universal, there is no escape unless perpetuity is considered as a mere expression or not. The first is like our saying: Whenever this is a human, it is actually laughing. And here both conclusions are obtained. As for the second: if we affirm the antecedent, the consequent is determined, and affirming the consequent does not produce a conclusion because the antecedent is absent. That is because when you make the subject of the absolute general proposition restricted by what has been made the consequent, as when we say: Whenever this is a human, it is actually laughing. So if we say: but it is a human, it follows that it is actually laughing in an absolute sense. So if we say: but it is not actually laughing in an absolute sense, it does not follow that it is not human in an absolute sense, for some of what is not actually laughing is necessarily human. So it cannot be said: Your statement "Whenever this is a human, it is actually laughing" is a false proposition.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage addresses a subtle issue in conditional syllogisms (qiyas istithna'i). The author has previously discussed the case where the conditional is composed of two universal propositions. Now he turns to a different scenario: the conditional itself is universal (e.g., 'Whenever this is a human, it is actually laughing'), but its antecedent and consequent are not universal terms—they refer to a particular individual ('this'). In such a case, the problem of the conclusion not following reappears. The author explains that if the conditional is universal but its parts are not universal, we must examine whether the universality is taken as a mere verbal form (i.e., the quantification is merely linguistic) or not. In the first case (e.g., 'Whenever this is a human, it is actually laughing'), if we affirm the antecedent ('but it is a human'), we can conclude the consequent ('it is actually laughing') because the conditional covers all times that 'this' is a human. However, if we deny the consequent ('but it is not actually laughing'), we cannot conclude the negation of the antecedent ('it is not human'), because the universal conditional only applies when 'this' is human; the negation of the consequent does not necessarily mean the antecedent is false in all absolute senses—there are humans who are not laughing at a given moment. Thus, the conclusion fails. This guards against the mistake of thinking any universal conditional yields a valid modus tollens regardless of the specificity of its parts.

Key Terms
شرطية كلية
استثناء المقدم
إطلاق عام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, because the conditional is universal.
B) No, because Zayd might be dead or alive but not breathing temporarily.
C) Yes, because breathing is a necessary condition for life.
D) No, because the antecedent and consequent refer to a particular individual, so the universal quantification may not hold in all cases.

Key Takeaway
A universal conditional with non-universal parts only validates modus ponens; modus tollens is invalid because the conditional's scope is limited to the antecedent's truth.

Verbal vs Genuine Universality in Conditionals
Author examines the problem when a universal conditional has non-universal parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between verbal universality (mere linguistic form) and genuine logical universality in conditional propositions, and apply this distinction to evaluate arguments.

Core Idea
A conditional may be universal in wording but restricted in logical force when its parts are not universal.

Al-Razi introduces the idea that the universality of a conditional may be 'mere expression' (لفظ مجرد) rather than true logical universality. This lesson explores how to tell the difference and why it matters for valid inference.

Guided Path
1
Identify verbal universality
In al-Razi's example 'كلما كان هذا إنساناً فهو ضاحك بالفعل', the word 'كلما' (whenever) gives a universal form, but the subject 'هذا' (this) is particular. The universality is merely verbal because it does not cover all instances of a class.
Verbal universality uses universal quantifiers with particular terms.
2
Compare with genuine universality
If the conditional had universal parts, e.g., 'كلما كان شيء إنساناً فهو حيوان' (Whenever something is a human, it is an animal), the subject is a universal term, so the universality is genuine.
Genuine universality requires universal terms in both antecedent and consequent.
3
Check the logical consequence
With verbal universality, modus ponens works within the conditional's scope, but modus tollens fails because the conditional does not cover cases where the antecedent is false. With genuine universality, both modus ponens and modus tollens are valid.
4
Apply to Islamic reasoning
In kalam or fiqh, when a scholar says 'Whenever a person is a mukallaf, they are responsible for their actions', if 'person' is a universal term, both inferences hold. If 'person' refers to a specific individual, modus tollens may not hold.

Problem of Non-Universal Parts in Conditional Syllogisms
Author discusses the difficulty when conditional parts are not universal and the need for continuity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a conditional syllogism fails due to non-universal parts and apply the condition of continuity (dawam) to ensure validity.

Core Idea
A conditional syllogism's conclusion may be invalid if the parts are not universal and continuity is absent.

In conditional syllogisms (qiyas istithna'i), the validity of the conclusion depends not only on the truth of the conditional but also on the universality of its parts. This passage from al-Mulakhkhas explains a critical problem: if the antecedent and consequent are particular, the exception may refer to a different individual than the one that made the conditional true. The solution lies in ensuring continuity (dawam) either explicitly in wording or implicitly in meaning. Understanding this nuance is essential for correctly applying exceptive syllogisms in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional and its parts
Find the conditional proposition and separate the antecedent (muqaddam) and consequent (tali). In the example, the conditional is 'If A is B, then C is D.' The parts are 'A is B' and 'C is D.'
The parts are themselves propositions.
2
Check whether each part is universal (kulli) or particular (juz'i)
Determine if the subject terms (A and C) are universal or particular. If they are not universal, each part can be true of just one individual. For instance, 'A is B' could be true because one specific A is B.
Universality here refers to the scope of the subject term.
3
Understand the problem with non-universal parts
If both parts are particular, the conditional may be true for one particular individual but not for all. When we make an exception (istithna') by affirming the antecedent, we may be referring to a different individual than the one that satisfied the conditional. Thus, the consequent may not hold for the new individual, and the conclusion does not follow.
The key insight: the exception is not guaranteed to be the same individual.
4
Apply the condition of continuity (dawam)
To avoid the problem, the predicate must hold for all individuals of the subject across all relevant contexts. This can be expressed linguistically (e.g., using 'whenever' — kullama) or understood in meaning. Only then does the conclusion follow necessarily.
Continuity is essentially the universality of the connection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا كانت الشرطية مركبة من جزئين غير كليين فقد ذكرناه فيما قبل متوجه هنا مع إشكال آخر: إن الجزئين إذا لم يكونا كليين كفى في صدق كل واحد منهما صدقه على شخص واحد. مثلا قولنا: "إن كان أ ب" فقولنا: "أ ب" كفى في صدقها أن يصادف شخص ما من أشخاص الألف بالباء. وكذلك القول الثاني. وإذا كان كذلك كفى في صدق القضية الشرطية المركبة من قضيتين غير كليتين أن يتصف شخص واحد من أشخاص موضوع الثاني بالمحمول عند اتصاف شخص واحد من أشخاص موضوع المقدم به. ثم إذا استثنينا وقلنا: فمن الجائز أن يكون الألف الذي صار هذا الاستثناء غير الذي لأجله صدقت تلك الشرطية وأن لا يكون الحكم في جميع الألفات واحدا. وإذا كان كذلك لم تلزم النتيجة. وإن كانت الشرطية كلية، فإن لم يكن جزؤها كليين عاد الإشكال. وإن كان فلا حاجة؟ ما لم يكن الدوام معتبرا لفظا أو معنى. فالأول كقولنا: "كلما كان ...".
If the conditional is composed of two non-universal parts, we have mentioned it before, but here another problem arises: If the two parts are not universal, it suffices for the truth of each of them that it is true of a single individual. For example, our statement 'If A is B' and our statement 'A is B,' it suffices for its truth that it encounters some individual of A that is B. Likewise the second statement. And if that is the case, it suffices for the truth of the conditional proposition composed of two non-universal propositions that one individual of the subject of the second is described by the predicate when one individual of the subject of the first is described by it. Then if we make an exception and say: it is possible that the A that is the subject of this exception is different from the one for which that conditional was true, and that the ruling is not one for all A's. And if that is so, the conclusion does not follow. And if the conditional is universal, then if its parts are not universal, the problem returns. And if it is, then there is no need? as long as continuity is considered in wording or meaning. The first is like our statement 'Whenever ...'.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of a problem in conditional syllogisms (qiyas istithna'i). The author explains that when a conditional proposition is composed of two non-universal parts (i.e., particular propositions), a difficulty arises because the truth of each part can be satisfied by different individuals. For example, the conditional 'If A is B, then C is D' might be true because one particular A is B and that same A's C is D. But when we make an exception (istithna) by affirming the antecedent, we might pick a different A that is B but whose C is not D, so the conclusion does not follow necessarily. This is the core issue: the exception may refer to a different individual than the one that made the conditional true. The author then considers the case where the conditional is universal (kulliyya), but if the parts themselves are not universal, the same problem recurs. The solution is that the continuity (dawam) of the predicate for all individuals must be considered, either in wording (like using 'whenever') or in meaning. This is an important refinement for the validity of exceptive syllogisms.

Key Terms
شَرْطِيَّة (shartiyya): A conditional proposition consisting of two parts (antecedent and consequent) connected by a conditional relation. [Note: No significant shift; stable across the tradition.]
كُلِّي (kulli): Universal; a term that applies to all individuals of its subject matter. [Note: Stable; note that in conditional contexts, 'kulli' refers to the universality of the parts, not the whole conditional.]
جُزْء (juz'): Part, here referring to the antecedent or consequent of a conditional proposition. [Note: Stable.]
اسْتِثْنَاء (istithna'): Exception; affirming or denying one part of a conditional in an exceptive syllogism. [Note: Stable.]
دَوَام (dawam): Continuity; the idea that the predicate holds for all individuals of the subject across all times or circumstances. [Note: In later texts, 'dawam' may be replaced by 'istighraq' (comprehensiveness).]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The conclusion is always valid because the conditional is true.
B) The exception might refer to a different A that is B but whose C is not D.
C) The consequent cannot be denied.
D) The conditional becomes false.

Key Takeaway
The validity of an exceptive syllogism requires that the conditional's parts be universal or that continuity (dawam) is ensured; otherwise, the conclusion may not follow.

Rules for Muttasil (Conjunctive) Conditional Syllogisms
Rules for muttasil conditional syllogisms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the valid conclusions of a conjunctive conditional syllogism (muttasil) by applying the rules of istithna' al-'ayn and istithna' al-naqid.

Core Idea
In a real conjunctive conditional, affirming one part yields the negation of the other, and negating one yields the affirmation of the other.

The author now explains how to derive results from a conjunctive conditional (muttasil) syllogism when we except one part. Understanding these rules is essential for constructing valid arguments in fields like kalam and fiqh where conditional reasoning is common. The rules here describe a special type of conditional where the parts are logically related as contradictories.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of conditional
Confirm that the proposition is a real conjunctive conditional (muttasila haqiqiyya) with exactly two parts: an antecedent and a consequent. The text defines this as having a strict relation preventing coexistence and joint absence.
If it is not real or has more than two parts, the rules change and are covered separately.
2
Determine the exceptive operation
Check whether you are affirming a part (istithna' al-'ayn) or denying a part (istithna' al-naqid).
3
Apply the rule for affirming a part
If you affirm either part, the conclusion is the contradictory of the other part. For example, if you affirm the antecedent, conclude the negation of the consequent.
This is because the two parts cannot both be true simultaneously.
4
Apply the rule for denying a part
If you deny either part (affirm its negation), the conclusion is the affirmation of the other part. For example, if you deny the antecedent, conclude the consequent.
This is because the two parts cannot both be false simultaneously.
5
Generalize to multiple parts
If the conditional has more than two parts, affirming one part yields the contradictory of all the rest, and denying one yields the affirmation of all the rest.
This is a mechanical extension of the two-part rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفن الثاني: أن تكون الشرطية متصلة. فإن كانت حقيقية وذات جزئين، كان استثناء عين أيهما كان منتجا لنقيض الآخر، واستثناء نقيض أيهما كان منتجا للآخر، لاستحالة اجتماع النقيضين وارتفاعهما معا. وإن كانت أكثر من ذات جزئين، كان استثناء عين أيهما كان منتجا لنقيض البواقي، واستثناء نقيض أيهما كان منتجا لها. وإن كانت غير حقيقية، فإن كانت مانعة من الجمع، كان استثناء عين أيهما كان منتجا لنقيض الآخر لاستحالة اجتماع تلك الأجزاء، واستثناء نقيض أيهما كان لا ينتج شيئا لصحة ارتفاع كلها. وإن كانت مانعة من الخلو، كان استثناء نقيض أيهما كان منتجا لحصول الآخر لاستحالة ارتفاعها معا، واستثناء عين أيهما كان لا ينتج نقيض الآخر لصحة اجتماعهما. فاعلم أن هذه الاستثنائيات المنفصلة إنما تنتج لكونها في قوة الاستثنائيات المتصلة، وهي إن رفع هذا البعد وضع بازائه دفع الآخر أو وضعه. وبالله التوفيق. ثم اللواحق في الإشارة إلى بعض توابع القياس، وذلك تسعة: ...
The second type: that the conditional is conjunctive (muttasil). If it is real (haqiqi) and has two parts, then excepting either one of them produces the contradictory of the other, and excepting the contradictory of either one produces the other, due to the impossibility of the two contradictories coexisting and both being absent together. And if it has more than two parts, then excepting either one of them produces the contradictory of the rest, and excepting the contradictory of either one produces them. And if it is not real, then if it is of the type preventing conjunction (mani'at al-jam'), excepting either one of them produces the contradictory of the other due to the impossibility of those parts coexisting, and excepting the contradictory of either one produces nothing due to the possibility of all being absent. And if it is of the type preventing void (mani'at al-khuluw), excepting the contradictory of either one produces the occurrence of the other due to the impossibility of their being absent together, and excepting either one of them does not produce the contradictory of the other due to the possibility of their coexistence. Know that these disjunctive exceptive syllogisms produce results only because they are equivalent to conjunctive exceptive syllogisms, and they are: if you remove this distance... (i.e., if you affirm one, you deny the other, or if you negate one, you affirm the other). And with God is the success. Then the appendices indicating some corollaries of the syllogism, and that is nine: ...
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage classifies the rules for exceptive syllogisms (qiyas istithna'i) based on the type of conditional proposition. The author first discusses the 'muttasil' (conjunctive/hypothetical) conditional. If it is 'haqiqi' (real) and has exactly two parts, then affirming either part (istithna al-'ayn) yields the contradictory of the other, and denying either part (istithna al-naqid) yields the affirmation of the other. This is because in a real conditional, the antecedent and consequent are related such that they cannot both be true and both false simultaneously. If the conditional has more than two parts, the same rule applies mechanically: affirming one part yields the contradictory of all the rest, and denying one yields the affirmation of the rest. Next, the author turns to 'ghayr haqiqi' (non-real) conditionals, specifically disjunctive (munfasil). There are three types: man'at al-jam' (preventing conjunction only), man'at al-khuluw (preventing void only), and the complete one that does both. The text here only mentions two subtypes: man'at al-jam' and man'at al-khuluw (the complete one is implied by the first case of haqiqi? Actually haqiqi munfasil usually prevents both). For man'at al-jam', affirming one part yields the contradictory of the other (since they cannot coexist), but denying one yields nothing (since they could all be absent). For man'at al-khuluw, denying one yields the affirmation of the other (since they cannot all be absent), but affirming one yields nothing (since they could coexist). The author concludes that all disjunctive exceptive syllogisms are valid only because they reduce to conjunctive (muttasil) conditionals. For example, 'Either A is B or C is D' (preventing both) is equivalent to 'If not A is B then C is D' and 'If not C is D then A is B'. Finally, the author mentions that there are nine corollaries of the syllogism to be discussed later.

Key Terms
مُتَّصِلَة
حَقِيقِيَّة
اسْتِثْنَاء عَيْن
اسْتِثْنَاء نَقِيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Nothing follows.
D) Either part is possible.

Key Takeaway
In a real muttasil conditional with two parts, the rule is symmetric: affirming one gives the negation of the other, and negating one gives the affirmation of the other.

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Rules for Munfasil (Disjunctive) Conditional Syllogisms
Rules for munfasil conditional syllogisms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two types of disjunctive conditionals (mani'at al-jam' and mani'at al-khuluw) and determine which inferences are valid in each.

Core Idea
In a disjunctive syllogism, validity depends on the type: affirming yields negation only in 'preventing conjunction', and negating yields affirmation only in 'preventing void'.

After discussing conjunctive conditionals, the author turns to disjunctive (munfasil) conditionals. These come in three types, but the text focuses on two: those that prevent conjunction (mani'at al-jam') and those that prevent void (mani'at al-khuluw). The rules for exceptive syllogisms differ depending on the type, and understanding this is crucial for correctly evaluating arguments in Islamic jurisprudence and theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of disjunctive
Determine whether the disjunctive conditional is mani'at al-jam' (at most one true), mani'at al-khuluw (at least one true), or both (exclusive disjunction). The text treats the first two here.
Mani'at al-jam' wal-khuluw (the complete form) behaves like the haqiqi muttasil, allowing both inferences.
2
Apply rule for mani'at al-jam' (preventing conjunction)
If you affirm one part (istithna' al-'ayn), you can conclude the contradictory of the other part (since they cannot coexist). But if you deny one part (istithna' al-naqid), you cannot conclude anything (since they could all be false).
Example: 'Either the prayer is valid or it is invalid' (mani'at al-jam'). Affirm validity -> conclude it is not invalid. Deny validity -> cannot conclude it is invalid (it might be something else).
3
Apply rule for mani'at al-khuluw (preventing void)
If you deny one part (istithna' al-naqid), you can conclude the other part (since they cannot all be false). But if you affirm one part (istithna' al-'ayn), you cannot conclude anything (since they could both be true).
Example: 'Either the sun rises or it is daytime' (mani'at al-khuluw). Deny sunrise -> conclude it is daytime. Affirm sunrise -> cannot conclude it is not daytime (both could be true).
4
Understand the reduction to muttasil
The author notes that all disjunctive syllogisms are valid because they reduce to conjunctive (muttasil) conditionals. For example, 'Either A or B' (preventing both) is equivalent to 'If not A then B' and 'If not B then A'.
This reduction explains why the rules mirror those of the muttasil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفن الثاني: أن تكون الشرطية متصلة. فإن كانت حقيقية وذات جزئين، كان استثناء عين أيهما كان منتجا لنقيض الآخر، واستثناء نقيض أيهما كان منتجا للآخر، لاستحالة اجتماع النقيضين وارتفاعهما معا. وإن كانت أكثر من ذات جزئين، كان استثناء عين أيهما كان منتجا لنقيض البواقي، واستثناء نقيض أيهما كان منتجا لها. وإن كانت غير حقيقية، فإن كانت مانعة من الجمع، كان استثناء عين أيهما كان منتجا لنقيض الآخر لاستحالة اجتماع تلك الأجزاء، واستثناء نقيض أيهما كان لا ينتج شيئا لصحة ارتفاع كلها. وإن كانت مانعة من الخلو، كان استثناء نقيض أيهما كان منتجا لحصول الآخر لاستحالة ارتفاعها معا، واستثناء عين أيهما كان لا ينتج نقيض الآخر لصحة اجتماعهما. فاعلم أن هذه الاستثنائيات المنفصلة إنما تنتج لكونها في قوة الاستثنائيات المتصلة، وهي إن رفع هذا البعد وضع بازائه دفع الآخر أو وضعه. وبالله التوفيق. ثم اللواحق في الإشارة إلى بعض توابع القياس، وذلك تسعة: ...
The second type: that the conditional is conjunctive (muttasil). If it is real (haqiqi) and has two parts, then excepting either one of them produces the contradictory of the other, and excepting the contradictory of either one produces the other, due to the impossibility of the two contradictories coexisting and both being absent together. And if it has more than two parts, then excepting either one of them produces the contradictory of the rest, and excepting the contradictory of either one produces them. And if it is not real, then if it is of the type preventing conjunction (mani'at al-jam'), excepting either one of them produces the contradictory of the other due to the impossibility of those parts coexisting, and excepting the contradictory of either one produces nothing due to the possibility of all being absent. And if it is of the type preventing void (mani'at al-khuluw), excepting the contradictory of either one produces the occurrence of the other due to the impossibility of their being absent together, and excepting either one of them does not produce the contradictory of the other due to the possibility of their coexistence. Know that these disjunctive exceptive syllogisms produce results only because they are equivalent to conjunctive exceptive syllogisms, and they are: if you remove this distance... (i.e., if you affirm one, you deny the other, or if you negate one, you affirm the other). And with God is the success. Then the appendices indicating some corollaries of the syllogism, and that is nine: ...
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage classifies the rules for exceptive syllogisms (qiyas istithna'i) based on the type of conditional proposition. The author first discusses the 'muttasil' (conjunctive/hypothetical) conditional. If it is 'haqiqi' (real) and has exactly two parts, then affirming either part (istithna al-'ayn) yields the contradictory of the other, and denying either part (istithna al-naqid) yields the affirmation of the other. This is because in a real conditional, the antecedent and consequent are related such that they cannot both be true and both false simultaneously. If the conditional has more than two parts, the same rule applies mechanically: affirming one part yields the contradictory of all the rest, and denying one yields the affirmation of the rest. Next, the author turns to 'ghayr haqiqi' (non-real) conditionals, specifically disjunctive (munfasil). There are three types: man'at al-jam' (preventing conjunction only), man'at al-khuluw (preventing void only), and the complete one that does both. The text here only mentions two subtypes: man'at al-jam' and man'at al-khuluw (the complete one is implied by the first case of haqiqi? Actually haqiqi munfasil usually prevents both). For man'at al-jam', affirming one part yields the contradictory of the other (since they cannot coexist), but denying one yields nothing (since they could all be absent). For man'at al-khuluw, denying one yields the affirmation of the other (since they cannot all be absent), but affirming one yields nothing (since they could coexist). The author concludes that all disjunctive exceptive syllogisms are valid only because they reduce to conjunctive (muttasil) conditionals. For example, 'Either A is B or C is D' (preventing both) is equivalent to 'If not A is B then C is D' and 'If not C is D then A is B'. Finally, the author mentions that there are nine corollaries of the syllogism to be discussed later.

The Syllogism
beginner
5 min
Direct syllogism requires exactly two premises
Author states that a direct syllogism has exactly two premises; more premises form compound syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether an argument is a single direct syllogism or a compound syllogism based on the number of premises.

Core Idea
A single direct syllogism never has more than two premises.

This lesson establishes the fundamental unit of valid reasoning: a direct syllogism. As you'll see, the author sets a strict boundary: any argument that claims to be a single syllogism must consist of exactly two premises. More premises mean more syllogisms. This distinction will prevent confusion when you encounter complex arguments in Islamic legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
Read the Arabic: 'ثم إن القياس المنتج بالذات للنتيجة الواحدة لا يزيد على مقدمتين ولا ينقص عنها'. Notice the strong negation: the direct syllogism does not exceed two premises nor fall short. This is a definitional boundary.
The phrase للنتيجة الواحدة specifies that this applies to a single conclusion.
2
Contrast with many premises
The author says that many premises are 'في الحقيقة مقدمات المقدمات' (premises of premises). This means extra premises belong to separate syllogisms, not the same one. They form a chain until the final conclusion.
This chain is called qiyas murakkab (compound syllogism).
3
Apply to a real argument
Take an Islamic legal example: 'Every intoxicant is haram. Nabidh is an intoxicant. Therefore nabidh is haram.' This has exactly two premises, so it is a direct syllogism. If you needed to prove that nabidh is an intoxicant via another syllogism (e.g., 'Every drink that ferments is an intoxicant. Nabidh ferments. Therefore nabidh is an intoxicant'), you would have a compound syllogism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن القياس المنتج بالذات للنتيجة الواحدة لا يزيد على مقدمتين ولا ينقص عنها. وأما المقدمات الكثيرة فهي بالحقيقة مقدمات المقدمات أن نصل إلى المطلوب ويسمى ذلك قياساً مركباً.
Then, the syllogism that produces by itself a single conclusion does not exceed two premises nor fall short of them. As for many premises, they are in reality premises of premises until we reach the sought conclusion, and that is called a compound syllogism.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage defines the fundamental unit of valid reasoning: a direct categorical syllogism (qiyas iqtirani) that yields one conclusion must consist of exactly two premises (muqaddimatan). The author emphasizes that if we see an argument with more than two premises, those extra premises are not part of the direct syllogism but are separate syllogisms chained together. Such chains are called 'compound syllogisms' (qiyas murakkab). This distinction prevents confusion between a single syllogism and a series of syllogisms. For example, in Islamic legal reasoning, proving that nabidh is forbidden might require a chain: first, that every intoxicant is forbidden (premise 1), then that nabidh is an intoxicant (premise 2) — that's a direct syllogism. If we then need to prove that nabidh is intoxicating via another syllogism, that becomes a compound.

Key Terms
القياس المنتج بالذات (al-qiyās al-muntij bi-dh-dhāt): A syllogism that directly yields its conclusion from its own premises without additional reasoning. [Note: No significant shift.]
مقدمتان (muqaddimatān): The two premises forming a single syllogism, which are sufficient and necessary for the conclusion. [Note: Stable term.]
قياس مركب (qiyās murakkab): A sequence of syllogisms where the conclusion of one becomes a premise for the next, until the ultimate desired conclusion. [Note: Al-Razi's usage is consistent with earlier logicians.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A direct syllogism for a single conclusion always consists of exactly two premises; more premises indicate a compound syllogism.

Connected and Separated Compound Syllogisms
Author classifies compound syllogisms into connected and separated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between mawsula and mafsula compound syllogisms and identify which type a given syllogistic chain belongs to.

Core Idea
Explicit intermediate conclusions make a compound syllogism connected; omission makes it separated.

Compound syllogisms are extended arguments with multiple steps. Jurists often use chains of reasoning to derive rulings. The author of the Mulakkhas teaches us to classify these chains into two types: those that explicitly state each intermediate conclusion (mawsula) and those that skip them (mafsula). This classification helps in analyzing the logical structure of complex arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the compound syllogism
Find an argument with multiple syllogistic steps in an Islamic text, e.g., a chain of analogies in fiqh.
A single syllogism is not compound.
2
Look for explicit intermediate conclusions
Check if the arguer states the result of each step before moving to the next, e.g., 'Therefore, A is B.' in the middle of the chain.
In a mawsula, these statements are present.
3
Classify as mawsula or mafsula
If intermediate conclusions are stated, it is mawsula; if they are omitted, it is mafsula.
Both can be valid if the logical chain is sound.
4
Consider the context
In usul al-fiqh, mawsula is used for clarity and teaching; mafsula is used for brevity when the steps are obvious.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقياسات المركبة قد تكون موطولة وهي التي تُذكر فيها النتائج قارة لبان كونها مفتحة وقادة لجعلها مقدمة لما بعدها وقد تكون مفصولة وهي التي لا تذكر فيها النتائج البته. ثم هذه المقدمات لا تبتدأ من الأوليات فلها بداية. فان أمكن ايتها وهَا إلى نتائج لا يمكن جعلها مقدمات لغيرها كانت ذوات نهاية وإلا فلا.
And compound syllogisms are sometimes connected—those in which the intermediate conclusions are explicitly stated as premises for what follows—and sometimes separated—those in which the conclusions are not mentioned at all. Then these premises do not begin from first principles, so they have a beginning. If it is possible to reach from them conclusions that cannot be made premises for others, they have an end; otherwise not.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage defines two subtypes of compound syllogisms (qiyasat murakkaba). The first is 'connected' (mawsula), where intermediate conclusions are openly stated so that they serve as premises for the next step. The second is 'separated' (mafsula), where intermediate results are omitted, and only the final conclusion appears. The author further notes that every chain of reasoning must start somewhere—from known first principles (awwaliyat). If the chain can eventually reach a conclusion that cannot be used as a premise for further reasoning, the sequence has an end; otherwise, it continues indefinitely (infinite regress). This is crucial in Islamic theology (kalam) when proving the existence of God: the chain of causes must end in a necessary being.

Key Terms
موطولة
مفصولة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

D) Mawsula has two premises, mafsula has more

Key Takeaway
The key distinction in compound syllogisms is whether the intermediate conclusions are explicitly stated (mawsula) or omitted (mafsula); both can be valid as long as the logical chain is sound.

Beginnings and Ends of Reasoning Chains
Author discusses how compound syllogisms begin from first principles and may or may not have an end.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a chain of reasoning must begin from first principles and when it must end, and apply this to kalam arguments for God's existence.

Core Idea
Every valid argument chain starts with self-evident first principles and must eventually reach a conclusion that cannot be used as a premise, preventing infinite regress.

Not all chains of reasoning can go on forever. In classical Islamic logic and kalam, every proof must rest on certain foundational truths (awwaliyyat) and must ultimately terminate in a conclusion that is not itself a premise for further reasoning. This is directly relevant to the kalam cosmological argument: if every event requires a cause, the chain of causes must end in an uncaused cause—Allah. The passage teaches us to identify a beginning and an end in any compound syllogism.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first premises of the chain
Look for propositions that are taken as self-evident (awwaliyyat) in the argument, e.g., 'Every contingent thing has a cause.'
These are not proven but are axiomatic.
2
Trace the chain of reasoning
Follow how each conclusion becomes a premise for the next step, forming a sequence of syllogisms.
3
Determine if the chain has an end
Check if the final conclusion can itself be used as a premise in another argument. If not, the chain has an end.
In kalam, the conclusion 'God exists' cannot be a premise for a further cause.
4
Apply to a kalam proof
In the proof for God's existence, the chain ends with 'therefore, the world has a necessary cause' which cannot be a premise for a further cause, thus avoiding infinite regress.

Source Text

This passage defines two subtypes of compound syllogisms (qiyasat murakkaba). The first is 'connected' (mawsula), where intermediate conclusions are openly stated so that they serve as premises for the next step. The second is 'separated' (mafsula), where intermediate results are omitted, and only the final conclusion appears. The author further notes that every chain of reasoning must start somewhere—from known first principles (awwaliyat). If the chain can eventually reach a conclusion that cannot be used as a premise for further reasoning, the sequence has an end; otherwise, it continues indefinitely (infinite regress). Reduction to Impossibility (Khalf) as a Compound Syllogism
Author explains khalf as a compound of categorical and conditional syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two component syllogisms in a khalf argument and construct a valid compound syllogism to prove a theological proposition.

Core Idea
Reductio ad absurdum is a compound of categorical and conditional syllogisms working together.

In this lesson, we examine how Muslim logicians formalized reductio ad absurdum (khalf). The author explains that khalf is not a simple syllogism but a compound of two: a categorical syllogism and a conditional syllogism. Understanding this structure is essential for evaluating proofs in kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition to be proved
Determine the goal proposition (e.g., 'every B is A'). This is what we want to establish as true.
In Islamic theology, goals often involve attributes of God or creation.
2
Assume the contradictory
For the goal 'every B is A', assume its contradictory: 'some B is not A' or 'not every B is A'. This assumption is the starting point of reductio.
The contradictory must be the exact negation of the goal.
3
Form a categorical syllogism
Combine the assumed contradictory with a known true premise (e.g., 'every A is C') to derive a conclusion that is impossible or false. This is the categorical syllogism (iqtirani).
The known true premise must be accepted by all parties.
4
Derive impossibility
The categorical syllogism leads to a conclusion that contradicts a known truth (e.g., 'some C is not C' or a violation of a first principle). This impossibility shows the assumption cannot hold.
Impossibility can be a logical contradiction or a known fact.
5
Apply conditional syllogism
Form a conditional: 'If the assumption (contradictory) were true, then impossibility would follow.' Since the impossibility is false, use modus tollens to conclude the assumption is false, thus the goal is true. This is the conditional syllogism (istithna'i).
Modus tollens: If P then Q; not Q; therefore not P.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حرارة الخلف خاصلة راجع إلى الاشد ال بامتناع لأنه احد النقيضين على امتناعه. و به علان الحق في الطرف الآخر وهو من القياسات المركبة لأنه مركب من قياسين أحدهما الاقتراني والآخر الاستثنائي كقولك لو كذب قولنا ليس كل ج ب صدق نقيضه وهو كل ج ب مع مقدمة غير الذي حته وعكسه وعكس نقبضه وكذب نقبضه والثالثة الحربية إنكات لها عكس نقيض فهو يتبعها والأفكذب النقيض حاصل ع ا كل حالك.
The method of khalf (reduction) is a special [method] referring to one of the two contraries due to its impossibility. And by it the truth in the other side is entailed. It is among compound syllogisms because it is composed of two syllogisms: one categorical and the other conditional. For example, if our statement 'not every B is A' is false, then its contradictory 'every B is A' is true, along with a premise other than that, and its converse and its contradictory's converse, and the falsehood of its contradictory...
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage introduces 'khalf' or reductio ad absurdum as a form of compound syllogism. The author explains that it works by showing that the denial of the desired conclusion leads to an impossibility (imtina'), which forces the acceptance of the opposite truth. Structurally, khalf consists of two syllogisms: a categorical syllogism (al-iqtirani) and a conditional syllogism (al-istithna'i). The example given: to prove that 'every B is A', we assume its contradictory 'not every B is A' is false; this falsehood implies the truth of 'every B is A'. The argument also involves the converse and contradictory converse of the proposition. In Islamic theology, this method is used to prove the existence of the Necessary Being: if we assume the world is eternal (a possible false assumption), it leads to contradiction; therefore, the world is created.

Key Terms
الخلف
الاقتراني
الاستثنائي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What two types of syllogisms compose a khalf argument?
A) Categorical and disjunctive
B) Categorical and conditional
C) Conditional and disjunctive
D) Inductive and deductive

Key Takeaway
Khalf combines a categorical syllogism that derives an impossibility from an assumption, and a conditional syllogism that uses modus tollens to reject the assumption, thereby proving the desired truth.

Proof That Bodies Are Finite in Extension
Author presents a reductio argument that extension must be finite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the reductio argument for the finitude of bodies and evaluate its validity against the objection of potential infinity.

Core Idea
A reductio proof shows infinite extension leads to contradiction, but potential infinity is not a contradiction.

In this passage, the author presents a proof that dimensions (ab'ād) are finite, using a reductio ad absurdum. The argument assumes the opposite leads to a contradiction, so the original claim must be true. However, an objection is raised: even if extension can be increased without end, that does not prove actual finitude—only potential infinity. The author responds by distinguishing between potential and actual infinity, a key distinction in Islamic kalām.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being proved
The conclusion is stated at the beginning: 'فالأبعاد متناهية' (dimensions are finite). This is the thesis the author defends.
2
Understand the reductio structure
The proof works by showing that assuming the opposite—infinite extension—leads to a contradiction (خلف). The phrase 'وقد كان ذلك واجبًا هذا خلف' indicates that a necessary consequence turned out to be false, so the assumption is rejected.
Reductio ad absurdum is a common logical form in kalām.
3
Examine the key premise about the part
The objection argues that the proof relies on a hypothetical part (الجزء) at the world's edge. The author then addresses whether the lack of extension outside is due to impossibility or absence of space.
This clarifies that the preventer is lack of place, not logical impossibility.
4
Distinguish potential from actual infinity
The author responds to the objection about volume (al-hajm) by showing that endless increase (like adding halves) is only potential, not actual. The body can always be larger but never actually infinite.
This distinction is crucial in Islamic thought: actual infinity is impossible, but potential infinity is not.
5
Evaluate the argument's validity
Consider whether the reductio successfully proves finitude. The author's responses strengthen the proof by clarifying that only actual infinite leads to contradiction, while potential infinite does not.

And that was necessary; this is a contradiction, so dimensions are finite. And [the opponent] might say that this argument is based on [the existence of] a part, because the one standing at the edge of the world, if he cannot extend his hand outside, then there is [a claim that] follows. If that were so, then what follows outside the world would be at the tip of his finger, not following each [part], so the outside of the world would be susceptible to increase and decrease, and thus there would be a quantitative existence. Also, volume is a quiddity that does not prevent [infinite extension]. The conclusion of this proof is that if there were unity from a quiddity that does not prevent [infinite extension], the premise would be false. [The argument goes]: from the occurrence of infinite regress, because every magnitude obtained from a body is such that the sum, because it is a sum, does not prevent it from being exceeded by something greater? So the infinite bodies are possible of existence. The answer to the first is that we count the full moon of existence? The second answer is that knowledge is known, because the king knows how to increase the body without end, that you can take a body and halve it, then halve one of its halves, and so on, then add to the first one of the halves of the second, then add to it half of the remaining quarter, and so on. And if the body is divisible without end, then that increase would continue without end, allowing the past and future to be actually infinite.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage presents a proof that bodies are finite in extension, followed by objections and responses. The author first states that dimensions are finite, using a reductio argument: if a straight line drawn from the center of a sphere were infinite, it would never reach the surface, contradicting the definition of a sphere. Therefore, the line must terminate, so extension is finite. The author then considers an objection: this argument assumes a part (the tip of the finger) at the boundary. If the world has an edge, an observer there could not extend his hand outward, but that does not prove finitude because the absence of space outside prevents extension. The objector also claims that volume (al-hajm) as a quiddity does not entail finitude. The author responds by noting that infinite regress is not impossible per se; rather, bodies could be arbitrarily large but not actually infinite. The answer to the first objection is that the lack of space (al-juz' wa al-makan) prevents extension, not impossibility. The second response argues that even if a body can be increased without end by adding halves, this is a potential infinite, not an actual infinite. The past and future are not actually existing infinite quantities. This aligns with the Ash'ari position that bodies are finite and actual infinites are impossible.
