Proof of Finitude of Bodies: Categorical Argument and Potential vs Actual Infinity
Author demonstrates bodies are finite and rebuts objection on infinite division

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the conditional syllogism (modus tollens) used to prove bodies are finite and distinguish potential from actual infinity in kalam demonstrations.

Core Idea
Finitude of bodies is proven by the impossibility of actual infinite; potential infinite division does not entail actual infinite parts.

In this passage, the author presents a burhan that all bodies must be finite. The proof uses a conditional reductio: if bodies could be infinitely expanded, an actual infinite would exist, which is impossible. An objection based on infinite divisibility is answered by distinguishing potential from actual infinity. Mastering this argument is essential for understanding kalam cosmology and the foundation of proofs for the world's temporality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim to be proved
The author aims to prove that bodies are finite (tanahi al-ajsam). This is the conclusion of the demonstration.
The claim is stated at the end of the first part of the argument.
2
Extract the conditional premise
The author says: 'If continuity were from a nature that does not prevent common participation to infinity, then bodies could be expanded infinitely.' This is the antecedent-consequent pair.
The antecedent is 'continuity from a nature not preventing infinite participation'; the consequent is 'infinite expansion leading to actual infinite'.
3
Reconstruct the modus tollens
The argument is: If P then Q. Not Q (actual infinite is impossible). Therefore not P (continuity must have a nature that prevents infinite participation, so bodies are finite).
The impossibility of actual infinite is a tacit premise in kalam; it is not explicitly stated in this snippet but assumed.
4
Analyze the objection
The objector claims that bodies can be divided infinitely, so they would have infinite parts. This would contradict finitude.
The objection focuses on infinite divisibility, not expansion.
5
Evaluate the reply: potential vs actual
The author responds that infinite division is only in potentiality (bi al-quwwa), not actuality (bi al-fi'l). Therefore no actual infinite parts exist.
This distinction is crucial: potential infinity does not entail actual infinity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حاصل هذا الدليل أنه لو كان الاتصال عن ماهية لا تمنع من وقوع الشركة إلى غير النهاية، لكان كل مقدار يحصل من الجسم فإنه لجسميته جرم لا يمنع من أن يكون محلا لأعظم منه، فلو افتتح من الأجسام لم يكن ذلك الاتساع لكونه جسما ولا لكونه من لوازم الماهية، فلا منع من وقوع الشركة إلى غير النهاية، فإذن لو امتنع كان ذلك المشاع من الأعراض التي يجوز زوالها، وما كان كذلك كان ممكنا التخلف عن الوجود، فإذن الأجسام العنصرية المتناهية ممكنة الوجود. والجواب عن الأول أن عدم الوجود في الخارج ليس لمانع بل لعدم الشرط وهو عدم الحيز والمكان. وعن الثاني أن الاتساع إلى غير النهاية إنما يمكن أن تؤخذ جزء فنصفه بنصفين ثم نصف أحد نصفيه بنصفين آخرين وهلم جرا إلى غير النهاية، وهذا التقسيم إنما هو بالقوة لا بالفعل، فلا يلزم وجود أجزاء لا نهاية لها بالفعل. وأما الأمور المستقبلة فالنظر إما في وجودها أو في تناهيها، فإن في وجودها لا شك أنها موجودة بالفعل بل بالقوة، وأما أن يقال إن كل واحد منها بالفعل في وقت معين فصحيح، وأما أن يقال إن الكل من حيث هو كل موصوف بالوجود فهذا باطل لأنه لا وجود لتلك الجملة حتى توصف بوصف.
The conclusion of this proof is that if continuity were from a nature that does not prevent common participation to infinity, then every quantity obtained from the body would be a body by its corporeality, not preventing it from being a locus for something greater. So if it increased by bodies, that expansion would not be due to its being a body nor due to something from the necessities of the nature. Thus there is no prevention of common participation to infinity. So if it were impossible, that commonality would be among the accidents that can cease. And what is like that is capable of lagging behind existence. Thus the elemental finite bodies are possible in existence. The answer to the first is that the absence of existence in external reality is not due to an obstacle but due to the absence of the condition, namely the absence of space and place. As for the second, the expansion to infinity is only possible such that you take a part and halve it into two halves, then halve one of its two halves into two other halves, and so on to infinity. This division is only in potentiality, not actuality, so it does not entail parts that are infinite in actuality. As for future matters, consideration is either about their existence or their finitude. As for their existence, there is no doubt that they exist in potentiality not actuality. But if it is said that each one of them exists in actuality at a specific time, that is correct. But if it is said that the whole as a whole is described by existence, that is false because that totality has no existence to be described by a description.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage presents a demonstration that bodies are finite and then replies to an objection based on potential infinite division. The author first constructs a reductio argument: if the nature of continuity did not prevent infinite extension, then a body could be expanded indefinitely by adding parts. Since this would lead to an actual infinite, and actual infinites are impossible, the premise is false. Therefore bodies must be finite. The objection argues that division can proceed infinitely, so bodies can be infinitely divisible. The answer distinguishes potential from actual infinity: infinite division is only potential, not actual, so it does not imply an actual infinite number of parts. The discussion then touches on future events, clarifying that they exist potentially, not actually as a whole. The argument is drawn from kalam cosmology, using the impossibility of actual infinite as a central principle.

Refuting the Philosophers' Conditional Proof of Void
Author refutes the philosophers' argument that void occurs when surfaces separate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the philosophers' conditional argument for void and identify the author's refutation of its possibility.

Core Idea
The philosophers' proof of void collapses when you examine what happens at the moment of separation.

The philosophers claimed that if two surfaces meet and then separate instantly, void must appear between them. But is that conditional necessarily true? This passage shows how the author takes that conditional apart, examining both possible scenarios of separation—instantaneous or gradual—and demonstrates that each leads to an impossibility. By the end, you'll see how a conditional argument can be refuted by analyzing its consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the philosophers' conditional claim
The philosophers argue: 'If a surface meets another surface and then rises from it all at once, then void necessarily occurs between them at the moment of rising.' This is a conditional statement: antecedent (surface separation) leads to consequent (void).
2
Examine the antecedent's possibility
The author first questions whether surface-to-surface contact is even possible. He argues that if it were impossible, then void would be apparent (an absurdity), so contact must be possible. This sets the stage for analyzing separation.
The author uses a reductio: if contact were impossible, void would be manifest—which is not observed.
3
Analyze the two modes of separation
Assuming contact occurs, when separation happens, it can be either instantaneous (all at once) or gradual (over time). The author treats these as two cases within the conditional.
The conditional's antecedent ('rises all at once') implies instantaneous separation, but the author considers gradual as a logical alternative.
4
Derive the impossibility from instantaneous separation
If separation is instantaneous, then at the moment of separation, the two surfaces would be in the same position, causing their boundaries to overlap. But that would mean points coincide, which is impossible because two points cannot occupy the same location.
This is a physical impossibility: overlapping points violate the nature of extended bodies.
5
Derive the impossibility from gradual separation
If separation is gradual, a middle space must be created to house the increasing gap. But that middle space would itself need a boundary, leading to an infinite regress of surfaces. Thus, gradual separation is also impossible.
The author implies that the conditional cannot hold because both possible ways of fulfilling the antecedent lead to absurdity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فاقوى الحج طريقان: فإن سطحاً إذا لقي سطحاً آخر ثم ارتفع عن دفعة واحدة، وجب وقوع الخلاء بينهما حال الارتفاع. فلنتأمل إمكان هذه القضية. أما أنه يمكن أن يلقي سطح سطحاً، فلأنه لو امتنع لكان الخلاء ظاهراً. وأيضاً ذلك... فإنه بران الماس هو اتحاد الطوافز فلنفرض وقوع الملك فنقول: اللأحماسة من الأحمد التي تحصل الآن فالتجا في الوضع والخلاف الذاتين المفروضان اشك انهما كانا متماسین. فاذا مارا لاحتماسين فهذا الذي صادراما حل دفعة لقااف يكون سطحال ونقطة. فان كان الاول فقد المطلوب وان كان الثاني لزم تشافع القط والى الآنات وما الان. واما انه لما ارتفع احدهم عن الآخر دفعة لزم خلق الوسط لانه لو حصل منهاجم فامارت بنال لند كان بينها او انقد الله حين رفعنا الاقلا عن الأسفل والأول باطل لانة من الممكن أن ينطبق سطح على سطح كما بيناه. واذا ذلك ملكنا اليف من وقوعة فيه فيكون اللانماس اخلاف الشرفة في الموضع فسلفی فیه آن واحد و مو ممنوع.
The strongest proof has two ways: if a surface meets another surface, then rises from it all at once, then void necessarily occurs between them at the moment of rising. So let us examine the possibility of this proposition. As for the possibility that a surface can meet a surface, because if it were impossible then void would be apparent. And also that... For it is that contact is the union of boundaries. So let us suppose the occurrence of contact, and we say: The two surfaces from among the two [bodies] that are now present, their relation in position and the two assumed [things]... it is not known whether they were touching. So if they cease to be touching, then this thing that happened either occurs at once and then there is a surface and a point. If the first, then the desired is obtained; if the second, then overlapping of points and atoms follows. And as for that when one rises from the other at once, the creation of the middle is necessary because if it happened [otherwise]... the first is false because it is possible for a surface to be superimposed on a surface as we have shown. And if that is the case, we can deny its occurrence. So contact is [something] else... and that is prevented.

Commentary
This passage presents a critical refutation of the philosophers' argument that void (al-khala') must exist when two surfaces separate. The author outlines the strongest proof for void, which claims that if one surface meets another and then rises from it instantly, void necessarily appears between them. He then examines this proposition. He considers whether it is possible for a surface to meet another, arguing that if it were impossible, void would be manifest—an absurdity. The discussion then turns to the nature of contact (al-tamass) as the union of boundaries. The author raises a sophisticated objection: if we assume contact occurs, and then separation happens, either it happens instantaneously (yielding a surface or a point) or gradually. If instantaneously, overlapping of points occurs, which is impossible. If gradually, the creation of a middle space is required, but that middle cannot exist because it would necessitate a regress. The author concludes that the philosophers' claim that void necessarily follows from surface separation is unfounded. Throughout, the argument uses conceptual analysis of surfaces, points, and contact, typical of falsafa debates on place and void.

Reductio Proof Against Void: A Conditional Syllogism
Author disproves void by showing it entails absurdity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the reductio argument against void and identify its conditional syllogism form as modus tollens.

Core Idea
If void existed, motion and rest would be impossible; but they are possible, so void doesn't exist.

In this passage, the author deploys a powerful reductio ad absurdum to refute the concept of void (al-khala'). By showing that the assumption of void leads to the impossibility of both motion and rest—which we know are possible—he concludes that void cannot exist. Our task is to unpack the logical skeleton of this conditional syllogism.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overall conditional claim
The author begins by stating that if void existed, then a body would neither move nor rest. This is the major conditional premise of the syllogism. Locate the phrase 'القول بالخلاء يُوجبُ أن لا يتحرك الجسمُ ولا يسكنَ'.
The conditional is implicit: 'If void exists, then motion and rest are impossible.'
2
Find the consequent breakdown
The argument unpacks why rest and motion are both impossible. For rest, the author argues that in void, a body has no priority (awlawiyya) to occupy one location over another, so no inclination to rest. For motion, any movement would require a preponderant factor (murajjih), which void lacks. Both are therefore impossible.
This is the reasoning within the consequent.
3
Recognize the modus tollens form
The argument does not directly state the denial of the consequent, but it relies on the fact that motion and rest do occur in reality (the consequent is false). Therefore, the antecedent must be false. This is modus tollens: If P then Q; not Q; therefore not P.
The author states 'ذلك محال' (that is impossible) for the consequent, implying its negation.
4
Trace the reductio structure
The argument is a reductio: assume the existence of void, derive a contradiction (both motion and rest impossible, but we observe them), so the assumption is false. The conditional premise is justified by the analysis of void's nature.
The phrase 'فالقول بالخلاء محال' concludes the reductio.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالقول بالخلاء يُوجبُ أن لا يتحرك الجسمُ ولا يسكنَ وذلك محالٌ. بيانُ الشرطِ: الخلاءُ إما أن يكون بُعدًا ممتدًا أو عدمًا محضًا. وعلى التقديرين فالخلاف فيه أصلًا. وإذا كان كذلك فكل جسمٍ يحلُّ في الخلاء لا أولى له بحصوله في بعض جوانب الخلاء دون بعض. وإذا كان كذلك اتحدَ أن يسكن الجسمُ في بعضها لأنه ليس حوله فيه أولى من حصوله عنه. سواء نسب ذلك إلى قوة الجسم أو إلى الفاعل المختار. وإذا امتنع السكون فهو متحرك، والحركة ترك حيز وطلب آخر، وذلك لا يتم إلا باستعانة المتروك على المطلوب. وهذا التمايل لا يتم لولا مرجح. فالقول بالخلاء محال.
The claim of void necessitates that a body neither moves nor rests, and that is impossible. Explanation of the condition: void is either an extended dimension or pure nothingness. In either case, the dispute about it is foundational. If that is the case, then whenever a body occupies the void, there is no priority for its occurrence in one side of the void over another. If that is the case, it would be uniform for the body to rest in some of them because there is no reason for its occurrence in one rather than another. Whether that is attributed to the body's power or to the free agent. If rest is impossible, then it is moving, and motion is leaving a place and seeking another, and that cannot happen except by the assistance of the left place over the sought place. And this inclination cannot occur without a preponderant factor. So the claim of void is impossible.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage presents a reductio ad absurdum argument against the existence of void (al-khala'). The author begins by stating the conclusion that the claim of void entails a contradiction: it would make both motion and rest impossible for a body, which is absurd. The proof proceeds by analyzing the nature of void. Void is either an extended dimension (dimension without matter) or pure nothingness. In both cases, the dispute about its existence is fundamental. The author then argues that if void existed, a body placed in it would have no reason to occupy one particular location over another. Consequently, the body would have no inclination to rest anywhere, and since rest is impossible (because rest requires a specific place), the body must be in motion. However, even motion is problematic because motion requires a preponderant factor (murajjih) to determine the direction from one place to another. Since void lacks any differentiation, such a preponderant factor is absent. Thus both rest and motion are impossible in void, which is absurd. Therefore, the existence of void is impossible. This argument relies on the logical principle of sufficient reason (al-murajjih) and is a classic example of a conditional syllogism in the form of modus tollens: if void existed, then motion and rest would be impossible; but motion and rest are possible (since we observe them), so void does not exist.

Key Terms
الخلاء
الترجيح
السكون

A) Because rest requires a preponderant factor, but void lacks any differentiation.
B) Because the body is forced to move by an external agent.
C) Because the void is an extended dimension that pushes the body.
D) Because rest is only possible for celestial bodies.

Key Takeaway
The reductio against void is a valid modus tollens: if void existed, motion and rest would be impossible; but they are possible, so void does not exist.

Syllogism for Directions: Sphere and Center
Author constructs a syllogism proving that spatial directions are defined by the sphere and center.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the categorical syllogism that proves spatial directions require a spherical determinant and evaluate its validity.

Core Idea
The cosmos itself determines directions—only a single sphere and its center can define all six directions uniquely.

This passage from al-Razi's Mulakhkhas presents a rigorous syllogistic proof that the six finite spatial directions (al-jihāt) must be defined by the celestial sphere (al-muḥīṭ) and its center (al-markaz). The argument eliminates all alternatives through a series of categorical and conditional steps. In this lesson, you will learn to identify the structure of this proof and see how logical reasoning is applied to a foundational cosmological question.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion (natīja)
Locate the final statement of the passage: 'directions are defined only by the center and sphere.' This is what the proof seeks to establish.
2
Extract the key premises
Find the premises that support the conclusion: (1) The determinant of directions must be simple (not composite). (2) A simple determinant must be spherical. (3) A spherical determinant defines both the sphere (outer boundary) and the center (farthest point).
The argument also eliminates the possibility of multiple determinants.
3
Identify the middle term (ḥadd al-awsaṭ)
The term 'simple spherical determinant' connects the premises. It appears as the predicate of the first premise and the subject of the second, linking them to the conclusion about 'directions'.
4
Check figure and mood
The syllogism fits the first figure: Major: Every simple spherical determinant defines by sphere and center. Minor: Every determinant of directions is a simple spherical determinant. Conclusion: Every determinant of directions defines by sphere and center. Mood: AAA-1 (Barbara), which is valid.
The elimination of multiple determinants strengthens the proof but is not part of the bare syllogism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ان تحدد الجهات لا يكون الا بالمحيط و المركز. هذه الجهات المتناهية لا بد لها من محدد و هو ما ان يكون واحد العلكثفان كان واحدال فاعات لا يكون مسند يدا وهو باطل لان المحدد لابد وان يكون بسيطا على ما ستعرف فيكون شكله الطبقي الله فان لم تكن مستدير لم يكن على مجلة الطبيقي وكل ما كان كذلك امكن عوده إلى شكله الطبيعي عند زوال القاسي وذلك انما يكون بتغير الشكل الذي لاتخلى عن حركة مجانية وكل حركة مكانية فعن منم الجمعة فالجهات محدّدة قبل المحدد هذا خلف أو مستديرا وهوا ما ان تجدد المحيطة أول المخيطه والثاني باطل لانه من حصل محيطة تحدد به غاية الغرب اليه وغاية البعد منه وهو المركز واذا كان ذلك كا فياغ الحديد لكن بنا حاجة إلى الساد هذا التحديد إلى غيره وإن كان اكثر من واحد فاما ان لا يكون الواحد منها خطا بالآخر وجيين تحدد غاية القرب من كل واحد لكن لا تجد دعاية البعد أو يكون الواحد منها محطا بالآخر وجينين يكون المحيط كا في الحديد علي ما تمناه فتح المحاط به حشو ا ع ذلك الحكم مثبت ان تحدد الحات اعمل الا بالمركز والمحيط
The definition of directions is only by the sphere and center. These finite directions must have a determinant, which is either one thing or more than one. If it is one, then directions would not be based on anything, which is false because the determinant must be simple, as will be known, so its shape is natural spherical. If it were not spherical, it would not be upon the natural sphere, and whatever is like that can return to its natural shape when the constraint is removed, and that is only by changing the shape, which does not occur without a natural motion, and every spatial motion is from a natural place, so directions would be defined before the determinant; this is a contradiction. Or it is spherical, and then it defines the sphere and the center. The second [i.e., the determinant being more than one] is false, because if the sphere is obtained, it defines the extreme nearness to it and the extreme distance from it, which is the center. And if that is the case, the sphere is sufficient, and there is no need for another determinant. If it is more than one, then either none of them includes the other, then the extreme nearness to each is defined but not the extreme distance; or one includes the other, then the including one is sufficient as the sphere, as we explained, and the included becomes redundant. Thus the ruling is established that directions are defined only by the center and the sphere.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage presents a syllogistic proof that all finite spatial directions (such as up, down, front, back, etc.) must be determined by a single spherical boundary (the celestial sphere) and its center. The argument proceeds by considering the possible number of determinants: if there is one determinant, it must be simple and naturally spherical; otherwise, directions would be defined before the determinant (a contradiction). If it is spherical, it necessarily defines the sphere and center. If there were more than one determinant, either they are unrelated (cannot give a unique farthest point) or one includes the other (making the included redundant). Thus the only coherent definition is via a single sphere and its center. This proof relies on the ancient view that the cosmos is spherical and that directions are absolute, not relative. The author uses a categorical syllogism implicitly: every finite set of directions has a simple determinant; every simple determinant of directions is spherical; every spherical determinant defines by sphere and center; therefore, directions are defined by sphere and center.

المحيط
المركز

C) Because if none includes the other, the farthest distance cannot be defined uniquely.
D) Because the Quran says there is only one direction.

Key Takeaway
The proof uses a first-figure categorical syllogism to logically establish that the cosmos must be spherical and centered, as only such a structure can uniquely define all spatial directions.

Al-Razi's Refutation of Denial of Multiple Intelligibles
Author refutes the claim that the soul cannot think multiple intelligibles simultaneously after separation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct al-Razi's argument against the claim that the soul cannot think multiple intelligibles simultaneously, and explain why this claim would invalidate judgment, definition, and syllogism.

Core Idea
If the soul cannot think multiple things at once, no judgment, no definition, and no syllogism would be possible.

In this passage from his Mulakhkhas, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi takes on a provocative claim: that after death, the soul can only think one thing at a time. Al-Razi shows that this position is self-defeating because it would destroy the very foundations of logic—judgment (tasdiq), definition (hadd), and syllogism (qiyas). Follow his reasoning step by step to see how he defends the soul's capacity for simultaneous intellection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the refuted claim
Find the passage where al-Razi states the opposing view: that the soul after separation cannot think multiple intelligibles simultaneously. He calls out 'the saying of one who claims...'
The claim is about the soul after death (mufaraqa).
2
First argument: judgment requires relation
Al-Razi argues that judgment (tasdiq) is a relation between two things (subject and predicate). For any relation to be present, both relata must be apprehended at once. Denying simultaneous cognition would make judgment impossible.
This links to the definition of tasdiq as a relational act.
3
Second argument: composite definition
Understanding a composite thing by its definition (al-murakkab bi-haddihi) requires grasping all its parts simultaneously. If the soul could only think one thing at a time, no composite definition could be understood.
Example: defining 'human' requires simultaneously thinking 'animal' and 'rational'.
4
Third argument: syllogism requires multiple premises
A syllogism requires at least two premises to produce a conclusion. If only one premise could be held at a time, no conclusion would follow. Al-Razi states that a single premise is never productive.
This is a standard logical principle; single premise cannot yield a new proposition.
5
Fourth argument: knowledge of cause and effect
Al-Razi adds that knowledge of the cause necessitates knowledge of the effect, implying that when the cause is known, the effect is also present in the mind—a simultaneous cognition.
Even when the cause is absent, a disposition for the effect remains.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

That thought (is the cause of) the ordered necessary sciences that necessitate theoretical knowledge. Theoretical knowledge is rarely obtained without [thought] because when the predicate's attribution to the subject is through an intermediary, if the intellect posits it without an intermediary, that judgment would be a judgment that corresponds to the existent, so it would be excellent. What is meant by the movements of abstraction are inseparable expressions for what we explained: that theoretical sciences must end at necessary sciences, either [as] necessary premises or [as] the genus of theoretical knowledge. And if those primary sciences necessitate the indication of theoretical science and are sufficient in their indication without thought, then theoretical science is still dependent on thought. It has become clear from the investigation of this principle: then he called out the saying of one who claims that the soul after separation does not think anything at all by way of place [and] the conjunction of multiple intelligibles simultaneously, and that it is not [possible] that it [?] so that the judgment is not opened at all, because judgment is a relation of one thing to another, and that does not occur except with thinking them both. The falsity of the second indicates the falsity of the premise. Also, we have understood the composed [thing] by its definition, and that does not occur except with all its parts simultaneously. As for the judgments, because he prevented the conjunction of the apparent [?] in the mind, the premise would be one always, and if it were so, the conclusion would not be obtained at all, because we know necessarily that a single premise is not productive. And the falsity of the second indicates the falsity of the premise. And what is said: we have found that we are more puzzled when directed to a more known [thing] than when directed to a known other, so the reference must be to imagination, not to the intellect. That which we have established in that [matter] is that knowledge of the cause necessitates knowledge of the effect, but not conversely. As for the matter, it is due to three aspects: for when the cause is absent, there occurs in the mind a disposition that necessitates the essence of the effect, and when this is so, knowledge of the effect is obtained. And the two premises are clear, based on the fact that intellection requires the obtaining of an essence equivalent to the intelligible in the intellect. When the cause is for us a [source of] affection...
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage begins by continuing the previous discussion on the necessity of thought for theoretical sciences. The author explains that theoretical sciences must ultimately rest on necessary premises, and that thought is needed to arrange these premises. He then presents a refutation of a specific objection: the claim that the soul cannot think multiple things simultaneously after separation from the body. The author argues that if that were true, then no judgment (tasdiq) could occur, because judgment involves a relation between two things and requires thinking both at once. Additionally, understanding a composite thing by its definition requires grasping all its parts simultaneously. Furthermore, in logical deduction, if only one premise could be held at a time, no conclusion would be produced, since a single premise is not productive. The author also addresses an alternative explanation that refers such simultaneous cognition to imagination rather than intellect. He counters that knowledge of a cause necessarily brings knowledge of the effect, and even when the cause is absent, a disposition in the mind can produce the effect's essence. This refutation defends the soul's ability to have multiple intelligibles at once, which is essential for any logical reasoning.

Knowledge of Cause and Effect: Universal vs. Particular
Author defends causal knowledge by distinguishing universal from particular.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between universal and particular knowledge of an effect when the cause is known, and apply this distinction to resolve objections in causal arguments.

Core Idea
Knowing the cause gives universal, not particular, knowledge of the effect.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi addresses a common objection to causal knowledge: if a possible thing in itself does not necessitate existence, how can knowing its cause give certainty about the effect? The objection confuses universal knowledge (the effect as a type) with particular knowledge (every detail of the instance). Al-Razi's response clarifies the scope of causal knowledge, a crucial distinction in both logic and Islamic legal theory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's claim
The objector argues that considering a possible thing (al-mumkin) in itself does not necessitate knowledge of whether it exists or not. This challenges the idea that knowing the cause yields knowledge of the effect.
The objector treats the possible as indifferent by nature.
2
Find the author's initial concession
Al-Razi concedes that considering the possible in itself does not necessitate knowledge of preponderance, but it does not prevent acquiring such knowledge from another source (the cause).
This sets up the distinction between self-evidence and acquired knowledge.
3
Locate the key statement on universality
Al-Razi states that whoever knows that A necessitates B, then B is known, because being caused by A does not preclude B from being universal. He concludes: 'the effect known by its cause is universal' (فالمعلول المعلوم بعلته كلي).
This is the core of the resolution: causal knowledge yields universal, not particular, knowledge.
4
Apply the distinction to the objection
The objector's doubt arises from expecting particular knowledge (e.g., every detail of the effect). Al-Razi shows that the cause gives universal knowledge (the effect as a type), which is sufficient for certain knowledge of the effect's existence and general nature.
This resolves the objection without requiring exhaustive particularity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقابل أن يقول: الممكن يصدق عليه أنه لذاته لا يقتضي الوجود[؟] بل يقتضي اللاوجود[؟]، ومقتضى ذلك ما يلزم من النظر إليه من حيث هو هو حصول الوجود[؟]، لا أن يلزم من النظر إليه من حيث هو[؟] حصول الوجود. وإذا بات كذلك نحن نقول: النظر إلى الممكن من حيث هو[؟] ما يقتضي العلم بترجح وجوده على عدمه، ولكنه لا يمنع من حصول العلم بذلك الترجح. فإن لم يكن العلم بتلك الماهية يقتضي العلم بذلك الترجح، عن[على] أن حصل الترجح. قلتم: إن المستدلين تعليق الدلالة على النفي إن بالعلم به[؟]... هذا هو الشعر. والحق أن من علم أن الألف س[ـبب؟] توجب الباء، فإن الباء كان لأن نفس صحة معناه لا تمنع من الشركة، وكون صادرا عن الألف لا يمنع منها، والكلي المقيد بالكلي كلي أيضا، فالمعلول المعلوم بعلته كلي.
And the objector may say: The possible is affirmed of it that it does not necessitate existence by itself, but rather necessitates non-existence, and the implication of that is what follows from considering it in itself as obtaining existence, not that following from considering it as obtaining existence follows. And if so, we say: Considering the possible in itself does not necessitate knowledge of the preponderance of its existence over its non-existence, but it does not prevent the acquisition of knowledge of that preponderance. For if knowledge of that essence does not necessitate knowledge of that preponderance, then the preponderance occurs. You said: The proponents attach the indication to negation, for knowledge of it... this is poetry. The truth is that whoever knows that A necessitates B, then B is because the very correctness of its meaning does not preclude sharing, and its being caused by A does not preclude it, and the universal qualified by the universal is also universal. So the effect known by its cause is universal.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange (jadal) concerning the relationship between knowledge of a cause and knowledge of its effect. The author presents an objection: a possible thing (al-mumkin) in itself does not necessitate existence or non-existence; considering it alone does not yield knowledge of which state preponderates. The author responds by distinguishing universal from particular: the consideration of the possible in itself does not necessitate knowledge of preponderance, but it does not prevent such knowledge from being acquired from another source. The author then refutes the objection by stating that if one knows that A (the cause) necessitates B (the effect), then B is known to exist, because the meaning of B does not preclude it from being caused by A, and the universal qualified by the universal remains universal. Thus, the effect known through its cause is known universally (kulli), not as a particular instance. This distinction resolves the doubt: knowledge of the cause yields universal knowledge of the effect, not knowledge of every particular detail.

Proof That Every Immaterial Intellect Understands Itself: A Syllogistic Analysis
Author constructs a syllogistic proof that every immaterial intellects itself and all other immaterial quiddities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the multi-step categorical syllogism proving that every immaterial substance intellects itself and all other immaterial quiddities.

Core Idea
Immaterial substances necessarily intellect themselves because intellection is the presence of quiddities, and immaterial quiddities include all quiddities.

This lesson unpacks a dense syllogistic proof from the Islamic philosophical tradition. The argument is foundational for Avicennan psychology and theology: if intellect is immaterial, its intellection of itself is not an act but a necessary concomitant of its essence. We will break down the logical steps, identify premises and conclusions, and see how self-intellection follows from immateriality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overall conclusion
Read the final sentence of the passage. The conclusion is that every immaterial (mujarrad) must be understanding of itself and of all other immaterial things. This is the target of the proof.
The conclusion is stated twice: once at the start and once at the end.
2
Extract the main syllogistic chain
The proof contains several linked syllogisms. First, it establishes that every immaterial quiddity is capable of understanding all other immaterial quiddities. Then from that it deduces self-intellection. List the intermediate conclusions.
Look for the phrase 'فإذن' (therefore) which marks conclusions.
3
Analyze the key lemma: all immaterial quiddities are intelligible together
The author argues that every immaterial is necessarily an intelligible. Because it is an intelligible, it can be understood together with every other intelligible. This implies its form (hay'a) is the other quiddities. Find this reasoning in the text.
This step uses the nature of intellection as the presence of the intelligible in the intellect.
4
Resolve the apparent circularity about existence and possibility
The text introduces a hypothetical objection: if the quiddity were separate, it would lead to a vicious circle where possibility is simultaneous with existence. Understand why this supports that the quiddity's being in the intellect is its concomitance with intellect.
This is a reductio ad absurdum: the opposite assumption (separation) is impossible.
5
Verify the logical form
The proof uses categorical syllogisms. Identify the middle term in each sub-argument. For the final step: 'whoever understands something can understand that he understands' – this is an immediate inference. Check that each step is valid.
The overall structure is a compound syllogism with an immediate inference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فكل مجرد يعقل ذاته. أما الصغرى فلأن كل مجرد يفتح أن يكون معقولا بالضرورة وكل كان كذلك صح أن يكون معقولا مع كل ما عداه من المعقولات وكل ما كان كذلك صح على ما هيئته سائر الماهيات. فإذن لم يتجد فان من أن يقارت ماهيته سائر الماهيات فلك الصحة أن يعبر فيها كون تلك الماهية في العقل عبارة عن كونها مقارنة للعقل. لزم أن تكون صحة وجود مناظرة من وجوده وقد كان الوجود متأخرا عن الصحة هذا خلف أو لا يعتبر فيها ذلك. وجيني تلك الماهية المعقولة إذا وجدت قائمة بنفسها في الخارج أمكن أن تفاوت ناهيتها ماهيات الإنشاء المعقولة ولا معنى للتعقل إلا هذه المقارنة. فإذن كل ماهية مجردة فانه تفتح عليها إن تعقل سائر الماهيات المجردة وكل ما فتح فى حى المفارقات وجب. فإذن كل ماهية مجردة فانها تعقل جميع الماهيات. فكل من عقل شيئا أمكنه أن يعقل كونه عاقلا لذلك المعقول وذلك يتضمن كونه عاقلا لذاته. فإذن المجرد يجب أن يكون عاقلا لذاته وجميع ما عداه من المجردات.
So every immaterial intellect understands itself. As for the minor premise, every immaterial is necessarily capable of being an intelligible; and everything like that is such that it is possible to be understood together with every other intelligible; and everything like that is such that its form (hay'a) is the other quiddities. So then it is not found that its quiddity is separated from other quiddities; thus it is valid that in it the being of that quiddity in the intellect is nothing but its being concomitant with the intellect. It would necessarily follow that the possibility of existence is simultaneous with its existence, but existence is posterior to possibility—this is a vicious circle. So then every immaterial quiddity has the capacity to understand other immaterial quiddities; and everything that has capacity for the separated beings is necessary. So every immaterial quiddity understands all quiddities. So whoever understands something can understand that he understands that intelligible, and that includes his understanding of himself. Therefore the immaterial must be understanding of itself and of all other immaterial things.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage presents a proof that every immaterial substance (mujarrad) intellects its own essence and all other immaterial quiddities. The author uses a multi-step syllogistic argument. First, he establishes that every immaterial is necessarily intelligible in itself (i.e., capable of being an object of intellection). Then, because it is intelligible, it can be understood together with all other intelligibles, implying that its form includes the forms of others. He then argues that if its quiddity were separate from others, it would either lead to an absurdity (the possibility of existence being simultaneous with existence, while possibility is prior) or else the quiddity would not be considered as such. Thus, when the quiddity exists in itself externally, it can be combined with other quiddities in the intellect. Since intellection is precisely this combination (muqarana), every immaterial quiddity can intellection all immaterial quiddities. Then from the fact that it understands something, it can understand that it understands, which entails self-intellection. Therefore, every immaterial intellects itself and all other immaterial things. This argument is typical of Avicennan psychology.

التعقل
المعقول
المقارنة

B) Because it is separate from all other quiddities
C) Because it is capable of being an intelligible and its form includes other quiddities
D) Because it is a body

Key Takeaway
The immaterial intellect necessarily understands itself because understanding is the presence of quiddities, and an immaterial quiddity, being free of matter, is intrinsically united with all quiddities, including its own.

Reconstructing the Categorical Syllogism on Abstract Entities
Author proves abstract entities are intellective and responds to an objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the categorical syllogism proving abstract entities are intellective and evaluate the objection using the distinction of concomitance types.

Core Idea
Concomitance is not a monolith: distinguishing its types saves the proof from the objection.

This passage presents a rigorous syllogistic proof that every abstract entity (mujarrad) is intellective, culminating in the conclusion that such entities intellect all essences. The author then anticipates a powerful objection – that the proof would wrongly imply God's dependence on others – and replies by analyzing the notion of 'concomitance' (muqarana) into four distinct types. By mastering this argument, you will see how subtle conceptual distinctions can rescue a philosophical proof from apparent contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Extract the categorical syllogism's premises
Identify the minor premise: 'Every abstract entity is necessarily intellectually grasped (ma'qul).' The major premise: 'Whatever is intellectually grasped can be intellectually grasped together with all other intelligibles.' Follow the chain to the conclusion: 'Every abstract entity intellects its essence to all other essences.'
The minor premise is justified by the nature of abstraction; the major is assumed as a property of intelligibles.
2
Locate the objection and its basis
Find where the opponent says 'We do not grant...' and then objects that separate entities (mufariqat) are agreed to be non-intelligible. Also note the objection that even if they are intelligible, they need not be intelligible with everything else, and that the proof would make God's existence depend on others.
The objector targets both the minor premise and the major premise's applicability.
3
Analyze the author's response using types of muqarana
The author counters by dividing concomitance into four specific types: two states in one subject, state-to-subject, two intellectual forms in the intellect, and intellectual form with the soul. He shows that the objection's problematic type (e.g., God's existence requiring another) does not follow because the relevant type is different.
The key move is that the type of concomitance in the proof (intellectual forms in the intellect) is not the type that would imply ontological dependence.
4
Assess the completeness of the defense
Note that the author also argues that even if an essence exists externally, it can still be concomitant with other intelligibles under a different type. The passage ends with a rhetorical question about whether mental essences when externalized are prevented from concomitance – leaving room for further debate.
The defense is not entirely closed; it invites further objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإنه يعقل ذاته فكل مجرد يعقل ذاته. أما الصغرى فلأن كل مجرد يجب أن يكون معقولا بالضرورة. وكل كان كذلك صح أن يكون معقولا مع كل ما عداه من المعقولات. وكل ما كان كذلك صح أن يعقل ماهيته سائر الماهيات. فإذا لم يجد [؟] فأنى يقال: ماهيته سائر الماهيات؟ بلى معنى العقل [؟] في حصول نهاية المعقولية للعاقل. فإذن لم يجد [؟]. والاعتراض: لا نسلم أن كل مجرد فإنه لا يفتقر إلى تعقل ماهيته الغير [؟]. ثم ننقض بالمفارقات غير وجودها بالاتفاق مع أنها غير معقولة. ثم لو سلمنا أن كل مجرد يجب أن يكون معقولا لكن الأمر أنه لا يجب أن يكون معقولا مع كل ما عداه. ثم قوله: كل معقول فان ماهيته حاصلة في العاقل، قلنا قد أبطلنا هذه القاعدة. ثم لو سلمنا لكن السلام أن صحة المقارنة لا تتوقف على حصولها في العقل. قوله: لو كان كذلك لزم توقف صحة وجود الله على وجود غيره، قلنا هذه مغالطة لأن المقارنة جنس تحته أنواع: مقارنة الحالين في المحل الواحد، ومقارنة الحال للمحل، ومقارنة الصورتين المعقولتين في العقل، ومقارنة الصورة العقلية مع النفس. فالنوعان الأخيران مختلفان فلا يلزم. ثم قد يقال أيضا: إن الماهية المعقولة إذا وجدت في الخارج أمكن أن يقارنها سائر الماهيات المعقولة لأن ذلك مقارنة الحال للمحل وهو نوع مخالف. ثم لو سلمنا أن صحة المقارنة لا تتوقف على كونها عقلية فلم علم أن هذه الماهية إذا وجدت في الخارج وجب أن تمتنع ذلك؟ بيانه أن تلك الماهية حركات ذهنية [؟] لا بد أن تعرض حين تصير خارجية فلم المجهزان؟
For it intellects its essence, so every abstract entity intellects its essence. As for the minor premise, because every abstract entity must necessarily be intellectually grasped. And everything that is such is correct to be intellectually grasped together with everything else among the intelligibles. And everything that is such is correct to intellect its essence to the rest of the essences. So if it does not find [?], then how can it be said: its essence is the rest of the essences? Rather the meaning of intellect [?] in attaining the ultimate intelligibility for the intellector. So it does not find [?]. And the objection: We do not grant that every abstract entity does not need to intellect its other essence [?]. Then we refute by the separate entities other than their existence by agreement despite being not intelligible. Then if we grant that every abstract entity must be intelligible, yet the matter is that it is not necessary that it be intelligible together with everything else. Then his statement: Every intelligible, its essence is present in the intellector, we say we have invalidated this principle. Then if we grant that, but the peace is that the validity of concomitance does not depend on its occurrence in the intellect. His statement: If that were so, it would necessitate the validity of God's existence depending on the existence of another, we say this is a fallacy because concomitance is a genus under which are types: concomitance of two states in one subject, concomitance of a state to a subject, concomitance of two intellectual forms in the intellect, and concomitance of an intellectual form with the soul. So the last two types are different, so it does not follow. Then it may also be said: Indeed the intelligible essence, when it exists externally, can be concomitant with the rest of the intelligible essences because that is concomitance of a state to a subject, which is a different type. Then if we grant that the validity of concomitance does not depend on it being intellectual, how do we know that this essence, when it exists externally, must be prevented from that? Explanation: that essence, mental movements [?], must be subject when it becomes external, so why the two unknowns?
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage presents a proof that every abstract entity (mujarrad) must be self-intellective and intellect all other abstract entities, followed by an objection and detailed response. The argument begins with a categorical syllogism: the minor premise asserts that every abstract entity is necessarily intellectually grasped (ma'qul), and the major premise states that anything intellectually grasped can be intellectually grasped together with all other intelligibles. The conclusion is that every abstract entity can intellect all essences, including itself. The author then addresses an objection: the opponent denies that every abstract entity is self-intellective, citing the example of separate entities (mufariqat) that are agreed to be non-intelligible. The response distinguishes different types of concomitance (muqarana): between two states in one subject, between a state and a subject, between two intellectual forms in the intellect, and between an intellectual form and the soul. The objection's apparent contradiction is resolved by noting that these types are different in essence, so the necessary concomitance of God's existence with another does not follow. The author further argues that even if an essence exists externally, it can still be concomitant with other intelligibles because that is a different type of concomitance. The passage concludes with a question about whether mental essences become external and why that would prevent concomitance.

Refuting the Separation Theory of Pain: A Logical Critique
Author refutes identity of pain with separation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the author's argument against the identity of pain with separation and evaluate its logical validity.

Core Idea
Pain is a positive existent quality, not merely the absence of continuity.

This passage from al-Razi's Mulakhkhas challenges a widely held theory that pain is merely the perception of a break in continuity. The author uses counterexamples and causal reasoning to argue that pain is a real, existent attribute (kayfiyya wujudiyya) rather than a privation. We will analyze his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target claim
What exactly is the author refuting? Locate the claim that pain is the perception of separation (tafriq) of continuity.
The target is the theory that pain is identical to or caused by separation.
2
Extract the author's positive thesis
Find where the author states that pain is 'wujudi bil-dharura' (necessarily existent). This is his positive claim: pain is a positive quality, not a privation.
Privation (adam) cannot cause existence (wujud).
3
Find the counterexample
The author gives examples where separation occurs without pain. Identify one: 'separation occurs in most times for most organs, yet we do not feel pain.'
This shows that separation is not sufficient for pain.
4
Examine the causal reasoning
The author argues that if separation were the cause, it would always produce pain. But since it does not, separation is not the cause. What logical principle is he using?
This is modus tollens: If P (separation) implies Q (pain), and not Q, then not P.
5
Analyze the objection and response
An objector says that pain does occur at separation sometimes. How does the author respond? He says correlation is not causation: 'what is known by sense is the occurrence of pain at separation in some cases, not in all.'
This refutes the inference from occasional conjunction to necessary causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يقال انه بالوجدان دفع ضد الشرف و التي تعرف الاتصال ليس حوا باللات من خالف الحث فيه لوجوه اذاعة في التفرق عدي لانه عدم الاتصال عما من شانه أن يتصل و الألم وجودى بالضرورة والعدم لا يكون بالذات للوجود السكين الحاد جدا بما عض الأصبع والعب بالالم الا بعدد مان ان ا علم لان من الحان ربات سالانہ کان مشاقا الى مطالعة الوجد بين الحلى والمال الخلي فاذا وجد هذ الحريات المعينة الدفع عنه ضرب الشوق الى الابيات ما شمال مانوالی بات علماه كانت متقی العلم ان الشكل واحصل الانفعال اتی اذا دوام الانات في النظر إلى الواعية الملاح ملاحة احان و موافقتها الذي عرايا عند الوسا و أعامل بعد الزان ولو كان التعرف عله بالنات للالم للتخلف عندج الكيفيات الحادثة الاجام التي تحتكرة القمر الما تحدث عن مبدا عام الفيض وانما اختلف الأعراض والقمر في اجسام هذا العالم لاختلافها في الاستعداد وهذه المقدمة متفق عليها بين الفلاسفة وان كنا لا نقوك بها ففوك الجسم المكب أنا اختص كيفية المخصوصة لان خلال المزاج إفادة استعداد القبول تلك الكيفية عن واهب القمر دون سكر الكيفيات - It should not be said that it is by intuition that one repels against honor, and that which is known as continuity is not [perceived] without pain, for those who disagree have points: in separation it is counted as lack of continuity from what is apt to be continuous, and pain is existent necessarily, while non-existence is not essentially a cause of existence. ... If the knife cuts the finger sharply, pain follows only after a while? ... And if the cause of pain were separation, it would lag when qualities arise that the moon acquires from the principle of emanation, but only differences in preparedness cause different qualities—this premise is agreed upon by the philosophers, though we do not hold it. So the body acquires a specific quality because the mixture (mizaj) gives a preparedness to receive that quality from the giver of forms, not other qualities. So the mixture does not remain, the transformation of that quality is prevented. ... The pain appears when the aggressor actually separates the continuity of the joint, and average between them and its sleeve. And if [separation] is the cause, separation occurs in most times for most organs, yet we do not feel pain. So separation is not the cause. ... As for the second: pain is a sensed quality, so if we do not find it despite safety and connection, that indicates it is an accident. And if pain is nonexistent, and separation is the cause, then the cause would be non-existent essentially. If it is said: we sense pain when separation occurs, and it becomes like a pain (?) ... he inferred to refute what you established by quality, so it is refuted. I say: what is known by sense is the occurrence of pain at separation in some cases, not in all. And we do not hasten from the first to the second, so that would not be a valid inference.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the theory that pain (al-alam) is merely the perception of separation (tafriq) of continuity. The author begins by stating that one should not accept the intuitive claim that pain is simply the loss of connection, because pain is a positive existent quality (wujudi bil-dharura), not a privation. The author gives examples: if pain were merely separation, then any cut would immediately cause pain, but there is often a delay, and many separations occur without pain (e.g., in organs that are frequently separated by movement). The author also invokes the theory of emanation from the giver of forms, arguing that qualities arise from preparedness, not from separation. The passage then addresses an objection: if someone says that pain is sensed at the time of separation, the author replies that this only shows occasional correlation, not necessary causation. The argument is a classic critique of a causal claim by showing counterexamples and logical gaps.

Key Takeaway
Pain is a positive existent quality, not a privation; the separation theory fails due to counterexamples and the logical fallacy of assuming causation from correlation.

Corporeal Power Cannot Act on the Infinite: A Conditional Proof
Author proves impossibility of motion in no time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the conditional proof that a corporeal power cannot traverse an infinite distance and identify its modus tollens structure.

Core Idea
A corporeal power cannot produce instantaneous motion because distance and time are divisible.

This passage presents a crisp logical refutation of the claim that a physical power could act over an infinite distance in zero time. The author uses a conditional argument (if P then Q) and then shows that Q is impossible, therefore P is false. This is a classic modus tollens inference, widely used in Kalam to argue for the temporal origination of the world. Let's walk through the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being disproven
The author aims to disprove the statement: 'A corporeal power can act on an infinite distance.' This is the antecedent of the conditional.
2
Extract the conditional premise
The author states: 'If that were correct, then motion would occur in no time.' This is a conditional: if corporeal power can act on infinite distance, then motion occurs in no time.
3
Follow the reductio: why the consequent is impossible
The author argues that motion in no time is impossible because any distance is divisible: traversing the whole distance would require traversing half first. If the whole took no time, half would also take no time, but that leads to a contradiction (half of no time is still no time, but half must precede the whole, requiring time). Alternatively, if half takes time, then the whole takes double that time, not no time.
4
Conclude the original claim is false
Since the consequent (motion in no time) is impossible, the antecedent (corporeal power can act on infinite distance) is also impossible. This is a valid modus tollens inference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القوة الجسمانية لا تقوى على ما لا نهاية له بحالة، لو صح ذلك لوقعت الحركة في لا زمان وهو محال، لأن كل حركة على مسافة منقسمة، فنصفها قبل كله، فلو كان قطع الكل في لا زمان لكان قطع النصف في لا زمان وهو أيضا محال، ولو كان قطع النصف في زمان منقسم لكان قطع الكل في ضعف ذلك الزمان، فلا يكون قطع الكل في لا زمان.
The corporeal power is not capable of what has no end in any state. If that were correct, motion would occur in no time, and that is impossible, because every motion over a divisible distance has its half before its whole. So if traversing the whole were in no time, then traversing half would be in no time, which is also impossible. And if traversing half were in divisible time, then traversing the whole would be in double that time, so traversing the whole would not be in no time.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage presents a proof by contradiction regarding the capability of corporeal power over infinite distances. The author begins by stating that a corporeal power (like physical force) cannot act upon an infinite extent. If it could, then motion over that infinite would occur in zero time, which is absurd. The reasoning is: any motion over a distance that is divisible (like a line segment) must traverse half the distance before the whole. If the whole is traversed in no time, then half must also be traversed in no time. But that leads to a contradiction because if half takes some time, the whole takes double that time, not zero. Thus, the premise is false. This argument is typical in Kalam and falsafah discussions against infinite motion in finite time.

Key Terms
القوة الجسمانية
لا نهاية
حركة
زمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what would happen if a corporeal power could act on an infinite distance?
A) Motion would occur in no time.
B) Motion would occur in finite time.
C) Motion would be impossible.
D) The power would be exhausted.

Key Takeaway
Motion over a divisible distance requires time, so traversing an infinite distance in no time is impossible, proving that corporeal power cannot act on the infinite.

Reconstructing the Syllogism for Finite Natural Motion
Author proves natural force yields finite motion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the categorical syllogism that proves natural force produces only finite motion using the proportion principle.

Core Idea
Natural force's motion is finite because its subject is divisible and the whole is stronger than the part.

This dense passage from al-Razi's Mulakhkhas deploys a rigorous categorical syllogism to demonstrate that natural force cannot generate infinite motion. The argument rests on the axiom that the proportion of effects mirrors the proportion of causes. By analyzing the ratio of the whole force to a part force, you will see how al-Razi deduces the finitude of the entire motion. Let's walk through the logical steps.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target conclusion
First, locate what the author aims to prove: 'So the action of the whole force is finite, which is what was sought.' This is the conclusion: the motion produced by the entire natural force is finite.
The conclusion appears at the very end of the passage.
2
Extract the major premise on proportions
The author states: 'the proportion of effects is like the proportion of causes.' This is a general principle that relates cause and effect ratios. It serves as the major premise.
In full: 'it has been established that the proportion of effects is like the proportion of causes.'
3
Extract the minor premise on force ratios
The author asserts: 'the proportion of part of the force to the whole is like the proportion of something to its like.' This means that the ratio of part-force to whole-force is a finite ratio (part to whole). This is the minor premise.
By 'something to its like' he indicates a finite comparison.
4
Derive the conclusion via syllogism
From the major premise (cause ratio equals effect ratio) and the minor premise (part-force to whole-force is finite), we conclude that the effect of the whole force (its motion) is finite relative to the effect of the part force. If the part force's motion is finite, then the whole force's motion is also finite, and if the part force's motion were infinite, the whole force would have to exceed the infinite, which is impossible.
Al-Razi also presents a reductio: if the part force performed infinite motions, the whole force either does not exceed (absurd) or does exceed (adding to infinite is impossible). Thus only the finite case stands.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القوة فاذن الجان لو اختلف ذلك القبول لا لكن ذلك الاخلال بسبب المنيك بر سبب المحرك فان القوة في الحجر الاكبر اعظم من حملة الاصغر لان الموجودة في الاصغر موجودة في الاكبر [؟] واما القوة المشرقة فانها مختلف تحرك كما للجم العظيم والصغير الاخلال المحرك بل ان القابل كلما كان اعظم كان العالق فيه اكبر واذا عرفت هذه المقدمة فقول اما القوة الطبيعية فانه طبع فيها ان تحرك تحريكا غير متناه لان كل قوة جسمانية فهي منقصة بقسمة محلها فقوة الكل اقوى من قوة البعض الفرد فاذا حل كلا جميعهما من مبدا مفروض فان حركت القوة الصغرى حركات غير متناهية فالكبرى ان لم تزد عليها كان حالها لا مع غير محال مع غيره واذا زادت عليها وقعت الزيادة على غير المتناهي من الحركات التي هي بها غير متناه وان حركت الصغرى حركات متناهية وقد ثبت ان نسبة الاثرين كنسبة المؤثرين ونسبة بعض القوة الى كلها نسبة شيء الى مثله كانت نسبة الاثرين ايضا نسبة متناه الى متناه ففعل كل القوة متناه وهو المطلوب
Force then [if] the body differs in acceptance, no, but that disruption is due to the mover. For the force in the larger stone is greater than that of the smaller because what exists in the smaller exists in the larger [?]. As for the emanating force, it differs in movement as the great and small [bodies] disrupt the mover. Rather, the more receptive the body, the greater the engagement therein. When you understand this premise, I say: as for natural force, it is by nature that it moves with an infinite motion, because every bodily force is diminished by the division of its subject. So the force of the whole is stronger than the force of the individual part. If both are set from a given starting point, then if the smaller force performs infinite movements, the larger force, if it does not exceed it, would be in a state of being not impossible with respect to the other. If it does exceed, then the increase occurs upon the infinite number of movements, which are themselves infinite. If the smaller force performs finite movements, and it has been established that the proportion of effects is like the proportion of causes, and the proportion of part of the force to the whole is like the proportion of something to its like, then the proportion of the two effects is also finite to finite. So the action of the whole force is finite, which is what was sought.
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage is a proof (burhan) that natural force (al-quwwa al-tabi'iyya) cannot produce infinite motion. The author begins by establishing a premise about force: the larger the receptive body, the greater the force acting upon it. He then observes that every bodily force is diminished when its subject is divided. Therefore, the force of the whole body is stronger than the force of any part. From a given starting point, if the smaller force performs an infinite number of movements, the larger force either does or does not exceed it. Both possibilities lead to contradiction: either the larger force is not exceeding the infinite, which is absurd, or it adds to the infinite, which implies adding to what is infinite, also absurd. If the smaller force performs finite movements, then due to the proportional relationship between causes and effects, the larger force's effect is also finite. Hence, the whole force's motion is finite.

Key Terms
القوة الطبيعية
غير متناه
نسبة

C) The larger force will be weaker due to its size.
D) The smaller force will cease to move.

Key Takeaway
Natural force produces finite motion because the proportional relation between cause and effect forces the whole force's effect to be finite when the part force's effect is finite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Responding to the Celestial Cycles Objection
Author refutes infinite forced force and answers a celestial cycles objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical flaw in the objection from celestial cycles to the claim that forced force cannot be infinite, and explain the author's response using the concept of potential vs. actual infinity.

Core Idea
Celestial cycles do not prove actual infinity; their sum is not fixed.

In this passage, the author argues that forced force cannot produce infinite motion. An objection is raised: celestial spheres exhibit countless cycles, suggesting infinite motion. The author responds by distinguishing between potential and actual infinity, showing that the sum of past cycles is not fixed, so they do not constitute an actual infinite. Understanding this argument sharpens your ability to detect ill-founded objections in kalam cosmology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
The author asserts that forced force (al-quwwa al-qasriyya) cannot produce an infinite action. Locate this claim in the Arabic passage.
2
Locate the objection
An objector points to celestial cycles (dawrat al-'umr) as evidence of infinite motion. Find where this objection appears.
The objection is implicit; the author says 'this refutes your principle regarding cycles'.
3
Extract the author's response
The author responds that the sum of past cycles is not fixed (ghayr thabit). This means the cycles are not a completed actual infinity but only potentially infinite.
The distinction between potential and actual infinity is key.
4
Evaluate the logical force
Assess whether the response adequately refutes the objection. Does denying that the sum is fixed truly defeat the claim of infinite motion? Consider how the objection assumes an actual infinite series.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واما القوة القسرية فيستحيل ان يكون فعلها غير متناه لان تحريكها لكل الجسم من بد امعين اقل من تحريكها جزءا منه منفع زيادة حركة الجن عا حركة الكل من الجانب الذى فرض الكل غير متناه فيه وذلك ايضا محاله بم ولكامل للقوات هذا ينقض ما اصلكم بات دورات العمر اكثر من دورات تحل مع ان ذلك لم يقيض ما قولكم اولاً لها فل ثم عند باتِ الحكم عليه بالن بادة والنقصان يجب ان يكون امر البونيا ومجموع الدورات الماضية غير ثابت كان ذلك بعينه جوابا عاد كر حق مع هذه الحجة ان يوجين اما لولا فلات ذات القوة وقات العلة الموحدة لها وتاثرهان اثرها اما ان ينتمى الى حيث ينقلك من الإمكان الذاتي إلى الإمتناع الذاتى وهو ظاهر الفساد اور الكون كذلك وجود فيكون القوة الجمانية موثرة ف أثرها ممكنا لحدا وهذ ابنا فض الفول بات القوة الجمانية القوى على الفعل داما و اما قانتا عنوان الأرض لو لقيت دا علم حتى ها خالية عن العوارض الغربية لوجد عن قوتها الطبيعية سكون دَامَ وَ الذى يقاك السكون عدم. فليس شيلات حصوله يحيره الطبيعي الخاص به المرقابت بالاتفاق. وسو المستمى با اين ومن الجانبين كلمات اخر مستقصاة في المباحث الشرقية
As for forced force, it is impossible for its action to be infinite, because its moving of the whole body from a given starting point is less than moving a part of it, [due to] the increase of movement of the part over the movement of the whole from the side where the whole is assumed infinite, and that is also impossible. This refutes your principle regarding [celestial] cycles: the cycles of the sphere are more than the cycles of [another]? But that does not contradict your initial statement? Then you ruled on it with increase and decrease. It must be that the matter of the [celestial] building and the sum of past cycles is not fixed. That itself is a response to the argument [that] ... [the force] either leads from essential possibility to essential impossibility, which is obviously false, or if it is such, then the bodily force is effective in its action as possible. This refutes the statement that bodily force is powerful over action always. But regarding the earth's force: if it were stripped of accidental attributes, its natural force would be perpetual rest, and rest is non-being. So its acquisition is not [due to] its natural agent but by agreement [?]. And what is called 'ayyn?' And from both sides are other words fully discussed in the Eastern Investigations.
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage first argues that forced force (al-quwwa al-qasriyya) cannot produce infinite motion because moving the whole body from a given point requires less force than moving a part? The argument is obscure but concludes that infinite motion from forced force is impossible. Then an objection is raised: celestial spheres exhibit many cycles, implying infinite motion, but the author notes that the sum of past cycles is not fixed, meaning they are not actually infinite in act. The response addresses a possible argument about essential possibility turning into impossibility, and affirms that bodily force can be actual. Finally, the author digresses to the earth's natural force: if the earth were free of accidental impediments, its natural force would cause perpetual rest (sukun), which is a privation. The passage ends with a reference to 'al-Mabahith al-Sharqiyya' for fuller treatment.
