The Impossibility of Infinite Regress: Proving the First Cause
Author argues against infinite regress of causes, establishing a first cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the modus tollens argument that proves the existence of a Necessary Being from the impossibility of infinite regress.

Core Idea
Infinite regress of causes is absurd; therefore, a Necessary Being must exist.

This passage from al-Razi's Mulakkhkhas tackles one of the most fundamental issues in Islamic philosophy: the proof of God's existence through causality. The author argues that an infinite chain of causes is impossible, forcing the mind to accept a First Cause that is uncaused and necessarily existent. We will dissect this argument into its logical form, focusing on the conditional reasoning that leads to the conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
Find the conclusion that the passage aims to prove. Al-Razi states that the chain of causes must end at a cause whose non-existence is impossible – the Necessary Being.
This appears near the phrase 'فينتهي لا محالة إلى علة يكون عدمها [؟] والعدم عليها'.
2
Extract the conditional structure
The argument is a modus tollens: If there were no first cause (i.e., every cause were contingent), then there would be an infinite regress. But infinite regress is absurd. Therefore, there must be a first cause.
The conditional is implied: 'If no Necessary Being, then infinite regress.'
3
Identify the premise that infinite regress is impossible
Al-Razi argues that infinite regress leads to absurdities: either a cause depends on itself (circularity) or multiple parts make others superfluous. Locate where he calls it 'محال' (absurd).
See the discussion about composition and the impossibility of non-existence being part of the cause.
4
Verify the modus tollens validity
Check the logical form: If P then Q. Not Q. Therefore, not P. Here, P = 'no Necessary Being exists', Q = 'infinite regress occurs'. Since Q is false, P must be false, so the Necessary Being exists.
The conclusion is entailed necessarily if the premises are true.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيستحيل اتصاف العدم به. أما أن يكون لنقص فيصير الموجود في وقت ممتنعا لذاته من وقت آخر وهو محال. وبتقدير [؟] فإذا جعلت المعلول مسلوبا عن العلة حتى يكون عدمه لذاته لا لغيره أول الفاقة فيكون السبب فيه إما عدم العلة وهو محال، والكلام فيه كالكلام في الأول. ولا يمكن التخلص لأن استناد الممكنات بالآخرة إلى واجب الوجود الذي يستحيل عليه العدم فينتهي لا محالة إلى علة يكون عدمها [؟] والعدم عليها. فيكون السبب الأول لعدم المعلول إلا عدم العلة بلا آخر. ثم نقول: إن ثبت أن عدم المعلول قد يحصل لعدم العلة لكنه قد حصل أيضا لحصول هذه [؟] كالبياض المنفي عن المحل لطرو السواد. والذي يقال من أن انتفاء ذلك الضد يكون لأمر لا يحصل هو إلا مع حصوله فكان استناده عند طرو الضد يكون بالحقيقة انتفاء المعلول لانتقاد العلة ضعيف. لأن العدم لا يكون جزءا من المؤثر في الوجود. بل الإشكال القوي فيه أن حدوث الضد الطاري مشروط بنوال [؟] فلو عللناه بطرد الحادث لزم الدور. وهو [؟] في أنه هل كل ما لا بد منه في تحقق المعلول كان جزءا من العلة فيه إشكالات. فإن العدم قد يكون معترضا ذلك مع أنه لا يمكن جعله جزءا من الموثر في الوجود، وإلا لزم التركيب في العلة الموثرة وهو محال. لأن كل واحد من تلك الأجزاء إذا اجتمعت فإما أن يحصل لها عند الاجتماع أمر ما لم يكن حاصلا قبل ذلك أو لا يحصل. فإن كان الأول فالمقتضي لذلك التأكد إن كان كل واحد منها أو الواحد منها لزم استغناء المستقل فبلغي غيره، وإن كان المجموع كان الكلام في كيفية حصول ذلك.
It is impossible for non-existence to be attributed to it. Either it is due to a deficiency, so that the existent becomes impossible in one time from another time, which is absurd. And assuming [؟], if you take the effect as negated from the cause so that its non-existence is for itself not for another, it is the first need; so the cause of that is either the non-existence of the cause, which is absurd, and the discussion is like the first. And one cannot escape because the dependence of contingent things ultimately is upon the Necessary Existence, upon whom non-existence is impossible; so it necessarily ends at a cause whose non-existence [؟] and non-existence is upon it. So the first cause of the non-existence of the effect is only the non-existence of the cause, without end. Then we say: If it is established that the non-existence of the effect may occur due to the non-existence of the cause, but it also occurs due to the occurrence of this [؟], like whiteness negated from the substrate due to the arrival of blackness. And what is said that the negation of that opposite is due to something that only occurs with its occurrence, so its dependence at the arrival of the opposite is in reality the non-existence of the effect due to the deficiency of the cause—this is weak. Because non-existence is not a part of the efficient cause in existence. Rather, the strong difficulty in it is that the occurrence of the supervening opposite is conditioned by the destruction [؟]; if we explain it by the expulsion of the occurrent, circularity follows. And it is [؟] whether everything necessary for the realization of the effect is part of the cause—there are problems. For non-existence may be an obstacle to that, while it is impossible to make it a part of the efficient cause in existence, otherwise composition would occur in the efficient cause, which is absurd. Because each of those parts, when they come together, either something occurs for them at the time of union that was not present before, or it does not occur. If it is the first, then the necessitation for that confirmation—if it is each one or one of them, then independence of the independent follows so the other is superfluous; if it is the collective, then the discussion is about how that occurs.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological argument about the impossibility of an infinite regress in causal chains, aiming to prove the necessity of a first cause, the Necessary Being. The author begins by stating that it is impossible for non-existence to be attributed to the Necessary Being—implying that the Necessary Being cannot not exist. He then considers an objection: perhaps a thing's non-existence is due to a deficiency in itself, but that would imply a change from existence to non-existence, which is absurd. He then assumes that the effect's non-existence is due to the non-existence of its cause, and argues that this leads to an infinite regress unless one stops at a cause whose non-existence is impossible (i.e., the Necessary Being). The author counters an objection that the non-existence of an effect can be due to the presence of an opposite (like blackness negating whiteness), which would mean the effect's non-existence is not directly from the cause's non-existence. He refutes this by noting that non-existence cannot be a part of an efficient cause. The passage then delves into the strong form of the skeptical objection: if the occurrence of a new opposite conditions the cause's non-existence, then explaining it by the expulsion of the earlier occurrent leads to circularity. The author finally considers the composition of causes: if multiple parts come together to produce an effect, either they produce a new property when combined or not; if they do, then either each part alone suffices (making others superfluous) or only the collective suffices, leading to further questions about the mechanism.

Key Terms
واجب الوجود
العلة
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ultimate outcome of the argument against infinite regress?
A) The chain of causes is infinite
B) The chain ends at a cause whose non-existence is impossible
C) The non-existence of an effect is always due to an opposite
D) Multiple causes can combine to form a composite cause

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of infinite regress forces the existence of a Necessary Being as the First Cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reconstruct the conditional (modus tollens) argument from the first half of al-Razi's passage up to 'فينتهي لا محالة إلى علة يكون عدمها [؟]'. Write it in the form: If P then Q. Not Q. Therefore not P. Identify P and Q.
?
Consider al-Ghazali's argument in the Tahafut: 'If the world were eternal, then the number of past events would be infinite; but an infinite regress is impossible; therefore the world is not eternal.' Does this follow the same logical form? Explain.

Objection and Answer in the Natural Motion Debate
Objection and response on natural motion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the dialectical structure of the natural motion argument and evaluate the strength of the objector's point and the author's reply.

Core Idea
Natural motion ceases when the body reaches its goal; motion itself proves nature's conditional action.

In this passage, the author presents an objection to the claim that motion in the elements is natural. The objector argues that natural motion does not imply perpetual motion, because motion occurs only when the body is away from its natural place, and rest when it arrives. The author then turns this argument back on the objector, showing that if nature requires a condition for motion, then rest is also natural. This dialectical exchange demonstrates the use of reductio ad absurdum in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's claim
Read the first part of the passage to find what the objector asserts. He says motion in the three elements is by nature because they move themselves. Note the key terms: 'by nature' (بالطبيعة) and 'they move themselves' (تتحرك لذاتها).
The objector starts with a concession: motion is natural, but then introduces a caveat about perpetuity.
2
Extract the conditional reasoning
The objector uses a conditional: 'If you say nature's requisition of motion is conditional upon some suitability, then motion occurs due to distance, and rest upon reaching suitability.' Identify the antecedent (nature acts conditionally) and consequent (motion occurs when away, rest when at goal).
The objector is trying to show that natural motion can be intermittent, not perpetual.
3
Identify the author's counter-argument
The author responds by turning the conditional back: 'You have understood this number in nature, so understand it as well in the body.' He argues that if the objector admits nature acts conditionally, then rest is also natural when conditions are met. This undercuts the objection.
The author uses the objector's own premise to support the original position.
4
Evaluate the dialectical outcome
The author concludes that the objection cannot be refuted unless one claims nature necessitates perpetual motion, which leads to absurdity. Determine who wins: the author shows the objector's argument leads to a contradiction or supports the original thesis.
This is a classic jadal (dialectic) move: turning the opponent's evidence against them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولقائل أن السرفر فى الثلاثة الأول بالطبيعة فانها تركة لذاتها مع أنه لا يلزم من دوامها دوام الحركة ودوام إبنانها وحصولها عند الوصول إلى المطلوب فلئن قلتم اقتضاء الطبيعة الحركة مشروط بنوع مناسبة فتجد الحركة لأجل تجدد الغرب والبعد من تلك الحالة والسكون إنما يحصل عند الوصول إلى الملائمة تقولوا: إذن عقلتم هذا العدد في الطبيعة فاعقلوا مسلم ف الجسم وحينئذ لا يمكن دفع هذا الإشكال إلا بأن الحمية لعا قضت ذلك لكان كل جم كذلك
It may be said that the motion in the three [elements] is by nature, for they move themselves, even though it does not follow from its perpetuity the perpetuity of motion and the perpetuity of its cessation, and its occurrence upon reaching the desired [place]. For if you say that nature's requisition of motion is conditional upon some suitability, so motion occurs due to renewed distance and remoteness from that state, and rest only occurs upon reaching suitability, then you are saying: you have understood this number in nature, so understand it as well in the body. And then it is impossible to repel this objection except by saying that nature, since it determined that, every body would be like that.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical exchange: an objector argues that motion in the lower elements (earth, water, air, fire) is natural because they move by themselves. He argues that if motion were natural, it would not follow that motion is perpetual; rather, motion occurs when the object is away from its natural place, and rest occurs when it reaches that place. The author then turns the argument: if you admit that nature requires motion only under certain conditions (suitability), then you should also admit that rest occurs in the body when it reaches its suitable place. The author concludes that the objection cannot be refuted unless one claims that nature necessitates perpetual motion, which would lead to absurd consequences. The discussion is based on Aristotelian physics of natural motion and place.

Analyzing Arguments on Rarefaction and Condensation
Dialectical refutation of rarefaction and condensation as motions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the Aristotelian refutation of rarefaction as genuine motion in quantity and identify the logical structure of the dialectical exchange.

Core Idea
Rarefaction and condensation are not genuine motions but merely apparent changes in quantity.

In this passage, the author engages in a dialectical examination of motion in the category of quantity. He presents the arguments of those who affirm rarefaction and condensation as genuine motions, then refutes them using principles from Aristotelian natural philosophy. By analyzing this exchange, you will learn how logical tools are used to critique physical theories.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim under debate
The passage discusses whether rarefaction (takhalkhul) and condensation (takāthuf) are real motions in the category of quantity.
2
Extract the opponent's proofs
Find the two arguments: the container example (water rising when air enters) and the bursting vessel example (water expands when heated).
The opponents use empirical observations to argue for rarefaction as a genuine increase in volume without addition of matter.
3
Analyze the author's refutation
The author rejects these by pointing out the impossibility of vacuum and the absurdity of generating new pores in the vessel.
The refutation targets the logical and physical assumptions behind the proofs.
4
Understand the author's alternative view
The author argues that bodies are composed of hyle and form; hyle has no quantity in itself, so quantity is accidental. Half of a simple body is identical in nature to the whole, so division does not change essence. Therefore, rarefaction would require a change in quantity without change in essence, which is impossible.
This is a metaphysical argument based on the nature of prime matter.

Also, [motion] should not actually exist, otherwise the movement of the outer sphere would be prevented when it stands on the horizon; rather it suffices that it exists potentially as the beginning of motion and its end for something. And it has that it is a beginning and an endpoint. These two accidents are either considered relative to the motion or each of them is relative to the other. The first is the relation of correlation (taḍāyuf) because the beginning is a beginning of the endpoint and vice versa. The second is not by way of correlation because it is not necessary that if one conceives the beginning one conceives the endpoint, nor vice versa. It is clear that it is not the opposite of negation and possession and deprivation and disposition. So there is nothing except the opposite of conversion? And with God is success. Regarding the relation of movers to things moved, its meaning is that something changes from one species of the genus of the category to another species of it. Motion is witnessed to occur in four: quantity, quality, position, and place? And they argued for the first with two [proofs]: the proof with the container: that water is sucked up into a vessel and then it rises and enters the air? [...] before penetration, and it is impossible because contact before penetration does not increase the volume of the penetrated? And that is impossible because penetration is motion, and there is no motion [...]. And it is impossible because penetration necessitates an increase in volume that necessitates bursting, so the occurrence of bursting before its occurrence is impossible? This is a fallacy. And if there is something inside the vessel, then either it is motion in a single direction? That is impossible because that motion, if it is to one direction, would necessarily transfer the vessel to that direction? Transferring it is easier than breaking it, or to different directions, so the nature similar to an agent would have different effects. And as for the increase in quantity of what is in it, that is the desired. And as for the body, it is composed of hyle and form, and hyle in itself has no quantity, otherwise its quantity in itself would be one and the same for all quantities, so the matter of a large part is receptive to a small quantity and vice versa. The body is an amount of hyle and form. Know that its quantity is not essential to it. Half of a simple body is equal to the whole in nature, and they are equal in the complete essence and in all rulings. Therefore, if one takes half of it, it is its half, and vice versa. And if the division is in the first two directions, they are two spheres bounding? And the third and fourth are rarefaction and condensation of the spheres, and they do not say it, and that the [...] quantity of the point and vice versa, and that there is no [...] deserving a specific quantity, so it is necessary that things are not restricted to their specific quantities, nor more.
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical examination of motion in the category of quantity, specifically rarefaction (takhalkhul) and condensation (takāthuf). The author begins by discussing the nature of motion—it does not actually exist instant by instant but has potential existence as a continuum. He then distinguishes between two types of relative terms: correlation (taḍāyuf) where each term implies the other, and a non-correlative relation. The main topic is motion in quantity, which is commonly held to occur through rarefaction and condensation, as well as growth and decay. The author reports the arguments of those who affirm rarefaction: the example of water rising in a container and the bursting of vessels due to boiling. They claim that if water heats and expands, its volume increases without adding new matter, implying rarefaction. The author refutes these arguments by pointing out the impossibility of vacuum and the necessity of generating new pores, which is absurd. He then presents his own view: that bodies are composed of hyle and form, and hyle is devoid of quantity by itself; therefore, quantity is accidental. A simple body's half is identical in nature to the whole, so division does not change essence. Hence, rarefaction would imply that a body can take on any quantity without alteration, which contradicts observed restrictions. The passage thus defends the position that quantity is not subject to genuine motion through rarefaction and condensation, but rather through addition and subtraction of parts. Arguments rely on Aristotelian natural philosophy and the categories. The author employs dialectical method: stating opponent's position, citing their proofs, then offering counterarguments.

Proof by Elimination: Disjunctive Syllogism in Transformation
Author uses a disjunctive syllogism to prove transformation by eliminating alternatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the disjunctive premise in a syllogism and validate the conclusion by elimination of all alternatives.

Core Idea
Eliminate all other possibilities, and the remaining one must be true.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi uses a disjunctive syllogism (qiyas istithna'i munfasil) to argue that water turns into fire when heated. The argument works by listing all possible causes and then refuting each one. This method is a powerful tool in Islamic logic and theology, known as 'burhan al-khalf' or proof by elimination. By mastering this, you can analyze arguments that reduce possibilities to a single truth.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunctive premise
Locate the part of the passage where the author lists the alternatives for water heating: external penetration, latent emergence, or transformation.
The phrase 'إما...أو...أو' (either...or...or) signals the disjunction.
2
List the alternatives (disjuncts)
Write down the three possibilities clearly: A) external fire particles penetrate, B) latent fire particles emerge, C) some water parts transform into fire.
These must be mutually exclusive and exhaustive for the argument to work.
3
Analyze the refutation of each alternative
The author provides reasons to reject A and B. For A: particles easily separate and would require a larger source. For B: it would imply constant perception and uncaused emergence. Note that C is not refuted directly.
The refutations are based on empirical and logical grounds.
4
Determine the conclusion
Since A and B are false, and C is the only remaining possibility, the conclusion is that transformation (istihala) actually occurs.
This is a classic disjunctive syllogism: either A, B, or C; not A and not B; therefore C.
5
Evaluate the strength of the elimination
Check if the disjunction is truly exhaustive and if the refutations are sound. Are there other possibilities? Are the refutations convincing? Based on the passage, the author considers the list complete.
In formal logic, the argument is valid if the disjunction is exhaustive and the eliminated disjuncts are correctly negated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيكون تخن الماء إما بنفوذ أجزاء نارية خارجة فيه، أو ببروز أجزاء نارية كامنة فيه، أو بانقلاب بعض أجزاء الماء نارية. أما الأول فباطل لوجهين: لأن الملاطف يسهل تفريق الصالة لعدم اتصاله طبيعيا، ولأنه يقتضي أن يكون أكثر من تلك الشعلة. وأما الثاني فباطل لأنه يقتضي أن يحس به أبدا وأن النار الكامنة لو اقتضت بروزا لاقتضت بروز كل جزء بدون المتمكن. وأما الثالث فباطل لأن الأجزاء المائية مشاعية فلا يختص بعضها بالانقلاب. فلما بطلت الاحتمالات الثلاثة ثبت القول بالاستحالة.
Heating of water occurs either by penetration of external fire particles, or by emergence of latent fire particles, or by transformation of some parts of water into fire. The first is invalid for two reasons: because the subtle particles easily separate due to non-natural contact, and because it would require that [the fire] be larger than that flame. The second is invalid because it would entail that it is always perceived and that the latent fire would necessitate emergence without a cause. The third is invalid because water particles are homogeneous so no part is more suited for transformation. When the three possibilities are invalidated, the theory of transformation is established.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage presents a disjunctive syllogism (qiyas istithna'i munfasil) to prove the occurrence of istihala (transformation) in the heating of water. The author enumerates three possible causes for water becoming hot: external fire entering, latent fire emerging, or the water itself transforming into fire. Each is refuted in turn. The first possibility (external particles) is rejected because subtle particles lack natural attachment and would require an impossibly large source. The second (latent fire) is rejected because it would imply constant sensation and spontaneous emergence without external cause. The third (transformation) is rejected on the grounds of homogeneity—no part of water is more apt to change. With all alternatives invalidated, the only remaining explanation is that water actually transforms (istihala) into fire. This is a textbook application of the disjunctive syllogism: either A, B, or C; not A, not B, not C; therefore C (though here the conclusion is that the only remaining option is true, which is a form of argument by elimination). The logical structure mirrors the method of burhan al-khalf (reductio) and is common in kalam and falsafah.

الاستحالة
القياس الاستثنائي المنفصل
الكمون

Key Takeaway

Reconstructing the Argument Against Gradual Generation of Forms
Author argues that forms cannot arise gradually, with proof by infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the syllogistic argument against gradual generation of forms and identify its premises and conclusion.

Core Idea
Gradual change of forms leads to infinite regress, proving forms must arise instantaneously.

The author presents a rigorous argument to show that forms—whether substantial or accidental—cannot emerge gradually over time. By analyzing the logical structure of this proof, you will see how a seemingly plausible notion (gradual change) collapses into impossibility. This lesson will guide you through the steps of the syllogism embedded in the passage, helping you apply similar reasoning in theological and philosophical contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being refuted
The author states that forms cannot arise gradually (على سبيل التدريج). This is the thesis he intends to disprove.
The opposite claim would be that forms do arise gradually, which is common intuitive view.
2
Extract the conditional premise
The argument begins: If a form were to change gradually (by intensification or diminution), at each moment we would have a different form. This leads to a series of instantaneous forms.
The text says: 'when the form intensifies or decreases... another form must occur after it.'
3
Recognize the impossibility of infinite series in finite time
The author argues that having an infinite number of distinct forms in a finite time is impossible. Each form would exist for only an instant, and an infinite number of such instants cannot be contained in a finite temporal interval.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum: gradual change implies infinitely many forms, which is impossible.
4
Formulate the categorical syllogism
We can reconstruct: Major premise: 'Every gradual change implies an infinite series of distinct forms in a finite time.' Minor premise: 'No infinite series of distinct forms can occur in a finite time.' Conclusion: 'Therefore, no gradual change of forms occurs.' This is a first-figure syllogism (Celarent-like).
The author's explicit conclusion: 'It prevents gradual occurrence absolutely.'
5
Check the validity and soundness
The syllogism is valid (Barbara/Celarent figure). The major premise is justified by the nature of gradual change, the minor premise by the impossibility of actual infinity in a finite duration. Thus, the conclusion follows.
The author notes that this argument applies to both substances and accidents, and to qualitative and quantitative motion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثاني: أن حدوث هذه الصور لا يمكن أن يكون على سبيل التدريج، هذه الوظيفة متوجبة عليه؟ واحتحول عليها؟ بعقد؟ فإذا الصورة عند اشتدادها أو تنقصها، إما أن يبقى ليعم؟ فيكون قد حدث مما لم يكن، وإلا؟ شئ كان مع كون تلك الصورة لما كانت، وإن يبقى عدم الصورة لا اشتداد، فلا بد وأن تحصل عقيبها صورة أخرى. ثم إن تلك الصور المتعاقبة إما أن يوجد فيها مانع أكثر من أن واحد. فكون حال؟ كحلة؟ أو الوجود؟ فتناك صور آنية متتالية، وذلك إما في العمر محال لامتناع؟ إلا أن الصور إنما تكون متعاقب صور لا توجد واحدة إلا بعد أخرى، ولا يكون أكثر من آن واحد. وعدم الصورة المفتوحة؟ لوجب عدم الذات، خذن؟ لا يبقى من تلك الذوات؟ نعم كل ما هو موجود زمان الحركة فلاش؟ من المواد الحركة صورها؟ الصورة جوهر؟ لا ضدّ؟ فلا تحرك عنها وإليها، فالصورة يتحرك عنها وإليها. اعلم أن الوجه الأقل؟ النحل؟ ولكنها مانعة من الحدوث على التدريج مطلقا، سواء كان الحادث جوهرا أو عرضا، وذلك يقضى المنع من الحركة الكيفية والكمية.
The second station: That the occurrence of these forms cannot be gradual. This function is incumbent upon it? And they turned upon it? With a knot? So when the form intensifies or decreases, either it remains a while? Then it would have occurred from what was not, or else? something existed with the existence of that form when it was not, and if the absence of the form remains, there is no intensification, so another form must occur after it. Then those successive forms, either there is an obstacle in them more than one. So the state of? kahlah? or existence? So there are successive instantaneous forms, and that is impossible in time due to impossibility? except that forms are only successive forms, one does not exist except after another, and not more than one instant. And the absence of the opened form? would necessitate the non-existence of the essence, because? nothing remains of those essences? The form is substance? has no opposite? so it does not move away from it and to it, but the form moves away from it and to it. Know that the lesser aspect? the beehive? but it prevents gradual occurrence absolutely, whether the occurring is substance or accident, and that necessitates preventing qualitative and quantitative motion.

Commentary
This passage presents the second station (المقام الثاني) in a discussion about the generation of forms. The author argues that forms cannot arise gradually (على سبيل التدريج). The argument proceeds as follows: If a form were to change gradually (by intensification or diminution), at each moment we would have a different form. This leads to an infinite series of instantaneous forms in a finite time, which is impossible. Moreover, if the form were to vanish entirely, the underlying matter would cease to exist, which is absurd. Therefore, forms must occur instantaneously, not gradually. This principle applies to both substances and accidents, and it rules out qualitative and quantitative motion in this context. The author notes that this argument is thorough but may not align fully with the principles of the philosophers.

Key Terms

The Syllogism
intermediate
5 min
Formalizing the Celestial Sphere Argument
First proof against rests using the celestial sphere

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the first proof against slowness being composed of rests as a conditional syllogism (modus tollens) and assess its validity.

Core Idea
The celestial sphere's continuous motion refutes the claim that slowness arises from interspersed rests.

The author's first proof uses the motion of the celestial sphere (al-falak al-aʿẓam) to argue that slowness is not composed of rests. This argument follows a logical form known as modus tollens: if the premise (slowness equals rests) were true, a consequence would follow (the sphere would have many rests), but that consequence is false, so the premise must be false. Understanding this reasoning helps in analyzing arguments in kalam and natural philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being refuted
The author is attacking the view that 'slowness is composed of rests' (البطء تحلل للسكنات). This is the hypothesis to be disproven.
In logical terms, this is the antecedent of a conditional.
2
Express the argument as a conditional
The author implies: 'If slowness were composed of rests, then the celestial sphere would have many rests.' This is because the sphere moves extremely fast; if rests caused slowness, such a fast motion would require many rests to be slow.
This step translates the physical claim into a logical conditional.
3
State the observed fact
The author observes: 'But we do not observe any rests in the motion of the celestial sphere.' This negates the consequent of the conditional.
This is the minor premise in modus tollens.
4
Apply modus tollens
From the conditional and the negation of the consequent, we validly deduce the negation of the antecedent: 'Therefore, slowness is not composed of rests.'
Modus tollens is a valid argument form: If P then Q, not Q, therefore not P.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم فيه إثباتات أن البطء ليس تحللا للسكنات لوجوه ثلاثة. الأول: لو كان البطء تحللا للسكنات لكانت في حركة الفلك الأعظم سكنات كثيرة، لأنه يقطع مسافة عظيمة في زمان قليل، لكن الأمر بالعكس، فإنه لا يشاهد في حركته سكنات أصلا، فوجب أن لا يكون البطء تحللا للسكنات. الثاني: أن حركة الظل عند ارتفاع الشمس تسرع، ولو كانت السكنات متخللة لكانت بطيئة، والمشاهد خلافه. الثالث: حركة الرحى والدلو، كلما زادت السرعة خلصت من شوب السكنات، فالتفاوت ليس لتخلل السكنات. ولقائل أن يقول: لا يلزم من هذا أن تكون العلة الفاعلية هي وحدها، بل لابد من المجانية في نفس الحركة. والجواب: أن السرعة والبطء يقبلان الاشتداد والتقصر، وليسا من الأمور العدمية، فلا يلزم أن يكون التفاوت بسبب السكنات. ثم التقسيم الثالث: الحركات قد تكون متطابقة وقد لا تكون، والمتطابقة هي التي حافاتها متطابقة.
Then there are proofs that slowness is not a composition of rests for three reasons. First: if slowness were a composition of rests, then there would be many rests in the motion of the celestial sphere, because it covers a great distance in a short time, but the opposite is the case, for we do not observe any rests in its motion at all, so it must be that slowness is not a composition of rests. Second: the motion of the shadow when the sun rises becomes faster, and if rests were interspersed it would be slow, but the observed is contrary. Third: the motion of the millstone and the bucket, whenever speed increases it becomes free from the admixture of rests, so the variation is not due to the interposition of rests. And an objector might say: this does not necessitate that the efficient cause alone is responsible, rather there must be a material aspect in the motion itself. The answer: slowness and speed admit of intensification and diminution, and they are not privative matters, so variation need not be due to rests. Then the third division: motions may be superimposed or not, and superimposed motions are those whose boundaries are superimposed.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage presents a set of proofs that slowness (بطء) in motion is not caused by the insertion of tiny rests (سكنات) between phases of motion, a view that some ancient philosophers held. The author argues against this by giving three examples. First, the celestial sphere (الفلك الأعظم) moves extremely fast yet we observe no rests in it; if slowness were due to interspersed rests, the fastest motion would have the fewest rests, but we see that even fast motions appear continuous. Second, the shadow of an object at sunrise moves faster as the sun rises; if rests were interspersed, the shadow's motion would be slower, but it is not. Third, the motion of a millstone or a bucket from a well becomes faster as it becomes freer from the admixture of rests, implying that speed increases when rests decrease, but the variation in speed is not solely due to the presence or absence of rests. Then an objection is raised: perhaps the efficient cause (العلة الفاعلية) is not the only factor; the material disposition (المجانية) of the moving body also plays a role. The author responds that speed and slowness are qualities that admit of degrees (اشتداد وتقصر) and are not mere privations, so their variation does not require the insertion of rests. Finally, the passage introduces a third division of motions: they may be superimposed (متطابقة) meaning that their boundaries coincide, or not. This classification is important for understanding the relationship between different motions in the same body.

Key Terms
البطء
السكنات
الفلك الأعظم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What logical form does the first proof use?
A) Modus ponens
B) Modus tollens
C) Disjunctive syllogism

Key Takeaway
The first proof against rests is a valid modus tollens argument: if slowness were composed of rests, the celestial sphere would have rests; it does not; therefore, it is not composed of rests.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a modus tollens argument for a fiqh ruling: 'If the prayer is invalid due to a missed essential part, then it must be repeated. But it does not need repetition (based on evidence). Therefore, the prayer is valid.' Identify the antecedent, consequent, and the negated consequent.
?
Analyze the following kalam statement: 'If the universe were without a creator, it would not exhibit order. It exhibits order. Therefore, it has a creator.' Identify the logical form and whether it is valid.

Objection and Response on Slowness
Objection and author's answer on the cause of slowness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of an objection and its response in a physical argument and explain why the response is logically adequate.

Core Idea
An objection introduces material cause as a factor; the answer shows that speed variation is not merely due to rests but to the nature of motion itself.

After presenting three proofs, the author considers an objection: maybe the efficient cause is not the only factor; the material disposition (al-mujāniyya) of the moving body also matters. The author responds that speed and slowness are qualities that admit of degrees and are not privations, so their variation does not require rests. This exchange highlights the interplay between logical reasoning and physical principles.

Guided Path
1
State the objector's point
The objector argues that the proofs only show that rests are not the sole cause of slowness, but they do not rule out a material contribution (al-‘illa al-fā‘iliyya is not the only factor).
This is a standard move in jadal: pointing out an alternative explanation.
2
Identify the author's response
The author replies that speed and slowness are not privative (i.e., not mere absence of speed) but have degrees (شدّة وضعف). Therefore, variation can be explained by the intensity of motion itself, not by added rests.
This refutes the need for rests altogether.
3
Assess the logical adequacy
The response shows that the objection does not invalidate the core claim because the objection assumes rests could still be a factor, but the author demonstrates that the phenomenon is fully explicable without rests.
This is a case of refuting by providing a better explanation.

Superimposed Motions and Their Boundaries
Definition and division of superimposed motions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define superimposed motions (mutābiqa) and explain how their boundaries coincide, using examples from Islamic natural philosophy.

Core Idea
Superimposed motions share boundaries; understanding this helps analyze composite motions in physics.

The passage ends with a brief classification: motions may be superimposed (mutābiqa) or not. Superimposed motions are those whose boundaries (ḥāfāt) coincide. This concept is used in discussions of compound motion, such as a body moving both circularly and linearly at once. Understanding this distinction is useful for analyzing motion in kalam and falsafa.

Guided Path
1
Understand the term 'superimposed'
The Arabic 'mutābiqa' means 'superimposed' or 'coinciding'. The author says that superimposed motions have their boundaries (الحدود, here حافات) coinciding.
Boundaries may refer to the edges or endpoints of the motion path.
2
Identify an example of superimposed motions
Consider a point on a rotating wheel that also moves forward. Its path is a cycloid, which combines circular and linear motions. The boundaries of these two motions (e.g., the start and end points) coincide in the resultant path.
Islamic scholars like Ibn Sīnā discussed such composite motions.
3
Contrast with non-superimposed motions
If motions do not share boundaries, they are separate. For example, a bird flying then perching: the flying motion ends before perching begins; their boundaries are distinct.
This classification helps in analyzing whether a motion is simple or compound.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Rectilinear vs Circular Motion: A Verbal Dispute
Author argues that opposition between rectilinear and circular motion is merely verbal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish a substantive dispute from a verbal dispute in Islamic philosophical arguments, using the case of motion.

Core Idea
What appears to be a contradiction in nature may be a contradiction in definitions.

In Islamic philosophy, many heated debates turn out to be about words rather than things. Al-Razi here dissects a classic problem: are rectilinear and circular motion opposites? He shows that the answer depends entirely on your definition of 'opposite'. This passage teaches us a key skill: before diving into an argument, check whether the disagreement is about facts or definitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim under scrutiny
The claim is that rectilinear motion is opposite to circular motion. Notice that this is a statement about the nature of motion, not just a verbal definition.
2
Extract al-Razi's reductio ad absurdum
Al-Razi argues: if rectilinear motion were opposite to circular motion, then a single straight line—capable of having infinitely many circular arcs—would have infinitely many opposites. This is impossible because one thing can have only one opposite.
This shows the contradiction if the claim were taken as a real opposition.
3
Understand the resolution: verbal dispute
Al-Razi states that the dispute is 'lafẓī' (verbal). He then specifies two meanings of 'opposite': (1) two things that cannot coexist in the same subject at once, and (2) things whose starting and ending points are actually existent and opposite. Under the first meaning, they are opposites; under the second, they are not.
The resolution comes from clarifying the definition.
4
Apply the distinction to other debates
The skill is to ask: Is the disagreement about the definition of a term, or about facts? If changing the definition removes the conflict, it is verbal. Practise by examining a similar debate in Islamic sciences.
For example, debates about whether 'ijmā' is infallible often hinge on the definition of 'ijmā'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تضاد الحركة المستقيمة لا تضاد المستديرة، فإن الخط المستقيم يمكن أن يكون ذا قوس غير متناهية، فلو كانت الحركة المستقيمة ضد الحركة المستديرة لمكان للخط الواحد أضداد غير متناهية، وهذا محال لأن ضد الواحد واحد. والذي عندي أن أمثال هذه الأبحاث لفظية، لأنه إن أريد بالضدين معنيين لمنع اجتماعهما في المحل الواحد دفعة واحدة، فالحركة المستقيمة والمستديرة متضادان، وإن أريد بالضدين مع ذلك أن يكون ما منه وما إليه موجودة بالفعل متضادين في الحقيقة، فالمستقر على هذا التفسير لا تضاد المستديرة وكذا المستديرات.
The opposition of rectilinear motion: it does not oppose circular motion. For the straight line can have infinite arcs. If rectilinear motion were opposite to circular motion, then a single line would have infinite opposites, and this is impossible because the opposite of one is one. My view is that such investigations are verbal. For if by 'opposites' is meant two meanings preventing their coexistence in a single place at once, then rectilinear and circular motion are opposites. But if by 'opposites' is meant that their termini and initia are actually existent and opposite in reality, then on this interpretation circular motion and other circular motions do not have opposites.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage is a critical discussion of whether rectilinear and circular motion are opposites. The author presents an argument: if a straight line can have an infinite number of arcs (i.e., different circular paths), then rectilinear motion would have to be opposite to each of those circular motions, leading to a single line having infinite opposites. Since one thing can have only one opposite, this is impossible. Hence, rectilinear and circular motion are not truly opposites. The author then remarks that such debates are merely verbal. He clarifies: if 'opposites' just mean they cannot coexist in one subject simultaneously, then they are opposites. But if 'opposites' require that the starting and ending points be actual and opposite, then circular motions have no opposite because they have no distinct termini. This aligns with Aristotelian physics, where circular motion is continuous without contrary.

Key Terms
تضاد
حركة مستقيمة
حركة مستديرة
لفظي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi, why does the argument that rectilinear motion is opposite to circular motion lead to an impossibility?
A) Because a straight line cannot have arcs.
B) Because a straight line can have infinite arcs, leading to infinite opposites.
C) Because circular motion is not a real motion.
D) Because rectilinear motion is not a motion.

Key Takeaway
The dispute over whether rectilinear and circular motion are opposites is resolved by clarifying the definition of 'opposite'; it is a verbal dispute, not a real one.

?

The Syllogism

The Syllogism Classifying Circular Motion as Non-Natural
Reconstructing the categorical syllogism on motion types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the categorical syllogism proving that circular motion is not natural, identifying major premise, minor premise, and conclusion.

Core Idea
Natural motion seeks rest; circular motion never rests—therefore it is not natural.

This passage uses a clear categorical syllogism to classify circular motion. By parsing it, you will see how the author applies the rule that natural motion must be rectilinear and seek rest, while circular motion violates both conditions—a typical structure in kalam demonstrations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion
The conclusion is that circular motion is not natural. Look for the statement 'فالحركة المستديرة غير طبيعية' (so circular motion is not natural).
This appears after the author discusses natural motion.
2
Find the major premise
The major premise is 'كل حركة طبيعية مستقيمة' (Every natural motion is rectilinear). This is stated explicitly.
The author also adds the contrapositive: what is not rectilinear is not natural.
3
Find the minor premise
The minor premise is that circular motion is not rectilinear. The author argues that at each point, the body moves away from that point, so it is not a straight line.
This premise is implicit but logically derived from the definition of circular motion.
4
Reconstruct the syllogism
Major: Every natural motion is rectilinear. Minor: Circular motion is not rectilinear. Conclusion: Therefore, circular motion is not natural. This is a second-figure syllogism (Camestres).
The author also uses the contrapositive: if not rectilinear then not natural.
5
Check the contrapositive
The author explicitly states 'ما لا يكون مستقيماً لا يكون طبيعياً' (what is not rectilinear is not natural). This is an immediate inference from the major premise.
This reinforces the conclusion without needing a full syllogism.

المفيد: ومن رمى حجرا إلى فوق فإنه لا يخطر بباله السكون والقوة الممكنة ولا يقصد إلا الجر إلى العلو. وأما ثانكا فلات تلك القوة حين ما صارت مكنة بالفعل وجب استعمالها إلا إذا وجد ما يعدمها وذلك المعدم ليت الطبيعة ولا أجر ولا شياكا كان فيه والألما وجدت هذه القوة فلابد وأن يكون أم الخارجا اتفاقيا وحنين بعود الإلزام المذكد البراق إذا فرضنا حجر عظيم من الهواء في تلك الحالة تساقط إلى فوق فحين ما وصل الجبل البهالا بد كان رجع تلك القوة له فلو وجب وقوفها وكنتها لوح چند وقوف الجبل في الهواء وذلك مستبعد. وحجة الشيخ: إن وجوب هذا التكون بالميل هو العلة الغربية لحركة الجر من حد إلى آخر من المسافة فإذا أراد الحجر أن يصل إلى حد فلابد وأن تكون القوة الموصل له إليه وعلة الوصول حاصلة معه فالوصول إلى الحدث الآن فذلك الميك موجود في ذلك الان فإذا رجع العين عن ذلك الحد فلابد مات بكرت عيل أخ وهو أيضا إن خاما أن تجتمع الميلان في آن واحد وهو محال. واستحالة أن يجتمع الميل إلى الشرق مع الميل عند أوني أنين فان مالان بينیمان كان لزم نشانی إيرانات كان كان فقد حصل الكون واخواتكن من وجهين. أما أولاً فهذه الحجة على الكم والكيف لأنه لا حاجة بما إلى الميل. وأما ثانكا فلا تسلم امتناع اجتماع الميلين دفعة واحدة وقد من تقرير ذلك في مباحث النقل والحفة. إن الحركة المستديرة بالذات لا تكون إلا إرادية. الحركة الطبيعية هي عن حالة منافية وطلب الحالة المناسبة ولذلك لا يمكن أن تكون مستديرة لأنها كل نقطة تحرك عنها الجسم حركة شادية فحركته عنها عن حركته إليها والمعروف لا يكون مقصوداً. فتلك الحركة ليست هرباً بالطبع عنها. لا يقال: إن الجسم المتقسم الحركة يطلب حركته إلى نقطة وعند وصوله إليها يفارقها بالطبع مع أنها كانت مطلوبة بالطبع. لأنا نقول: هربه عنها ليس بعينه توجها إليها خلاف المستديرة فإنها ليست طلب الحالة الملائمة لوجهين: في الطبيعة إذا أوصلت الجسم إلى الحالة المطلوبة سكنته والمستديرة ليست كذلك. الطلب الطبيعي للكمال فلا بد أن يكون على أقرب الطرق والألكانت الطبيعة صادقة عنه فيكون الطبيعة محركة للجسم مصادفة عنه وذلك محال. وأقرب الطرق من المستقيم فكل حركة طبيعية مستقيمة وينعكس بانعكاس النقيضان: ما لا يكون مستقيماً لا يكون طبيعياً. فالحركة المستديرة غير طبيعية ولا قسرية لأن القسر خلاف الطبيعة والمالم يكن هناك طبيعة تقتضي أمراً فإن الشمال إن وجدها يعاندها فالحقيقة كونها قسرية فهي إذن إرادية. فإن قيل: الحركة الإرادية لا تكون إلا لواحد. قلت: هذا باطل فإن علامة الاختيار الواحد يمكن أن تبع وهو أن الفاعل إذا كان مختاراً يمكن أن يفعل معيناً. ومتى يعنى الاختيار يفي الفاعل لذلك المختار؟ قلت: لا محالة على ما مر، فإذا أمكن استمرار الفعل الحواني نكانا قصرا لكن ذلك دانا. وعنه قال بطلميوس: المختار إن طلب الأفضل لكن بينه وبين الطبيعي فرق. في أن الحركة المستديرة هي العلة لحدوث الحوادث بالذات. الحوادث العنصرية مبادئها إما أن تكون عنصرية أو لا تكون. فإن كانت عنصرية فإما أن تكون في أجسامها أو في أجزائها مركونة فيها. الأول باطل على عامر عرمة والثاني فإما أن تكون القوى لها شعور بما يصدر عنها أو لا تكون. الأول هو الاختيار والثاني.
The useful: And whoever throws a stone upward, he does not think about rest and possible power, nor does he intend anything but pulling upward. ... And the proof of the Sheikh: That the necessity of this motion by inclination is the final cause of the movement of the stone from one limit to another of the distance... And the impossibility of two inclinations meeting at one moment... Natural motion is away from a contrary state and seeking a suitable state. Therefore it cannot be circular because at each point the body moves away from it by a violent motion, so its motion away from it is not its motion towards it... So that motion is not a natural escape from it... Every natural motion is rectilinear, and the converse by contrapositive: what is not rectilinear is not natural. So circular motion is neither natural nor forced,... so it is voluntary... Ptolemy said: The voluntary [agent] seeks the better... circular motion is the cause of the occurrence of events essentially.
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage presents a syllogistic argument to prove that circular motion is voluntary, not natural or forced. The author begins with a practical example: a person throwing a stone upward does not intend rest or possible power but only upward movement. Then he discusses the concept of inclination (mayl) as the efficient cause of motion. The key argument: natural motion is rectilinear because nature seeks the shortest path and aims at a state of rest. In circular motion, the body never reaches a state of rest; at each point it moves away from that point, so it does not seek a suitable state. Therefore, circular motion is not natural. It is also not forced (qasr) because forced motion is against nature, and if there is no natural inclination, then what exists is voluntary. The author then addresses an objection about voluntary motion being for one thing only, and refutes it by noting that a voluntary agent can choose among options. He concludes with a reference to Ptolemy that circular motion is the primary cause of generation and corruption. Throughout, the argument uses categorical syllogisms with premises about the nature of motion and inclination.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is circular motion not natural?
A) Because it is uniform and eternal.
B) Because natural motion must be rectilinear and seek a state of rest.
C) Because it is caused by an external force.
D) Because it is the cause of generation and corruption.

Key Takeaway
Circular motion is classified as non-natural because it fails the condition of rectilinearity and does not seek rest.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a categorical syllogism to prove that the world is temporally originated (hadith) using the premises: 'Everything temporally originated has a cause' and 'The world is temporally originated.' Identify the figure and mood.
?
In the passage, the author uses the contrapositive. Apply the same technique to the premise 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' to derive 'What is not forbidden is not an intoxicant.'
